The Conditional 'Law': Linguistic and Logical Uses
Commentary on Quranic conditional 'law' and its uses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the privative (imtina'iyya) and necessary-implication (luzumiyya) uses of the Arabic conditional particle 'law' and identify which use is employed in a given Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Law shifts between privative and necessary implication depending on context.

This lesson examines the classical discussion of the Quranic conditional 'law'. Scholars like al-Sa'd argued that the verse 'ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لأسمعهم' is not a simple privative conditional but a case of necessary implication (luzumiyya). We will explore the differences between these two uses and how they affect logical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main uses of 'law'
Privative (imtina'iyya): implies the antecedent did not happen. Necessary implication (luzumiyya): the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent regardless of actual occurrence.
Al-Sa'd categorizes the verse as luzumiyya.
2
Analyze privative examples
Examples like 'لو جئتني لأكرمتك' indicate the coming did not happen. Similarly, Quranic verses like 'ولو أنما في الأرض من شجرة أقلام' remove a presumed conflict.
The passage provides multiple Quranic examples.
3
Analyze the necessary-implication use
In 'ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لأسمعهم', the connection between knowing good and making hear is necessary, even if the antecedent is false.
Al-Sa'd says it is 'of the category of necessary implication'.
4
Apply to the verse
Determine which use is intended based on context. If 'tawalli' (turning away) is interpreted as 'not benefiting', it is luzumiyya; if as 'turning aside', it is privative.
The passage discusses different interpretations of 'tawalli'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَعْصِي، فَرَفَعَ ذَلِكَ التَّوَهُمَ، وَمِثْلُهُ : ﴿وَلَوْ أَنَّمَا فِي الْأَرْضِ مِن شَجَرَةٍ أَفَلَامُ [لقمان: ۲۷] الآيَةَ، ولو كَانَ الْبَحْرُ مِدَادًا لِكَلِمَتِ رَبِّي ﴾ [الكهف: ۱۰۹] ،الآيَةَ قُل لَّوْ كُنتُمْ فِي بُيُوتِكُمْ لَبَرَزَ الَّذِينَ كُتِبَ عَلَيْهِمُ الْقَتْلُ إِلَى مَضَاجِعِهِمْ ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٥٤] ، وَمِثْلُ ذَلِكَ : وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَوَلَّوا وَهُم مُعْرِضُونَ ﴾ [الأنفال: ۲۳] ، وَمِثْلُ ذَلِكَ فِي الكَلَامِ كَثِيرٌ . وَأَمَّا : ﴿ وَلَوْ عَلِمَ اللهُ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا لَأَسْمَعَهُمْ ﴾ [الأنفال: ۲۳] ، فَقَالَ السَّعْدُ : إِنَّهُ مِنْ قَبِيل اللُّزُومِيَّةِ وَارِدٌ عَلَى قَاعِدَةِ اللُّغَةِ.
قصارة.
نعم؛ في الحلية» عن ابن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنهما : أنَّ النَّبيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قال في سالم مولى أبي حذيفة : أنَّ سالماً شديد الحب الله ، لو كان لا يخافُ الله ما عصاه، وفي إسناده: ابن لهيعة، وهو ضعيف .
قوله : (وَمِثْلُهُ: ﴿وَلَوْ أَنَّمَا فِي الْأَرْضِ .... إلخ) أي : فإنَّه ربَّما يُتوهَّمُ التَّنافر بين كون الأشجار أقلاماً ، وعدم نفاد كلمات الله تعالى فرفع هذا التّوهُم وصار المعنى: أنَّ كلمات الله تعالى لا نفاد لها، مع كون الأشجار أقلاماً، فكيف مع عدم ذلك؟!
قوله: (وَمِثْلُ ذَلِكَ : ﴿وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ ) يعني: أنَّ التَّولّي لازم عـلـى تـقـديـر الإسماع، فكيف على تقدير عدم الإسماع؟! فهو دائم الوجود.
وهذا إن فسِّر «التَّولّي» بـ : «عدم الانتفاع» كما في «الكشاف»، وأمَّا لو فسر بـ: «الإعراض» كما في السَّعد، فهي وارده على الاستعمال المشهور ؛ أي: من قبيل استعمال «لو» الامتناعيَّة؛ نحو: «لو جئتني لأكرمتك».
قوله : (فَقَالَ السَّعْدُ: إِنَّهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ اللُّزُومِيَّةِ وَارِدٌ عَلَى قَاعِدَةِ اللُّغَةِ) ذكر ذلك في «المطول» بعد أن زيف قول من قال : إنَّ الآية على صورة قياس اقتراني، والحد الوسط هو : «لأسمعهم»، فإذا حذف عند الإنتاج، قيل : ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لتولُّوا»، وهذا محال؛ لأنه على تقدير علم الخير فيهم لا يحصل منهم التّولّي، بل الإذعان والقبول، وإنَّما جاء البطلان من فقد كلّية الكبرى في الشكل الأوَّل ؛ لأنَّها هنا مهملةٌ ؛ كقولك : الإنسان حيوان والحيوان فرس» فالنتيجة باطلة. وكيف يقال : إنَّ الله تعالى أتى بقياس ليس فيه شرط الإنتاج ؛ لأنَّه إن لم يعلمه فهو جهل، وإن علمه وأتى به على خلاف ذلك فهو سفه، فالحقُ : أنَّها واردة على قاعدة اللُّغة ؛ يعني : أنَّ سبب
عدم الإسماع عدم العلم بالخير فيهم، ثمَّ ابتدأ قوله: ﴿وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَولَّوا) [الأنفال : ٢٣] كلاماً آخر على طريقة : «لو لم يخف الله، لم يعصه .
وأجاب في المغني عن هذه الشُّبهة : بأنَّ التَّقدير فى الآية : ولو علم الله تعالى فيهم خيراً وقتاً ما لأسمعهم، ولو أسمعهم لتولُّوا بعد ذلك»، فيكون قياساً فيه شرط الإنتاج؛ أي: ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً وقتاً مَّا لتولّوا بعد ذلك، ولا استحالة فيها حينئذ.
واعترض السَّيِّد قوله : «على قاعدة اللُّغة : بأنَّه يُفهم من ظاهره: أنَّ المعنى الثاني إنَّما هو بحسب الأوضاع الاصطلاحية لأرباب المعقول، وفيه بعد جدا، بل الحقِّ : أنَّه مِنَ المعاني المعتبرة عند أهل اللُّغة الواردة في استعمالاتهم؛ إلَّا أنَّه أقل استعمالاً مِنَ الأوَّل كالثَّالث الآتي،
He disobeys, so that removed the presumption. And similar to it: 'And if whatever trees in the earth were pens...' the verse. And 'If the sea were ink for the words of my Lord...' the verse. 'Say: Even if you were in your houses, those decreed to be killed would have gone forth to their resting places.' And similar to that: 'And if He had made them hear, they would have turned away while they are turning away.' And similar to that in speech is abundant. As for 'And if Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear,' al-Sa'd said: It is of the category of necessary implication and is based on the linguistic rule.
Qasara. Yes, in al-Hilya from Ibn 'Umar: that the Prophet said about Salim, the freed slave of Abu Hudhayfa: 'Salim is intensely loving Allah; if he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.' In its chain is Ibn Lahi'a, and he is weak.
His saying: 'And similar to it: "And if whatever trees in the earth were pens..." etc.' i.e., perhaps it might be presumed a discrepancy between the trees being pens and the non-exhaustion of the words of Allah, so He removed this presumption, and the meaning became: the words of Allah are not exhausted with the trees being pens, so how about without that?
His saying: 'And similar to that: "And if He had made them hear"' means: turning away is necessary on the assumption of making them hear, so how about on the assumption of not making them hear? So it is always existing.
And this is if 'turning away' is interpreted as 'not benefiting' as in al-Kashshaf. But if interpreted as 'turning aside' as in al-Sa'd, then it is based on the common usage, i.e., from the category of the privative 'law' like 'If you came to me, I would honor you.'
His saying: 'al-Sa'd said: It is of the category of necessary implication and is based on the linguistic rule.' He mentioned that in al-Mutawwal after refuting the statement of one who said that the verse is in the form of a categorical syllogism, and the middle term is 'He would make them hear,' so if it is omitted in production, it is said: 'And if Allah had known any good in them, they would have turned away.' And this is impossible because on the assumption of knowledge of good in them, turning away does not occur from them, but rather acceptance and reception. The invalidity comes from the lack of universality of the major premise in the first figure, because here it is neglected, like your saying: 'Man is an animal, every animal is a horse,' so the conclusion is false. And how can it be said that Allah Almighty came with a syllogism that lacks the condition of production? Because if He does not know it, it is ignorance, and if He knows it and brings it contrary to that, it is foolishness. So the truth is that it is based on the linguistic rule; meaning that the cause is...
Not giving hearing is not knowing good in them, then He began His saying: 'And if He had made them hear, they would have turned away' as another statement on the way: 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.'
And he responded in al-Mughni to this doubt by saying: The estimation in the verse is: 'And if Allah had known any good in them at some time, He would have made them hear, and if He had made them hear, they would have turned away after that,' so it becomes a syllogism with the condition of production; i.e., if Allah had known good in them at some time, they would have turned away after that, and there is no impossibility in that.
And al-Sayyid objected to his saying 'based on the linguistic rule' because it is understood from its apparent meaning that the second meaning is only according to the conventional definitions of the people of intellection, and that is far-fetched. Rather, the truth is that it is among the meanings considered by the people of language occurring in their usages, except that it is less used than the first, like the third to come.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the usage of the conditional particle 'law' (لو) in the Quran, focusing on the verse 'ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لأسمعهم' from Surat al-Anfal. The author begins by presenting examples of 'law' that remove a presumed conflict, such as the verse about trees becoming pens and the sea as ink, demonstrating that the intended meaning is the non-exhaustion of Allah's words regardless of the hypothetical. Al-Sa'd is cited claiming that the verse in question (Anfal 23) is a form of 'luzumiyya' (necessary implication) based on linguistic rules. The author then explains the interpretation of 'turning away' (al-tawalli) and the difference between al-Kashshaf and al-Sa'd. A significant objection is raised: some considered the verse a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) with middle term 'لأسمعهم', but this leads to absurdity because the major premise is not universal (muhmala). The author refutes this by demonstrating that the syllogism is invalid due to the lack of universality, comparing it to the invalid syllogism 'Man is an animal, every animal is a horse.' Then, a response from al-Mughni provides an alternative reading: the verse contains two conditionals: first, if Allah knew good in them at any time, He would make them hear; second, if He made them hear, they would turn away. This yields a valid conditional chain. Finally, al-Sayyid critiques al-Sa'd's phrasing that the verse is 'based on the linguistic rule', arguing that the second meaning (the necessary implication) is not merely a technical logical concept but a valid linguistic usage, albeit less common. This passage illustrates how classical scholars integrated logical analysis with Arabic linguistics to interpret Quranic conditionals, highlighting the importance of distinguishing between logical and linguistic levels.

Key Terms
لو
لزومية
قياس اقتراني

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does al-Sa'd categorize the use of 'law' in the verse 'ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لأسمعهم'?
A) Privative (imtina'iyya)
B) Necessary implication (luzumiyya)
C) Categorical syllogism
D) Inductive reasoning

Key Takeaway
The conditional 'law' can function as either privative or necessary implication; identifying the correct use depends on context and the interpretation of the consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the conditional 'law' in the verse 'وَلَوْ أَنَّمَا فِي الْأَرْضِ مِن شَجَرَةٍ أَقْلَامٌ' (Luqman 27). Determine whether it is privative or necessary implication and explain your reasoning.
?
Construct a sentence using 'law' in the necessary-implication sense on an Islamic topic, such as the attributes of Allah (e.g., 'Law kana Allahu 'aliman bi-shay'in la kana...').

The Syllogistic Objection and Its Resolution
Objection that the verse forms an invalid categorical syllogism and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that the verse 'ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لأسمعهم' forms an invalid categorical syllogism, and explain the responses that show it is not a syllogism but a conditional chain.

Core Idea
The alleged syllogism fails because the major premise lacks universality; the verse is actually a sequence of conditionals.

Some scholars argued that the verse 'ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لأسمعهم' could be interpreted as a categorical syllogism in the first figure. However, as al-Sa'd and others pointed out, this leads to an impossible conclusion. In this lesson, we examine the logical objection and the responses, particularly the resolution from al-Mughni that treats the verse as two nested conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alleged syllogism
The major premise is taken as 'If He makes them hear, they turn away'; the minor premise is 'If He knew good, He makes them hear'; the conclusion is 'If He knew good, they turn away'.
The middle term is 'makes them hear'.
2
Check the validity in the first figure
In the first figure, the major premise must be universal. Here it is muhmala (indefinite) – not all who are made to hear turn away. So the syllogism is invalid.
The author compares it to 'Man is an animal, every animal is a horse'.
3
Recognize the absurdity
The conclusion that they would turn away contradicts the assumption that if Allah knew good in them, they would accept and not turn away.
This shows the interpretation is impossible.
4
Understand the resolution from al-Mughni
The verse actually contains two separate conditionals: (1) If Allah knew good at some time, He would make them hear; (2) If He made them hear, they would turn away after that. This forms a valid conditional chain, not a categorical syllogism.
This avoids the impossibility because the turning away occurs after the hearing, not at the same time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَعْصِي، فَرَفَعَ ذَلِكَ التَّوَهُمَ، وَمِثْلُهُ : ﴿وَلَوْ أَنَّمَا فِي الْأَرْضِ مِن شَجَرَةٍ أَفَلَامُ [لقمان: ۲۷] الآيَةَ، ولو كَانَ الْبَحْرُ مِدَادًا لِكَلِمَتِ رَبِّي ﴾ [الكهف: ۱۰۹] ،الآيَةَ قُل لَّوْ كُنتُمْ فِي بُيُوتِكُمْ لَبَرَزَ الَّذِينَ كُتِبَ عَلَيْهِمُ الْقَتْلُ إِلَى مَضَاجِعِهِمْ ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٥٤] ، وَمِثْلُ ذَلِكَ : وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَوَلَّوا وَهُم مُعْرِضُونَ ﴾ [الأنفال: ۲۳] ، وَمِثْلُ ذَلِكَ فِي الكَلَامِ كَثِيرٌ . وَأَمَّا : ﴿ وَلَوْ عَلِمَ اللهُ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا لَأَسْمَعَهُمْ ﴾ [الأنفال: ۲۳] ، فَقَالَ السَّعْدُ : إِنَّهُ مِنْ قَبِيل اللُّزُومِيَّةِ وَارِدٌ عَلَى قَاعِدَةِ اللُّغَةِ.

نعم؛ في الحلية» عن ابن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنهما : أنَّ النَّبيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قال في سالم مولى أبي حذيفة : أنَّ سالماً شديد الحب الله ، لو كان لا يخافُ الله ما عصاه، وفي إسناده: ابن لهيعة، وهو ضعيف .
قوله : (وَمِثْلُهُ: ﴿وَلَوْ أَنَّمَا فِي الْأَرْضِ .... إلخ) أي : فإنَّه ربَّما يُتوهَّمُ التَّنافر بين كون الأشجار أقلاماً ، وعدم نفاد كلمات الله تعالى فرفع هذا التّوهُم وصار المعنى: أنَّ كلمات الله تعالى لا نفاد لها، مع كون الأشجار أقلاماً، فكيف مع عدم ذلك؟!
قوله: (وَمِثْلُ ذَلِكَ : ﴿وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ ) يعني: أنَّ التَّولّي لازم عـلـى تـقـديـر الإسماع، فكيف على تقدير عدم الإسماع؟! فهو دائم الوجود.
وهذا إن فسِّر «التَّولّي» بـ : «عدم الانتفاع» كما في «الكشاف»، وأمَّا لو فسر بـ: «الإعراض» كما في السَّعد، فهي وارده على الاستعمال المشهور ؛ أي: من قبيل استعمال «لو» الامتناعيَّة؛ نحو: «لو جئتني لأكرمتك».
قوله : (فَقَالَ السَّعْدُ: إِنَّهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ اللُّزُومِيَّةِ وَارِدٌ عَلَى قَاعِدَةِ اللُّغَةِ) ذكر ذلك في «المطول» بعد أن زيف قول من قال : إنَّ الآية على صورة قياس اقتراني، والحد الوسط هو : «لأسمعهم»، فإذا حذف عند الإنتاج، قيل : ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لتولُّوا»، وهذا محال؛ لأنه على تقدير علم الخير فيهم لا يحصل منهم التّولّي، بل الإذعان والقبول، وإنَّما جاء البطلان من فقد كلّية الكبرى في الشكل الأوَّل ؛ لأنَّها هنا مهملةٌ ؛ كقولك : الإنسان حيوان والحيوان فرس» فالنتيجة باطلة. وكيف يقال : إنَّ الله تعالى أتى بقياس ليس فيه شرط الإنتاج ؛ لأنَّه إن لم يعلمه فهو جهل، وإن علمه وأتى به على خلاف ذلك فهو سفه، فالحقُ : أنَّها واردة على قاعدة اللُّغة ؛ يعني : أنَّ سبب
عدم الإسماع عدم العلم بالخير فيهم، ثمَّ ابتدأ قوله: ﴿وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَولَّوا) [الأنفال : ٢٣] كلاماً آخر على طريقة : «لو لم يخف الله، لم يعصه .
وأجاب في المغني عن هذه الشُّبهة : بأنَّ التَّقدير فى الآية : ولو علم الله تعالى فيهم خيراً وقتاً ما لأسمعهم، ولو أسمعهم لتولُّوا بعد ذلك»، فيكون قياساً فيه شرط الإنتاج؛ أي: ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً وقتاً مَّا لتولّوا بعد ذلك، ولا استحالة فيها حينئذ.
واعترض السَّيِّد قوله : «على قاعدة اللُّغة : بأنَّه يُفهم من ظاهره: أنَّ المعنى الثاني إنَّما هو بحسب الأوضاع الاصطلاحية لأرباب المعقول، وفيه بعد جدا، بل الحقِّ : أنَّه مِنَ المعاني المعتبرة عند أهل اللُّغة الواردة في استعمالاتهم؛ إلَّا أنَّه أقل استعمالاً مِنَ الأوَّل كالثَّالث الآتي،
He disobeys, so that removed the presumption. And similar to it: 'And if whatever trees in the earth were pens...' the verse. And 'If the sea were ink for the words of my Lord...' the verse. 'Say: Even if you were in your houses, those decreed to be killed would have gone forth to their resting places.' And similar to that: 'And if He had made them hear, they would have turned away while they are turning away.' And similar to that in speech is abundant. As for 'And if Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear,' al-Sa'd said: It is of the category of necessary implication and is based on the linguistic rule.

His saying: 'And similar to it: "And if whatever trees in the earth were pens..." etc.' i.e., perhaps it might be presumed a discrepancy between the trees being pens and the non-exhaustion of the words of Allah, so He removed this presumption, and the meaning became: the words of Allah are not exhausted with the trees being pens, so how about without that?
His saying: 'And similar to that: "And if He had made them hear"' means: turning away is necessary on the assumption of making them hear, so how about on the assumption of not making them hear? So it is always existing.
And this is if 'turning away' is interpreted as 'not benefiting' as in al-Kashshaf. But if interpreted as 'turning aside' as in al-Sa'd, then it is based on the common usage, i.e., from the category of the privative 'law' like 'If you came to me, I would honor you.'
His saying: 'al-Sa'd said: It is of the category of necessary implication and is based on the linguistic rule.' He mentioned that in al-Mutawwal after refuting the statement of one who said that the verse is in the form of a categorical syllogism, and the middle term is 'He would make them hear,' so if it is omitted in production, it is said: 'And if Allah had known any good in them, they would have turned away.' And this is impossible because on the assumption of knowledge of good in them, turning away does not occur from them, but rather acceptance and reception. The invalidity comes from the lack of universality of the major premise in the first figure, because here it is neglected, like your saying: 'Man is an animal, every animal is a horse,' so the conclusion is false. And how can it be said that Allah Almighty came with a syllogism that lacks the condition of production? Because if He does not know it, it is ignorance, and if He knows it and brings it contrary to that, it is foolishness. So the truth is that it is based on the linguistic rule; meaning that the cause is...
Not giving hearing is not knowing good in them, then He began His saying: 'And if He had made them hear, they would have turned away' as another statement on the way: 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.'
And he responded in al-Mughni to this doubt by saying: The estimation in the verse is: 'And if Allah had known any good in them at some time, He would have made them hear, and if He had made them hear, they would have turned away after that,' so it becomes a syllogism with the condition of production; i.e., if Allah had known good in them at some time, they would have turned away after that, and there is no impossibility in that.
And al-Sayyid objected to his saying 'based on the linguistic rule' because it is understood from its apparent meaning that the second meaning is only according to the conventional definitions of the people of intellection, and that is far-fetched. Rather, the truth is that it is among the meanings considered by the people of language occurring in their usages, except that it is less used than the first, like the third to come.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the usage of the conditional particle 'law' (لو) in the Quran, focusing on the verse 'ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً لأسمعهم' from Surat al-Anfal. The author begins by presenting examples of 'law' that remove a presumed conflict, such as the verse about trees becoming pens and the sea as ink, demonstrating that the intended meaning is the non-exhaustion of Allah's words regardless of the hypothetical. Al-Sa'd is cited claiming that the verse in question (Anfal 23) is a form of 'luzumiyya' (necessary implication) based on linguistic rules. The author then explains the interpretation of 'turning away' (al-tawalli) and the difference between al-Kashshaf and al-Sa'd. A significant objection is raised: some considered the verse a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) with middle term 'لأسمعهم', but this leads to absurdity because the major premise is not universal (muhmala). The author refutes this by demonstrating that the syllogism is invalid due to the lack of universality, comparing it to the invalid syllogism 'Man is an animal, every animal is a horse.' Then, a response from al-Mughni provides an alternative reading: the verse contains two conditionals: first, if Allah knew good in them at any time, He would make them hear; second, if He made them hear, they would turn away. This yields a valid conditional chain. Finally, al-Sayyid critiques al-Sa'd's phrasing that the verse is 'based on the linguistic rule', arguing that the second meaning (the necessary implication) is not merely a technical logical concept but a valid linguistic usage, albeit less common. This passage illustrates how classical scholars integrated logical analysis with Arabic linguistics to interpret Quranic conditionals, highlighting the importance of distinguishing between logical and linguistic levels.

Key Terms
لو
لزومية
قياس اقتراني

D) The conclusion contradicts the known attributes of Allah.

Key Takeaway
The verse is not a categorical syllogism but a conditional statement (luzumiyya) or a chain of conditionals; the objection highlights the danger of applying syllogistic rules without proper linguistic and contextual analysis.

Three Uses of 'Law' (لو) in Arabic Logic
Poem enumerating three logical-linguistic uses of 'law' with Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the three uses of the Arabic conditional particle 'law' (لو) as identified by Abu Hafs al-Fasi and match each use to its Quranic example.

Core Idea
The three uses of 'law' transform a simple conditional into a precise logical tool: privation, demonstration, and continuity.

This lesson unpacks a classical classification of the particle 'law' (لو) by Abu Hafs al-Fasi, encapsulated in a poem. Each use corresponds to a distinct logical function, crucial for interpreting conditionals in Quranic exegesis and theological reasoning. We will work through the poem's lines, the accompanying commentary, and the Quranic examples provided.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the poem's three categories
Read the poem line by line. The first line describes the first use (imtina'): negation of the consequent due to negation of the antecedent. The second line describes the second use (burhan): knowledge of the consequent's negation proves the antecedent's negation. The third line describes the third use: linking the apodosis to the more distant contradictory to show continuity.
The poem is a mnemonic; note the key terms: 'imtina' (prevention), 'burhan' (demonstration), 'ab'ad al-naqidayn' (more distant contradictory).
2
Match each use to its Quranic example
For the first use, note verses like 'ولو شئنا لرفعناه بها' (imtina' of the second because of imtina' of the first). For the second use, identify verses like 'ولو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتا' (demonstrative: if the consequent were false, the antecedent is false). For the third use, the text gives no complete verse but explains the logic; try to find a verse where the apodosis is linked to a more distant contradictory to show continuity.
The second use is less common; the third use is subtle and requires understanding of 'ab'ad al-naqidayn'.
3
Distinguish the first and third uses
Both involve 'law', but the first negates the consequent because the antecedent is negated. The third preserves the consequent even if the antecedent is negated, by linking it to a more extreme opposite. For example, 'If he were rich, he would donate; and if he were poor, he would donate even more' – the donation occurs in both cases, but the apodosis is linked to the more distant (poor) to stress continuity.
Think of the third use as emphasizing that the apodosis holds regardless of the protasis.
4
Apply the classification to a new Quranic verse
Given a new 'law' verse not in the text, determine which use it exemplifies and explain your reasoning. For example, consider the verse: 'ولو أنما في الأرض من شجرة أقلام والبحر يمده من بعده سبعة أبحر ما نفدت كلمات الله' (Luqman 31:27). This is a hypothetical condition; does it match the first use (imtina') or third use (continuity)?
Practice by creating your own example for each use using Islamic themes.

باب في القضايا وأحكامها
وقد أشار إلى الأقسام الثَّلاثة أبو حفص الفاسي، ونظمها بقوله:
وَاسْتَعْمَلُوا «لَوْ فِي انْتِفَاءِ الثَّالِي لِنَفْيِ شَيْطٍ غَالِبَ الأَحْوَالِ وَفِي دَلَالَةِ انْتِفَائِهِ عَلَى نَفْي مُقَدَّمِ بِبُرْهَانٍ جَلَا وَرَبْطُهُ بِأَبْعَدِ النَّقِيضَيْنِ لِيُعْلَمُ اسْتِمْرَارُهُ بِلَا مَيْنِ وَكُلُّهَا فِي مُحْكَمِ الكِتَابِ فَاحْفَظُ لِكَيْ تُهْدَى إِلَى الصَّوَابِ
مثال الاستعمال الأوّل: ﴿وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَرَفَعْتَهُ بِهَا ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٧٦]، وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ﴾ [يونس: ٩٩]، ﴿وَلَوْ عَلِمَ اللهُ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا لَأَسْمَعَهُمْ ﴾ [الأنفال ٢٣] ولغلبته اقتصر الجمهور في تفسيرها عليه فقالوا حرف امتناع لامتناع ; يعنون : لامتناع الثاني لامتناع الأول.
ومثال الاستعمال الثَّاني، وهو المسمَّى عند علماء البيان بالطريقة البرهانية، وهو: «أن تفيد أنَّ العِلم بانتفاء الثَّاني عِلَّةٌ للعلم بانتفاء الأول» ; نحو: وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا وَالهَهُ إِلَّا اللهُ لَفَسَدَت الأنبياء : ۲۲]، ولو كان هَؤُلَاءِ وَالِهَةً مَا وَرَدُّوهَا ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٩٩] ، ولَوْ كَانُوا يَعْلَمُونَ الْغَيْبَ مَا لَبِثُوا فِي الْعَذَابِ الْمُهِينِ ﴾ [سبأ : ١٤]، لَوْ كَانَ خَيْرًا مَا سَبَقُونَا إِلَيْهِ ﴾ [الأحقاف: ١١]، وهذا الوجه أقل استعمالاً من الوجه الأول.
ومثالُ الاستعمال الثَّالث - وهو : أن تفيد استمرار وجود مضمون الجزاء على تقدير، وجود مضمون الشَّرط، وعلى تقدير عدمه بالأولى، وذلك إذا ربط ذلك الجزاء بأبعد النقيضين، فيكون
Chapter on Propositions and Their Judgments
Abu Hafs al-Fasi pointed to the three categories and organized them in his poem:
'They used 'law' for the negation of the consequent due to the negation of a thing, in most cases, / And for the indication of its negation upon the negation of the antecedent with clear proof, / And for linking it to the more distant of the two contradictories so that its continuity is known without delay. / All of them are in the decisive Book, so memorize them that you may be guided to the truth.
Example of the first use: 'And if We had willed, We would have raised him with it,' 'And if your Lord had willed, all who are on earth would have believed,' 'And if Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear.' Due to its prevalence, the majority of commentators restricted their interpretation to it, saying it is a particle of prevention for prevention; meaning: prevention of the second because of prevention of the first.
Example of the second use, which is called by the scholars of eloquence the demonstrative method: it conveys that knowledge of the negation of the consequent is a cause for knowledge of the negation of the antecedent. Like: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have corrupted,' 'If these were gods, they would not have come to it,' 'If they knew the unseen, they would not have remained in humiliating punishment,' 'If it were good, they would not have preceded us to it.' This aspect is less used than the first.
Example of the third use: it conveys the continuity of the existence of the content of the apodosis on the assumption of the existence of the content of the protasis, and on the assumption of its absence by priority. And that is when that apodosis is linked to the more distant of the two contradictories, so it becomes...
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage presents a classification by Abu Hafs al-Fasi of the three uses of the Arabic conditional particle 'law' (لو), encapsulated in a poem for memorization. The first use is the most common: privative conditional (imtina'), where the conditional implies that the consequent is prevented because the antecedent is prevented. The second use is called the 'demonstrative' (burhani) method, where the conditional implies that knowledge of the negation of the consequent proves the negation of the antecedent, essentially a logical contrapositive. The third use indicates the continuity of the apodosis regardless of the protasis, especially when the apodosis is linked to the more distant of two contradictories. Examples from the Quran are provided for each use. This passage is fundamental for understanding how classical scholars analyzed the logical/rhetorical functions of conditionals. The poem serves as a mnemonic device, emphasizing the three distinct categories.

طريقة برهانية
أبعد النقيضين

The three uses of 'law' transform it from a simple conditional to a tool for privation, demonstration, and continuity, key for analyzing Quranic logical arguments.

Three Uses of the Particle 'Law' in the Quran
Commentary analyzes three logical-linguistic uses of the particle 'law'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three logical uses of the particle 'law' in the Quran and classify Quranic verses accordingly.

Core Idea
The Arabic particle 'law' has three distinct logical uses, each conveying a different conditional relationship.

The particle 'law' ('if') is not always a simple conditional. In the Quran, it appears in three distinct logical patterns. This lesson breaks down those uses as identified by classical scholars, helping you read Quranic conditionals with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three uses from the poem
Abu Hafs al-Fasi's poem lists the three uses: (1) negation of consequent due to negation of antecedent, (2) negation of antecedent from negation of consequent (demonstrative), (3) permanence of consequent regardless of antecedent. Note the key phrases: 'negation of the consequent', 'indication of its negation', and 'linking to the farthest opposite'.
2
Match Quranic examples to each use
The passage gives examples: for first use, verses like 7:176, 10:99, 8:23; for second use, 21:22, 21:99, 34:14, 46:11. The third use is incomplete but involves linking to the farthest opposite. Practise by checking which logical pattern each verse follows.
The third use is cut off; refer to other sources for complete examples.
3
Explain the logical function of each use
First use: prevention (imtina') – the antecedent prevents the consequent. Second use: demonstrative (burhani) – the negation of consequent proves negation of antecedent. Third use: permanence – consequent holds whether antecedent is present or absent, with emphasis on the opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عدم الإسماع عدم العلم بالخير فيهم، ثمَّ ابتدأ قوله: ﴿وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَوَلَّوا﴾ [الأنفال : ٢٣] كلاماً آخر على طريقة : «لو لم يخف الله، لم يعصه».
وأجاب في المغني عن هذه الشُّبهة : بأنَّ التَّقدير فى الآية : ولو علم الله تعالى فيهم خيراً وقتاً ما لأسمعهم، ولو أسمعهم لتولُّوا بعد ذلك»، فيكون قياساً فيه شرط الإنتاج؛ أي: ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً وقتاً مَّا لتولّوا بعد ذلك، ولا استحالة فيها حينئذ.
واعترض السَّيِّد قوله : «على قاعدة اللُّغة : بأنَّه يُفهم من ظاهره: أنَّ المعنى الثاني إنَّما هو بحسب الأوضاع الاصطلاحية لأرباب المعقول، وفيه بعد جدا، بل الحقِّ : أنَّه مِنَ المعاني المعتبرة عند أهل اللُّغة الواردة في استعمالاتهم؛ إلَّا أنَّه أقل استعمالاً مِنَ الأوَّل كالثَّالث الآتي، وقد أشار إلى الأقسام الثَّلاثة أبو حفص الفاسي، ونظمها بقوله:

Due to its prevalence, the majority restricted their interpretation to it, saying it is a particle of prevention due to prevention, meaning: prevention of the second due to prevention of the first. Example of the second use, which is called by the scholars of eloquence the demonstrative method: that it conveys that the knowledge of the negation of the second is a cause for knowledge of the negation of the first; like: 'And if there were in them gods other than Allah, they would have been corrupted' [21:22], 'And if these were gods, they would not have come to it' [21:99], 'And if they knew the unseen, they would not have remained in the humiliating punishment' [34:14], 'If it were good, they would not have preceded us to it' [46:11]. And this aspect is less used than the first aspect. And example of the third use - which is: that it conveys the continuation of the existence of the content of the consequent upon the condition of the existence of the content of the condition, and upon the condition of its non-existence with greater reason, and that is when that consequent is linked to the farthest of the two opposites, so it becomes...

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the uses of the Arabic particle 'law' (if) in logical contexts, focusing on Quranic examples. The author begins by addressing a specific interpretation of verse 8:23, where 'law' is used in what is called the 'demonstrative method' (al-tariqa al-burhaniyya). He then cites Abu Hafs al-Fasi's poetic summary of the three uses: first, 'law' indicates prevention of the consequent due to prevention of the antecedent (the common usage); second, it indicates that negation of the consequent proves negation of the antecedent (the demonstrative usage); third, it indicates the permanence of the consequent on both the existence and non-existence of the antecedent, with greater reason when linked to the farthest opposite. The passage then gives multiple Quranic examples for the first two uses. The text is cut off during the explanation of the third use, which would involve examples like the hadith about Suhayb and the narration about Durra. This analysis is important for understanding how conditional language functions in Islamic legal and theological argumentation.

Which of the following Quranic verses is an example of the 'demonstrative' use of 'law' (where negation of the consequent proves negation of the antecedent)?
A) وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَرَفَعْتَهُ بِهَا (7:176)
B) وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا آلِهَةٌ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَتَا (21:22)
C) وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَنْ فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ (10:99)
D) وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَوَلَّوْا (8:23)

Key Takeaway
The particle 'law' in the Quran has three logical uses: prevention, demonstration, and permanence, each serving a distinct argumentative purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following Quranic verses as first, second, or third use of 'law': (a) 8:23, (b) 21:22, (c) 46:11. Explain your reasoning.
?
Write a short paragraph explaining why verse 21:22 is a strong logical proof for the oneness of Allah, using the terminology of the demonstrative method.

The Demonstrative Method: Logical Inference with 'Law'
Commentary analyzes three logical-linguistic uses of the particle 'law'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of the demonstrative use of 'law' as a conditional proof by contrapositive (modus tollens).

Core Idea
The demonstrative use of 'law' is Quranic modus tollens: negate the consequent to deny the antecedent.

The second use of 'law' is called the 'demonstrative method' (tariqa burhaniyya) because it provides a logical proof. It works like this: if the consequent is false, the antecedent must be false. This lesson unpacks that logical structure and shows how it appears in Quranic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
In the demonstrative use, the verse has the form 'لو كان... لـ...' (if... then...). Identify the antecedent (condition) and consequent (result). For example, in 21:22: antecedent = 'there were other gods', consequent = 'they would be corrupted'.
2
Check the truth of the consequent
The argument assumes the consequent is false (the heavens and earth are not corrupted). This is understood from context. Note: the verse itself does not state the negation; it relies on known fact.
3
Apply modus tollens
From 'if P then Q' and 'not Q', we infer 'not P'. So, because corruption is absent, multiple gods are absent. This is a valid deductive inference.
4
Contrast with the first use
The first use only says the antecedent prevents the consequent; it does not allow inference from consequence to antecedent. The demonstrative use goes further, establishing a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها
قصارة .
عدم الإسماع عدم العلم بالخير فيهم، ثمَّ ابتدأ قوله: ﴿وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَوَلَّوا﴾ [الأنفال : ٢٣] كلاماً آخر على طريقة : «لو لم يخف الله، لم يعصه».
وأجاب في المغني عن هذه الشُّبهة : بأنَّ التَّقدير فى الآية : ولو علم الله تعالى فيهم خيراً وقتاً ما لأسمعهم، ولو أسمعهم لتولُّوا بعد ذلك»، فيكون قياساً فيه شرط الإنتاج؛ أي: ولو علم الله فيهم خيراً وقتاً مَّا لتولّوا بعد ذلك، ولا استحالة فيها حينئذ.
واعترض السَّيِّد قوله : «على قاعدة اللُّغة : بأنَّه يُفهم من ظاهره: أنَّ المعنى الثاني إنَّما هو بحسب الأوضاع الاصطلاحية لأرباب المعقول، وفيه بعد جدا، بل الحقِّ : أنَّه مِنَ المعاني المعتبرة عند أهل اللُّغة الواردة في استعمالاتهم؛ إلَّا أنَّه أقل استعمالاً مِنَ الأوَّل كالثَّالث الآتي، وقد أشار إلى الأقسام الثَّلاثة أبو حفص الفاسي، ونظمها بقوله:
وَاسْتَعْمَلُوا «لَوْ فِي انْتِفَاءِ الثَّالِي لِنَفْيِ شَيْءٍ غَالِبَ الأَحْوَالِ وَفِي دَلَالَةِ انْتِفَائِهِ عَلَى نَفْي مُقَدَّمِ بِبُرْهَانٍ جَلَا وَرَبْطُهُ بِأَبْعَدِ النَّقِيضَيْنِ لِيُعْلَمُ اسْتِمْرَارُهُ بِلَا مَيْنِ وَكُلُّهَا فِي مُحْكَمِ الكِتَابِ فَاحْفَظُ لِكَيْ تُهْدَى إِلَى الصَّوَابِ مثال الاستعمال الأوّل: ﴿وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَرَفَعْتَهُ بِهَا ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٧٦]، وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ﴾ [يونس: ٩٩]، ﴿وَلَوْ عَلِمَ اللهُ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا لَأَسْمَعَهُمْ ﴾ [الأنفال ٢٣] ولغلبته اقتصر الجمهور في تفسيرها عليه فقالوا حرف امتناع لامتناع ؛ يعنون : لامتناع الثاني لامتناع الأول. ومثال الاستعمال الثَّاني، وهو المسمَّى عند علماء البيان بالطريقة البرهانية، وهو: «أن تفيد أنَّ العِلم بانتفاء الثَّاني عِلَّةٌ للعلم بانتفاء الأول»؛ نحو: وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا آلِهَةٌ إِلَّا اللهُ لَفَسَدَتَا ﴿الأنبياء : ۲۲]، ولو كان هَؤُلَاءِ آلِهَةً مَا وَرَدُّوهَا ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٩٩]، وَلَوْ كَانُوا يَعْلَمُونَ الْغَيْبَ مَا لَبِثُوا فِي الْعَذَابِ الْمُهِينِ ﴾ [سبأ : ١٤]، لَوْ كَانَ خَيْرًا مَا سَبَقُونَا إِلَيْهِ ﴾ [الأحقاف: ١١]، وهذا الوجه أقل استعمالاً من الوجه الأول.
ومثالُ الاستعمال الثَّالث - وهو : أن تفيد استمرار وجود مضمون الجزاء على تقدير، وجود مضمون الشَّرط، وعلى تقدير عدمه بالأولى، وذلك إذا ربط ذلك الجزاء بأبعد النقيضين، فيكون
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. [Marginal note: ...] Not hearing is not knowing good in them. Then he began His saying: 'And if He made them hear, they would turn away' [8:23] as another speech in the manner: 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.' And he answered in al-Mughni regarding this doubt: that the estimation in the verse is: 'And if Allah knew in them any good at some time, He would make them hear, and if He made them hear, they would turn away after that,' so it is a conditional syllogism with a condition of production; i.e., 'And if Allah knew in them any good at some time, they would turn away after that,' and there is no impossibility then. And al-Sayyid objected to his saying 'according to the rule of language' that from its apparent meaning one understands that the second meaning is only according to the technical conventions of the people of the intellect, and this is very far; rather the truth is that it is among the meanings considered by the people of language occurring in their usages, except that it is less used than the first, like the third coming later. And Abu Hafs al-Fasi pointed to the three divisions and versified them: 'They used law for the negation of the consequent to negate a thing in most cases, and for the indication of its negation to the negation of the antecedent by clear proof, and for linking it to the farthest of the two opposites to know its continuity without flaw. All of them are in the firm Book, so memorize to be guided to correctness. Example of the first use: 'And if We willed, We would have raised him with it' [7:176], 'And if your Lord willed, all those on earth would believe' [10:99], 'And if Allah knew in them any good, He would make them hear' [8:23]. Due to its prevalence, the majority restricted their interpretation to it, saying it is a particle of prevention due to prevention, meaning: prevention of the second due to prevention of the first. Example of the second use, which is called by the scholars of eloquence the demonstrative method: that it conveys that the knowledge of the negation of the second is a cause for knowledge of the negation of the first; like: 'And if there were in them gods other than Allah, they would have been corrupted' [21:22], 'And if these were gods, they would not have come to it' [21:99], 'And if they knew the unseen, they would not have remained in the humiliating punishment' [34:14], 'If it were good, they would not have preceded us to it' [46:11]. And this aspect is less used than the first aspect. And example of the third use - which is: that it conveys the continuation of the existence of the content of the consequent upon the condition of the existence of the content of the condition, and upon the condition of its non-existence with greater reason, and that is when that consequent is linked to the farthest of the two opposites, so it becomes...

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the uses of the Arabic particle 'law' (if) in logical contexts, focusing on Quranic examples. The author begins by addressing a specific interpretation of verse 8:23, where 'law' is used in what is called the 'demonstrative method' (al-tariqa al-burhaniyya). He then cites Abu Hafs al-Fasi's poetic summary of the three uses: first, 'law' indicates prevention of the consequent due to prevention of the antecedent (the common usage); second, it indicates that negation of the consequent proves negation of the antecedent (the demonstrative usage); third, it indicates the permanence of the consequent on both the existence and non-existence of the antecedent, with greater reason when linked to the farthest opposite. The passage then gives multiple Quranic examples for the first two uses. The text is cut off during the explanation of the third use, which would involve examples like the hadith about Suhayb and the narration about Durra. This analysis is important for understanding how conditional language functions in Islamic legal and theological argumentation.

Definition of the Disjunctive Proposition – Scope and Defense
Commentary on the definition of the disjunctive proposition and its defense against the charge of incompleteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical deficiency in the classical definition of the disjunctive proposition and explain the commentator's defense.

Core Idea
A definition that excludes negatives is incomplete, but affirmative can serve as the foundation.

The commentator examines the definition of the disjunctive proposition (al-munfasila) given in the base text: 'that which necessitated a divergence between the two parts.' He immediately points out that this definition is not comprehensive because it only covers affirmative disjunctives, not negative ones. This lesson unpacks that criticism and the justification offered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature
Locate the phrase 'ما أوجبت تنافراً بينهما' (what necessitated a divergence between them). This is the core of the definition. Note that the term 'tanafur' (divergence) is the distinguishing characteristic of a disjunctive proposition.
This definition is taken from the matn (al-Sullam).
2
Spot the criticism
The commentator says the definition is 'غير جامع' (not comprehensive) because it fails to include negative disjunctives. For example, 'ليس العدد زوجاً أو ليس فرداً' (the number is not even or it is not odd) is a valid disjunctive but does not fit the definition as stated.
The term 'سالبة' refers to negative propositions.
3
Understand the defense
The commentator defends the author by arguing that the affirmative is the original (الأصل) and the negative is derived (طارئ). The definition focuses on the original form; negatives are understood by analogy.
This is a common logical principle: the affirmative is prior.
4
Apply the insight to a new example
Consider the disjunctive: 'إما أن يكون هذا الحكم حراماً أو حلالاً' (either this ruling is forbidden or permissible). This is affirmative. Now construct its negative counterpart and check if the same divergence holds.
Negative counterpart: 'إما أن لا يكون حراماً أو لا يكون حلالاً'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (٦١) «مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا» يَعْنِي : أَنَّ المُنْفَصِلَةَ هِيَ : «الَّتِي أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ»، وَلِذَلِكَ سُمِّيَتْ «مُنْفَصِلَةٌ»، وَهُوَ أَيْضاً غَيْرُ جَامِعٍ؛ لِعَدَمِ شُمُولِ السَّالِبَةِ . ثُمَّ المُنْفَصِلَةُ قِسْمَانِ : (۱) عِنَادِيَّةٌ ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي يَكُونُ التَّنَافُرُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا لِمُوجِبٍ مِنْ تَنَاقُضِ، أَوْ تَضَادٌ»؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ : زَوْجاً أَوْ فَرْداً ، وَ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ : شَجَراً أَوْ حَجَراً» . وَقِيلَ : سُمِّيَتْ مُنْفَصِلَةً؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى أَدَاةِ الانْفِصَالِ، وَهِيَ إِمَّا». قَوْلُهُ : (وَهُوَ أَيْضاً غَيْرُ جَامِعٍ؛ لِعَدَمِ شُمُولِ السَّالِبَةِ) يَعْنِي : أَنَّ تَعْرِفَ النَّاظِمِ خَاصٌّ بِالْمُوجِبَةِ؛ نَحْوُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً». فَقَالَ : وَيُجَابُ عَنِ النَّاظِمِ : بِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا اقْتَصَرَ عَلَى الإِيجَابِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ الأَصْلُ، وَالسَّلْبُ طَارِئٌ عَلَيْهِ . قَوْلُهُ : (قِسْمَانِ عِنَادِيَّةٌ) سُمِّيَتْ : «عِنَادِيَّةَ؛ لِتَعَانُدِ طَرَفَيْهَا بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى أَنْفُسِهِمَا . وَتَرَكَ النَّاظِمُ هَذَا التَّقْسِيمَ وَلَمْ يَتَعَرَّضْ لَهُ، وَقَدْ تَعَرَّضَ لَهُ شَيْخُنَا سَيِّدِي حَمْدُونُ فِي «خُرَيْدَتِهِ»، وَانُسُبُ إِلَى العِنَادِ حَيْثُ يُوجَبُ أَوْ لَا، فَلِاتّفَاقِ أَيْضاً تُنْسَبُ قَوْلُهُ : (نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ ... إلخ) هذا المثال غير مطابق لِمَا التَّمْثِيل لَهُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ تَرَكَّبَ
His saying: (61) 'What necessitated a divergence between them' means: that the disjunctive is 'that which necessitated a divergence between the two parts', and due to that it is called 'disjunctive'. And it is also not comprehensive, because it does not include the negative. Then the disjunctive is two divisions: (1) Inadiyya, which is 'that wherein the divergence between its two parts is due to a cause of contradiction or contrariety'; like: 'Either the number is even or odd', and: 'Either the thing is a tree or a stone'. And it is said: it was called disjunctive because it includes the tool of disjunction, which is 'either'. His saying: (And it is also not comprehensive, because it does not include the negative) means: that the author's definition is specific to the affirmative; like: 'Either the number is even, or it is odd'. So he said: And the author is answered: that he only restricted to affirmative because it is the original, and the negative is supervenient upon it. His saying: (Two divisions: Inadiyya) it is called 'inadiyya due to the opposition of its two parts by consideration of themselves. And the author did not mention this division and did not address it, and our master Sayyid Hamdun addressed it in his 'Kharida', and attribute to opposition where it is affirmative or not, so due to agreement also it is attributed. His saying: (Like: 'Either the number... etc.') This example does not correspond to what is exemplified for it, because it is composed...
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining the disjunctive conditional sentence (al-munfasila) and its types. The commentator explains that the author of the matn (likely al-Sullam) defined the disjunctive as 'that which necessitated a divergence between the two parts'. This definition is criticized for not covering negative disjunctives (e.g., 'Either it is not even or it is not odd'). The commentator then notes that the author restricted himself to affirmative because it is the original; negative is derived. Next, the commentator divides the disjunctive into two types: the 'inadiyya' (oppositional), where the divergence between the parts is due to contradiction (like even/odd) or contrariety (like tree/stone). The second type is not fully described in this excerpt but is alluded to in the mention of 'agreement' (ittifaq) from Shaykh Hamdun's Kharida, likely referring to the 'maniat al-jam' wa al-khuluw' (exclusive disjunction). Finally, the commentator critiques an example given in the original text, saying it does not match what is being exemplified because it is 'composed' (possibly a compound disjunction). The passage is crucial for understanding the logical structure of disjunctive propositions in Islamic logic, particularly the distinction between strict opposition and other forms.

منفصلة
تنافر
سالبة

The definition of the disjunctive proposition is built on the affirmative as the original; negatives are derived and not explicitly covered.

The 'Inadiyya' Disjunctive – Oppositional Disjunction
Commentary divides the disjunctive into two types and describes the first (inadiyya) with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the characteristics of the inadiyya disjunctive and distinguish it from other types.

Core Idea
Inadiyya disjuncts are inherently opposite due to contradiction or contrariety.

The commentator divides the disjunctive proposition into two types: the inadiyya and another type not fully described in this excerpt. The inadiyya is defined as a disjunction where the divergence between the parts is due to contradiction (tanāquḍ) or contrariety (taḍādd). This lesson focuses on that type, its definition, and examples.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of inadiyya
Find the phrase: 'عِنَادِيَّةٌ ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي يَكُونُ التَّنَافُرُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا لِمُوجِبٍ مِنْ تَنَاقُضِ، أَوْ تَضَادٌ»'. This tells you the two causes of divergence: contradiction and contrariety.
The term 'inadiyya' comes from 'inād' or 'opposition'.
2
Understand the two causes
Contradiction (tanāquḍ) means the two parts cannot be true together nor false together (e.g., even/odd). Contrariety (taḍādd) means they cannot be true together but can be false together (e.g., tree/stone). Both are forms of opposition.
In logic, contradiction is stricter than contrariety.
3
Examine the examples
The commentator gives examples: 'إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ : زَوْجاً أَوْ فَرْداً' (either the number is even or odd) – this is contradiction. And 'إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ : شَجَراً أَوْ حَجَراً' (either the thing is a tree or a stone) – this is contrariety.
Notice that in contrariety, a thing could be neither tree nor stone (e.g., water).
4
Apply the concept to an Islamic science example
In kalam, a proposition like 'Either the world is eternal or temporally originated' is a disjunction of contradictories (if one is true, the other is false). Identify which cause applies.
This is an example of contradiction (the two cannot both be false).

قَوْلُهُ : (٦١) «مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا» يَعْنِي : أَنَّ المُنْفَصِلَةَ هِيَ : «الَّتِي أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ»، وَلِذَلِكَ سُمِّيَتْ «مُنْفَصِلَةٌ»، وَهُوَ أَيْضاً غَيْرُ جَامِعٍ؛ لِعَدَمِ شُمُولِ السَّالِبَةِ . ثُمَّ المُنْفَصِلَةُ قِسْمَانِ : (۱) عِنَادِيَّةٌ ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي يَكُونُ التَّنَافُرُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا لِمُوجِبٍ مِنْ تَنَاقُضِ، أَوْ تَضَادٌ»؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ : زَوْجاً أَوْ فَرْداً ، وَ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ : شَجَراً أَوْ حَجَراً» . وَقِيلَ : سُمِّيَتْ مُنْفَصِلَةً؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى أَدَاةِ الانْفِصَالِ، وَهِيَ إِمَّا». قَوْلُهُ : (وَهُوَ أَيْضاً غَيْرُ جَامِعٍ؛ لِعَدَمِ شُمُولِ السَّالِبَةِ) يَعْنِي : أَنَّ تَعْرِفَ النَّاظِمِ خَاصٌّ بِالْمُوجِبَةِ؛ نَحْوُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً». فَقَالَ : وَيُجَابُ عَنِ النَّاظِمِ : بِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا اقْتَصَرَ عَلَى الإِيجَابِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ الأَصْلُ، وَالسَّلْبُ طَارِئٌ عَلَيْهِ . قَوْلُهُ : (قِسْمَانِ عِنَادِيَّةٌ) سُمِّيَتْ : «عِنَادِيَّةَ؛ لِتَعَانُدِ طَرَفَيْهَا بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى أَنْفُسِهِمَا . وَتَرَكَ النَّاظِمُ هَذَا التَّقْسِيمَ وَلَمْ يَتَعَرَّضْ لَهُ، وَقَدْ تَعَرَّضَ لَهُ شَيْخُنَا سَيِّدِي حَمْدُونُ فِي «خُرَيْدَتِهِ»، وَانُسُبُ إِلَى العِنَادِ حَيْثُ يُوجَبُ أَوْ لَا، فَلِاتّفَاقِ أَيْضاً تُنْسَبُ قَوْلُهُ : (نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ ... إلخ) هذا المثال غير مطابق لِمَا التَّمْثِيل لَهُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ تَرَكَّبَ
His saying: (61) 'What necessitated a divergence between them' means: that the disjunctive is 'that which necessitated a divergence between the two parts', and due to that it is called 'disjunctive'. And it is also not comprehensive, because it does not include the negative. Then the disjunctive is two divisions: (1) Inadiyya, which is 'that wherein the divergence between its two parts is due to a cause of contradiction or contrariety'; like: 'Either the number is even or odd', and: 'Either the thing is a tree or a stone'. And it is said: it was called disjunctive because it includes the tool of disjunction, which is 'either'. His saying: (And it is also not comprehensive, because it does not include the negative) means: that the author's definition is specific to the affirmative; like: 'Either the number is even, or it is odd'. So he said: And the author is answered: that he only restricted to affirmative because it is the original, and the negative is supervenient upon it. His saying: (Two divisions: Inadiyya) it is called 'inadiyya due to the opposition of its two parts by consideration of themselves. And the author did not mention this division and did not address it, and our master Sayyid Hamdun addressed it in his 'Kharida', and attribute to opposition where it is affirmative or not, so due to agreement also it is attributed. His saying: (Like: 'Either the number... etc.') This example does not correspond to what is exemplified for it, because it is composed...
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining the disjunctive conditional sentence (al-munfasila) and its types. The commentator explains that the author of the matn (likely al-Sullam) defined the disjunctive as 'that which necessitated a divergence between the two parts'. This definition is criticized for not covering negative disjunctives (e.g., 'Either it is not even or it is not odd'). The commentator then notes that the author restricted himself to affirmative because it is the original; negative is derived. Next, the commentator divides the disjunctive into two types: the 'inadiyya' (oppositional), where the divergence between the parts is due to contradiction (like even/odd) or contrariety (like tree/stone). The second type is not fully described in this excerpt but is alluded to in the mention of 'agreement' (ittifaq) from Shaykh Hamdun's Kharida, likely referring to the 'maniat al-jam' wa al-khuluw' (exclusive disjunction). Finally, the commentator critiques an example given in the original text, saying it does not match what is being exemplified because it is 'composed' (possibly a compound disjunction). The passage is crucial for understanding the logical structure of disjunctive propositions in Islamic logic, particularly the distinction between strict opposition and other forms.

تناقض
تضاد

Key Takeaway
The inadiyya disjunctive is defined by its parts being in opposition through contradiction or contrariety.

Defining Coincidental Disjunction (Ittifaqiyya)
Defines ittifaqiyya with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a coincidental disjunction (ittifaqiyya) and distinguish it from a true disjunction (haqiqiyya) using the criterion of necessary vs. accidental opposition.

Core Idea
In ittifaqiyya, disjuncts conflict only by coincidence, not by logical necessity.

In the logic of propositions, a disjunction can be either necessary (haqiqiyya) or coincidental (ittifaqiyya). The author now defines the second type: ittifaqiyya, where the truth values differ accidentally. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments that use disjunctive premises, especially in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjuncts
Look at the two parts connected by 'either...or'. In the example: 'Either man is an animal' and 'the donkey is inanimate'. Are these two statements related in meaning?
2
Check for inherent opposition
Ask: Is it impossible for both to be true or both false? For ittifaqiyya, there is no inherent opposition; one could be true and the other false accidentally.
3
Determine the truth values
Assess the actual truth values. In the example, 'man is an animal' is true, 'donkey is inanimate' is false. But note: the truth of the first does not cause the falsity of the second.
4
Contrast with haqiqiyya
Compare with a true disjunction like 'The prayer is either valid or invalid'. Here, opposition is necessary by the nature of the rulings.
This contrast is key for usul al-fiqh when analyzing disjunctive legal options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢) - وَاتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي يَكُونُ التَّنَافُرُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا لِغَيْرِ مُوجِبٍ؛ بِأَنْ لَا يُنَافِي أَحَدُهُمَا الآخَرَ ، لَكِن اتَّفَقَ أَنْ صَدَقَ أَحَدُهُمَا وَكَذَبَ الآخَرُ، وَلَيْسَ صِدْقُ صَادِقُهُمَا هُوَ الَّذِي أَوْجَبَ كَذِبَ الآخَرِ، وَلَا العَكْسُ ؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الإِنْسَانُ حَيَوَاناً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الحِمَارُ جَمَاداً » ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا تَنَا فِي بَيْنَهُمَا ، لَكِنِ اتَّفَقَ أَنْ صَدَقَ الأَوَّلُ وَكَذَبَ الثَّانِي
(2) - And the coincidental: it is that the conflict between its two sides is without a cause; meaning that one does not oppose the other, but it accidentally happens that one is true and the other false, and the truth of the true one is not what caused the falsity of the other, nor the opposite; example: 'Either man is an animal, or the donkey is inanimate' — for there is no opposition between them, but it accidentally happens that the first is true and the second false.
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of disjunctive proposition: the coincidental disjunction (ittifaqiyya). The author explains that in this type, the two disjuncts are not in genuine opposition; rather, their truth values happen to differ coincidentally, without one causing the other's falsehood. The example given is 'Either man is an animal, or the donkey is inanimate.' Both statements are unrelated in meaning—'man is an animal' is true, 'donkey is inanimate' is false—but their truth values do not arise from any logical conflict between them. This contrasts with the true disjunction (haqiqiyya) where the opposition is necessary.

Key Terms
اتِّفَاقِيَّة
مُنْفَصِلَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Either water is wet or fire is cold.

Key Takeaway
Coincidental disjunction (ittifaqiyya) occurs when disjuncts conflict only by coincidence, not by logical necessity; this is crucial for properly handling disjunctive propositions in Islamic logic and jurisprudence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Three Types of Disjunctive Propositions (Munfasila)
Author enumerates the three types of munfasila with a poetic summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the three types of disjunctive propositions (haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khulu) and explain their truth conditions.

Core Idea
Disjunctions are not all equal: they differ in whether they forbid both true, both false, or both.

In this passage, the author lays out the threefold classification of disjunctive propositions. This classification is crucial for understanding how disjunctions behave logically, especially in argumentation and legal reasoning. The poetic summary helps you memorize the key distinction: some disjunctions prevent both disjuncts from being true, some prevent both from being false, and the true disjunction (haqiqiyya) prevents both.

Guided Path
1
Learn the three names
Memorize the three Arabic terms: haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khulu. They correspond to the three types of disjunctions in the text.
The poem says: 'Its divisions are three: preventing conjunction, or void, or both – and it is the true, the most specific.'
2
Understand the truth conditions for each
For each type, determine what combinations of truth values are allowed. Mani'at al-jam' forbids both true but allows both false. Mani'at al-khulu forbids both false but allows both true. Haqiqiyya forbids both true and both false.
Think of 'jam'' as joining (both true) and 'khulu' as emptiness (both false).
3
Connect the poem to the definitions
The poem says 'or both' – this refers to the type that prevents both jam' and khulu, which is the haqiqiyya. Verify that haqiqiyya is the intersection of the two restrictions.
The haqiqiyya is the most specific because it has the strongest conditions.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Practice by classifying real Islamic disjunctions. For example, 'This act is either wajib or haram' – can it be both? No. Can it be neither? Yes, it could be mubah. So it is mani'at al-jam'? Wait, check: If it cannot be both true but can be both false (if it's neither wajib nor haram), then it is mani'at al-jam'. But careful: The act could be both? No. So yes, that fits. Another example: 'The deceased is either male or female' – cannot be both, cannot be neither. That is haqiqiyya.
Use the truth condition test to determine the type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ تَنْقَسِمُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : حَقِيقِيَّةٍ، وَمَانِعَةِ جَمْعِ، وَمَانِعَةِ خُلُو. وَأَشَارَ إِلَى ذَلِكَ بِقَوْلِهِ : (٦١) أَقْسَامُهَا ثَلَاثَةٌ فَلْتُعْلَمَا (٦٢) مَانِعُ جَمْعٍ ، أَوْ خُلُو، أَوْ هُمَا وَهُوَ الحَقِيقِيُّ الأَخَصُّ فَاعْلَمَا
Then the disjunctive proposition divides into three divisions: true (haqiqiyya), preventing conjunction (mani'a jam'), and preventing void (mani'a khulu). And he indicated that with his saying: 'Its divisions are three, so let them be known: preventing conjunction, or void, or both—and it is the true (al-haqiqi), the most specific, so know.'
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage lays out the threefold classification of disjunctive propositions (munfasila). The author states that a disjunctive proposition falls into one of three types: (1) haqiqiyya — the true disjunction, where the disjuncts cannot both be true and cannot both be false; (2) mani'a jam' — preventing conjunction, where the disjuncts cannot both be true but can both be false; (3) mani'a khulu — preventing void, where the disjuncts cannot both be false but can both be true. The author then cites a poetic verse that summarizes these three types: the third type, 'both' (i.e., preventing both conjunction and void), is the haqiqiyya, the most specific. This classification is foundational for understanding the logical behavior of disjunctions.

Key Terms
حَقِيقِيَّة (haqiqiyya): True disjunction: a disjunction where the two disjuncts cannot both be true and cannot both be false; they mutually exclude each other in both truth and falsehood. [Note: Stable; also called 'al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya'.]
مَانِعَةُ جَمْع (mani'at al-jam'): Exclusive disjunction that prevents conjunction: the disjuncts cannot both be true but can both be false. [Note: Stable.]
مَانِعَةُ خُلُو (mani'at al-khulu): Exclusive disjunction that prevents void: the disjuncts cannot both be false but can both be true. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the threefold division, which type of munfasila prevents both conjunction and void?
A) Mani'at al-jam'
B) Mani'at al-khulu
C) Haqiqiyya
D) Ittifaqiyya

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive propositions are not uniform; they are classified into three types based on whether they exclude both disjuncts being true, both being false, or both, which is essential for correct logical inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following disjunctions from Islamic law into one of the three types, and justify: (a) 'A prayer is either valid or invalid.' (b) 'This food is either halal or haram.' (c) 'The imam is either in a state of wudu or not in a state of wudu.'
?
Memorize the poetic verse from the passage and write it out. Then, for each line, explain how it corresponds to the definitions of the three types.

The True Disjunction (Haqiqiyya) and Its Composition
Author defines haqiqiyya and specifies its composition from contradictories or equivalents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a disjunctive proposition is a haqiqiyya by checking its composition from contradictories or equivalents.

Core Idea
A haqiqiyya requires that the disjuncts be contradictories or equivalents, not just any opposites.

In this passage, the author defines the true disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) as one where the two sides cannot both be true nor both false. More importantly, he specifies that its affirmative form must be composed of a thing and its direct negation, or something equivalent to that negation. This precision distinguishes haqiqiyya from other disjunctions. Let's unpack the definition and the logic behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition of haqiqiyya
The author states that a haqiqiyya disjunction cannot have both disjuncts true, nor both false; one must be true and the other false. Write down this condition in your own words.
This is the defining property that distinguishes haqiqiyya from man'i jam' and man'i khuluw.
2
Examine the composition requirement
The author says the affirmative contradictory (al-mujaba al-'inadiyya) can only be composed from a thing and its negation (naqid) or something equal to its negation (musawi naqid). Why is this necessary? Because only such pairs guarantee the truth division.
The term 'mujaba 'inadiyya' refers to the true affirmative of the disjunction.
3
Analyze the two examples
First example: 'Either the existent is eternal, or it is not eternal' – uses direct negation. Second example: 'Either the existent is eternal, or it is originated' – here 'originated' is equal to 'not eternal' when applied to existent things. See how the second example uses an equivalent instead of the direct negation.
The commentator clarifies that the intended meaning is more general: composition is not limited to strict contradictories but includes equivalents.
4
Check the logic with a real Islamic example
Consider a proposition from kalam: 'The world is either eternal or originated.' Is this a haqiqiyya? Since 'originated' is equivalent to 'not eternal' in the context of the world, it qualifies. Test other pairs from Islamic theology.
Always ensure that the disjuncts are in a relation of contradiction or equivalence in that domain.

فَالحَقِيقِيَّةُ هِيَ : الَّتِي لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ، وَلَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ، بَلْ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ صِدْقِ أَحَدِهِمَا وَكَذِبِ الآخَرِ ، وَلَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مُوْجَبَتُهَا العِنَادِيَّةُ الصَّادِقَةُ إِلَّا مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ أَوْ مُسَاوِي نَقِيضِهِ : قصارة مِنَ الشَّيء والمساوي لنقيضه، والمطابق أن يقول: «إما أن يكون الموجود قديماً، وإما أنَّ لا يكون قديماً». ويمكن الجواب عنِ الشَّارح : بأنَّه قصد بالمثالين التنبيه على أنَّ المقصود ما هو أعم مما ذكر، فليس تركيبها خاصًا بالتناقض والتضاد. قول النَّاظم : (أَقْسَامُهَا ثَلَاثَةٌ ... إلخ) : قوله : (فَالحَقِيقِيَّةُ هِيَ : الَّتِي لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ، وَلَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ» وقد أشار لهذا المعنى شيخنا سيدي حمدون في «الخريدة»، فقال: ثُمَّ الحَقِيقِيَّةُ مَاقَدْ مَنَعَا فِيهَا اجْتِمَاعُ الصِّدْقِ وَالمَيْنَ معا قوله : (وَلَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مُوْجَبَتُهَا العِنَادِيَّةُ) أشار بهذا : إلى أنَّ التَّعاريف التي ذكروا للمنفصلات الثَّلاث إنَّما هي لموجبتها، وأمَّا سالبة كلِّ منها ، فهي التي حكم فيها بسلب معنى موجبتها . قوله : (إِلَّا مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ ... إلخ) يعني : لأنَّه بذلك يقتسم طرفاها الصدق والكذب؛ لأنَّ النَّقيضين لا يجتمعان على صدقٍ ولا كذب وكذا ما يساويهما، وهو واضح، وإلى كون الحقيقيَّة تتركَّب ممَّا ذكر ، أشار صاحب «القادريَّة」 بقوله : وَمِنْ نَقِيضَيْنِ وَمِمَّا قَدْ بَدَا مُسَاوِيـــا لــذيـــن رُكْبَ أَبَــــدا فَالنَّقِيضَانِ ؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ قَدِيماً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ قَدِيماً». - وَالمُسَاوِي لِلنَّقِيض ; نَحْوُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ قَدِيماً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَادِثاً»، فَنَقِيضُ قَدِيم»: «لَا قَدِيمَ وَهُوَ مُسَاوِ لِـ «حَادِثِ ، وَنَقِيضُ «حَادِثِ»: «لَا حَادِثَ»، وَهُوَ مُسَاءٍ لِـ «قَدِيمِ» عِنْدَ حَمْلِهِمَا عَلَى المَوْجُودِ، كَمَا فِي المِثَالِ، وَإِلَّا فَالسَّلْبُ أَعَمُّ لِصِدْقِهِ بِمَا لَمْ يُوجَدْ، وَلِكُوْنِ التَّنَافُرِ فِيهِمَا فِي جِهَتَيِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ (۱) سُمِّيَتْ : حَقِيقِيَّةٌ
The true (haqiqiyya) is: that whose two sides cannot both be true, nor both false, but necessarily one is true and the other false. And its affirmative contradictory (mujabatuha al-'inadiyya) that is true is not composed except from a thing and its negation or something equal to its negation: from the thing and what is equal to its negation. The exact thing is to say: 'Either the existent is eternal, or it is not eternal.' And it is possible to answer the commentator: that he intended by the two examples to indicate that the intended meaning is more general than what he mentioned, so its composition is not specific to contradiction and opposition. The saying of the author: 'Its divisions are three ... etc.': His saying: 'The true is that whose two sides cannot both be true, nor both false' — and our master Sayyidi Hamdun in 'al-Kharida' indicated this meaning, saying: 'Then the true is that which prevents both truth and falsehood together.' His saying: 'And its affirmative contradictory is not composed' — he indicated by this that the definitions they mentioned for the three disjunctions are only for their affirmatives, and as for the negative of each, it is that in which the negation of the meaning of its affirmative is ruled. His saying: 'Except from a thing and its negation ... etc.' means: because thereby its two sides divide truth and falsehood; because the two contradictories do not combine in truth nor falsehood, and likewise what is equal to them. And it is clear. And that the true is composed of what he mentioned, the author of 'al-Qadiriyya' indicated by his saying: 'And from two contradictories and from what appears equal to those two, it is ever composed.' So the two contradictories: example: 'Either the existent is eternal, or it is not eternal.' And what is equal to the negation: example: 'Either the existent is eternal, or it is originated.' So the negation of 'eternal' is 'not eternal', and it is equal to 'originated', and the negation of 'originated' is 'not originated', and it is equal to 'eternal' when they are applied to the existent, as in the example. Otherwise, the negative is more general because it applies to what does not exist. And because the conflict between them is in both aspects of truth and falsehood, it is called 'haqiqiyya' (true).
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed definition of the true disjunction (haqiqiyya) and explains its necessary composition. The author begins by restating the definition: a haqiqiyya is a disjunction where the two disjuncts cannot both be true nor both false; one must be true and the other false. He then specifies a crucial point: the affirmative contradictory (mujabatuha al-'inadiyya) that is true can only be composed of a thing and its negation (naqid) or something equal (musawi) to its negation. For example, 'Either the existent is eternal, or it is not eternal' uses a thing and its direct negation. Another valid composition is 'Either the existent is eternal, or it is originated' where 'originated' (hadith) is equal to the negation of 'eternal' (i.e., 'not eternal') in the context of existent things. The commentary clarifies that this composition is not limited to strict contradictories but includes equivalents. The author then responds to an objection regarding the commentator's examples, stating they are meant to show the generality of the principle. He also cites verses from al-Kharida and al-Qadiriyya that reinforce the same point. Finally, he explains that the term 'haqiqiyya' is used because the conflict between the disjuncts is real and applies to both truth and falsehood aspects, unlike the other two types where conflict is only partial.

First Interpretation of Mani'at al-Jam' (Preventing Conjunction)
Author gives first interpretation of mani'at al-jam' with definition and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics and composition condition of mani'at al-jam' as per its first interpretation.

Core Idea
Mani'at al-jam' prevents conjunction but allows both false, requiring a specific composition condition.

This passage introduces the first and more specific interpretation of a key disjunction type in Islamic logic: mani'at al-jam'. Understanding its precise definition and the condition for its affirmative contradictory is essential for correctly analyzing disjunctive propositions in Islamic sciences. We will break down the author's words to see exactly what distinguishes this type from other disjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined: 'mani'at al-jam'' (preventing conjunction). Note that the author says it has two interpretations, and this is the more specific (akhas) one.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'disjunction' (a compound proposition with two sides). The differentia is 'prevents conjunction only' – meaning it prohibits both sides being true together, but allows both to be false. Compare with other disjunctions that also prevent conjunction but may not allow both false.
This differentia distinguishes it from haqiqiyya, which prevents both conjunction and avoidance.
3
Understand the composition condition
The author says the affirmative contradictory (muwajibatuhu al-'inadiyya al-sadiqa) can only be composed from a thing and what is more specific than its negation. For example, 'white' and 'black' – black is more specific than 'not white' because every black is not white, but not every not-white is black. This condition ensures the disjuncts are not exhaustive and allows both false.
This condition is crucial for constructing valid examples of mani'at al-jam'.
4
Analyze the example
The text begins an example: 'Either the garment is white, or it is...' but cuts off. Based on the condition, the missing disjunct should be a colour more specific than 'not white', such as 'black' or 'green'. This would satisfy that both cannot be true together (a garment cannot be both white and black at the same spot), but both can be false (e.g., if the garment is red).
The incomplete example is due to manuscript break; we infer the likely conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلِمَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ تَفْسِيرَانِ : أَحَدُهُمَا أَخَصُّ ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي تَمْنَعُ الجَمْعَ فَقَط ; بِأَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ، وَيَجْتَمِعَانِ عَلَى الكَذِبِ، وَهَذِهِ لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مُوجَبَتُهَا العِنَادِيَّةُ الصَّادِقَةُ إِلَّا [أ/ ٤٠] مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَمَا هُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الثَّوْبُ أَبْيَضَ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ ...
And for 'preventing conjunction' there are two interpretations: one of them is more specific, and it is: 'that which prevents conjunction only; meaning its two sides cannot both be true, but they can both be false. And this type's affirmative contradictory that is true is not composed except from a thing and what is more specific than its negation; example: 'Either the garment is white, or it is ...'
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage introduces the first interpretation of the disjunction type known as 'mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction). According to this more specific interpretation, a mani'at al-jam' is a disjunction where the two disjuncts cannot both be true (it prevents conjunction) but they can both be false. The author then specifies a condition for its affirmative contradictory: it must be composed of a thing and something more specific (akhas) than its negation. An example is given: 'Either the garment is white, or it is ...' but the text cuts off. The example likely contrasts 'white' with a more specific colour, such as 'black', which is a specific type of 'not white'. This shows that the disjuncts are not exhaustive; they exclude each other only in truth, not in falsehood. The passage sets the stage for the second interpretation, which follows later.
