Real Disjunction vs. Equivalent to Contradiction
Author defines real disjunction and equivalent to contradiction with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real disjunction (haqiqiyya) and a disjunction equivalent to contradiction (musawi lil-naqid), and identify which type applies to given examples from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Real disjunction uses exact contradictories; equivalent type uses opposites only within a domain like 'eternal' and 'originated' for existents.

The passage introduces two foundational types of disjunctive propositions: the real disjunction where the two sides are direct contradictories, and the equivalent type where they are mutually exclusive only under a specific domain. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing theological and juristic arguments that use 'either-or' statements, such as in debates about the attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The author begins by distinguishing 'al-naqidan' (the two contradictories) and 'al-musawi lil-naqid' (the equivalent of the contradictory). These are the two basic categories of disjunction.
2
Examine the haqiqiyya example
The example 'Either the existent is eternal or it is not eternal' shows genuine contradictories: cannot both be true nor both false. The author says this opposition in both truth and falsehood is why it is called 'haqiqiyya'.
3
Examine the musawi lil-naqid example
The example 'Either the existent is eternal or it is originated' is not an exact contradictory pair because 'originated' is equivalent to 'not eternal' only when applied to the existent. The author explains that 'not eternal' is broader as it covers non-existents.
4
Test with a theological example
Consider the statement 'The Quran is either created or uncreated.' Identify whether this is haqiqiyya or musawi lil-naqid. Since 'created' and 'uncreated' are contradictories when applied to the Quran, this is a haqiqiyya disjunction.
5
Generalize the difference
The key is whether the disjuncts are exact logical opposites (haqiqiyya) or opposites only within a specific context (musawi lil-naqid). This affects the logical behavior in arguments, such as whether the disjunction is exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالنَّقِيضَانِ ; نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ قَدِيماً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ قَدِيماً». - وَالمُسَاوِي لِلنَّقِيض ; نَحْوُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ قَدِيماً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَادِثاً»، فَنَقِيضُ قَدِيم»: «لَا قَدِيمَ وَهُوَ مُسَاوِ لِـ «حَادِثِ ، وَنَقِيضُ «حَادِثِ»: «لَا حَادِثَ»، وَهُوَ مُسَاءٍ لِـ «قَدِيمِ» عِنْدَ حَمْلِهِمَا عَلَى المَوْجُودِ، كَمَا فِي المِثَالِ، وَإِلَّا فَالسَّلْبُ أَعَمُّ لِصِدْقِهِ بِمَا لَمْ يُوجَدْ، وَلِكُوْنِ التَّنَافُرِ فِيهِمَا فِي جِهَتَيِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ سُمِّيَتْ : حَقِيقِيَّةٌ». وَلِمَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ تَفْسِيرَانِ : أَحَدُهُمَا أَخَصُّ ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي تَمْنَعُ الجَمْعَ فَقَط ; بِأَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ، وَيَجْتَمِعَانِ عَلَى الكَذِبِ، وَهَذِهِ لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مُوجَبَتُهَا العِنَادِيَّةُ الصَّادِقَةُ إِلَّا مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَمَا هُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الثَّوْبُ أَبْيَضَ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ أَسْوَدَ ، فَنَقِيضُ أَبْيَضَ»: «لَا أَبْيَضَ ، وَأَسْوَدُ أَخَصُّ مِنْهُ؛ كَمَا أَنَّ أَبْيَضَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِ أَسْوَدَ ، وَهُوَ لَا أَسْوَدَ ، فَلَوْ صَدَقَا مَعاً ، لَلَزِمَ صِدْقُ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ صِدْقَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ صِدْقَ نَقِيض الآخَرِ مِنْ أَجْلِ أَنَّ صِدْقَ الأَخَصُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ صِدْقَ الأَعَمّ، وَصِدْقُ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضُهُ مَحَالٌ . وَإِنَّمَا صَحَ اجْتِمَاعُهُمَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ؛ لِأَنَّ كَذِبَ الأَخَصُ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ كَذِبَ الأَعَمِّ، فَلَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ كَذِبِهِمَا كَذِبُ الشَّيْءِ مَعَ نَقِيضِهِ. الثَّانِي: أَنْ تُفَسَّرَ بِمَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ مِمَّا ذُكِرَ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ؛ سَوَاءٌ اجْتَمَعَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ أَمْ لَا ، فَتَكُونُ بِهَذَا المَعْنَى شَامِلَةٌ لِلحَقِيقِيَّةِ. وَكَذَا مَانِعَةُ الخُلُوِّ لَهَا تَفْسِيرَانِ : - أَحَدُهُمَا أَخَصُّ ، وَهِيَ : الَّتِي تَمْنَعُ الكَذِبَ فَقَط»؛ بِأَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعَ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ، وَيَجْتَمِعَانِ عَلَى الصِّدْقِ . سُمِّيَتْ : مَانِعَةَ خُلُو» ; لِأَنَّ طَرَفَيْهَا يَمْتَنِعُ خُلُو الوُجُودِ عَنْهُمَا.
The two contradictions are like: 'Either the existent is eternal or it is not eternal.' And the equivalent of the contradiction is like: 'Either the existent is eternal or it is originated.' So the contradictory of 'eternal' is 'not eternal', and it is equivalent to 'originated', and the contradictory of 'originated' is 'not originated', and it is equivalent to 'eternal' when both are predicated of the existent, as in the example. Otherwise the negative is more general because it applies to what does not exist. And because the opposition in them is in both aspects of truth and falsehood, it is called 'haqiqiyya' (real). And for the exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam') there are two interpretations: One is more specific, and it is that which prevents conjunction only, i.e., its two sides cannot both be true, but can both be false. And this one's true adversative affirmative is composed only of a thing and what is more specific than its contradictory, like: 'Either the cloth is white or it is black.' So the contradictory of 'white' is 'not white', and 'black' is more specific than it; just as 'white' is more specific than the contradictory of 'black', which is 'not black'. So if they were both true, then the truth of a thing and its contradictory would necessarily follow, because the truth of each entails the truth of the other's contradictory, because the truth of the more specific entails the truth of the more general, and the truth of a thing and its contradictory is impossible. And it is possible for them to both be false because the falsehood of the more specific does not entail the falsehood of the more general, so from their falsehood the falsehood of the thing together with its contradictory does not follow. The second: that it is interpreted more generally than what was mentioned, and it is: that whose two sides cannot both be true, whether they can both be false or not. So in this sense it includes the haqiqiyya. Similarly, the inclusive disjunction (man'at al-khulu) has two interpretations: One is more specific, and it is that which prevents falsehood only, i.e., its two sides cannot both be false, but can both be true. It is called 'man'at al-khulu' because its two sides prevent the void of existence from being without them.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila) in logic. It begins by distinguishing two basic types based on the relationship between the two disjuncts. The first type is called 'haqiqiyya' (real disjunction), where the two sides are genuine contradictories (naqidan), like 'Either the existent is eternal or it is not eternal'. Here, the two sides cannot both be true nor both false; they are exact opposites. The second type is called 'musawi lil-naqid' (equivalent to contradiction), where the two sides are not direct contradictories but are mutually exclusive under a specific domain, like 'Either the existent is eternal or it is originated'. In this example, when predicated of the existent, 'eternal' and 'originated' are opposites: the contradictory of 'eternal' is 'not eternal', which is equivalent to 'originated' for existents. But generally, 'not eternal' is broader because it includes non-existent things as well. The author then explains that the opposition in the first type (haqiqiyya) is real because it holds in both truth and falsehood (i.e., both cannot be true and both cannot be false). Next, the text delves into the exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam'), which prevents the conjunction (jam') of its sides—meaning they cannot both be true. This type has two interpretations: a narrower one and a broader one. The narrower interpretation is that the two sides cannot both be true, but they can both be false. An example is 'Either the cloth is white or it is black'. Here, 'white' and 'black' are not contradictories (since a cloth can be neither, e.g., red), but they are specific contraries. The text explains that in such a disjunction, the affirmative form (al-mujaba al-'inadiyya) must be composed of a thing and something more specific than its contradictory. For example, 'white' and 'black' are more specific than 'not white' and 'not black' respectively. If both were true (i.e., the cloth is both white and black), then the truth of 'white' would entail the truth of 'not black' (since black is more specific, its contradictory is more general, and truth of the specific entails truth of the general? Actually it's the opposite: truth of the general is entailed by truth of the specific. Wait: The passage says 'the truth of the more specific entails the truth of the more general'. So if 'black' is true, then 'not white' is true? No, 'not white' is more general? Let's analyze: 'black' is more specific than 'not white'? Actually 'black' implies 'not white' (since it's a color opposite), so 'black' is more specific and 'not white' is more general. So if 'black' is true, 'not white' is true. Similarly, if 'white' is true, 'not black' is true. So if both 'white' and 'black' are true, then both 'not white' and 'not black' are true, leading to a contradiction (white and not white, black and not black). So the disjunction prevents conjunction. They can both be false (e.g., the cloth is red) because falsehood of a specific does not entail falsehood of the more general. The broader interpretation of man'at al-jam' is simply that the two sides cannot both be true, regardless of whether they can both be false. This broader sense includes the haqiqiyya (which also cannot both be false). Finally, the text discusses the inclusive disjunction (man'at al-khulu), which prevents the void (khulu) of its sides, meaning they cannot both be false. This also has two interpretations. The narrower interpretation is that the two sides cannot both be false, but they can both be true. It is called 'man'at al-khulu' because the two sides prevent existence from being empty of both—i.e., at least one must be true. An example would be 'Either the number is even or it is odd' (where they can't both be false but can't both be true either? Actually even and odd are exclusive, so they can't both be true, so that would be haqiqiyya. Another example: 'Either this liquid is water or it is not water' — that is a tautology. But the text does not give an explicit example here. The second interpretation (broader) is not given in this extract, but it would be that they cannot both be false, regardless of truth—which would include the haqiqiyya as well.

A) Because eternal and originated are not real opposites.
B) Because the negative (not eternal) is broader than 'originated'.
C) Because they cannot both be false.
D) Because they are exact contradictories.

Key Takeaway
A real disjunction requires exact logical contradictories; a disjunction equivalent to contradiction holds only within a specific domain.

Exclusive and Inclusive Disjunctions: Narrow and Broad Interpretations
Author defines exclusive and inclusive disjunctions with their narrow and broad interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the narrow and broad interpretations of exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and inclusive (mani'at al-khulu) disjunctions, and apply them to analyze Islamic legal and theological examples.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunction prevents both being true; inclusive disjunction prevents both being false; each has narrow and broad senses.

After establishing the two fundamental types, the author delves into the specific categories of exclusive and inclusive disjunctions. These are not just two types but have interpretative layers: a narrow sense that allows one opposite possibility (e.g., both false for exclusive, both true for inclusive) and a broad sense that is more strict. Knowing these nuances prevents errors in logical analysis, especially when evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two families
The author states that exclusive (man'at al-jam') has two interpretations and inclusive (man'at al-khulu) has two interpretations. First, understand the narrow interpretations.
2
Narrow exclusive: cannot both be true, can both be false
The narrow exclusive 'prevents conjunction only' – the sides cannot both be true but can both be false. Example: 'The cloth is white or black.' The author proves that if both were true, it would lead to a contradiction (thing and its contradictory), but both can be false (e.g., red cloth).
3
Broad exclusive: only prevents conjunction, regardless of void
The broad interpretation is simply that the two sides cannot both be true, whether or not they can both be false. This includes the haqiqiyya (which also prevents void) and the narrow exclusive.
4
Narrow inclusive: cannot both be false, can both be true
The narrow inclusive 'prevents falsehood only' – the sides cannot both be false but can both be true. It is called 'man'at al-khulu' because it prevents the void (both false). Example (not in text): 'Either he is a scholar or he is pious' could both be true.
5
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider the ruling on liquids for wudu: 'Water is either pure (tahir) or impure (najis).' Is this narrow exclusive, broad exclusive, or something else? Since water cannot be both pure and impure, and it can be neither if it is doubtful? Actually it is always either pure or impure, so it's haqiqiyya, which is a broad exclusive. To find a narrow exclusive, consider 'The liquid is either water or milk' – these cannot both be true, but both can be false (if it is juice).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالنَّقِيضَانِ ; نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ قَدِيماً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ قَدِيماً». - وَالمُسَاوِي لِلنَّقِيض ; نَحْوُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ قَدِيماً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَادِثاً»، فَنَقِيضُ قَدِيم»: «لَا قَدِيمَ وَهُوَ مُسَاوِ لِـ «حَادِثِ ، وَنَقِيضُ «حَادِثِ»: «لَا حَادِثَ»، وَهُوَ مُسَاءٍ لِـ «قَدِيمِ» عِنْدَ حَمْلِهِمَا عَلَى المَوْجُودِ، كَمَا فِي المِثَالِ، وَإِلَّا فَالسَّلْبُ أَعَمُّ لِصِدْقِهِ بِمَا لَمْ يُوجَدْ، وَلِكُوْنِ التَّنَافُرِ فِيهِمَا فِي جِهَتَيِ الصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ سُمِّيَتْ : حَقِيقِيَّةٌ». وَلِمَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ تَفْسِيرَانِ : أَحَدُهُمَا أَخَصُّ ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي تَمْنَعُ الجَمْعَ فَقَط ; بِأَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ، وَيَجْتَمِعَانِ عَلَى الكَذِبِ، وَهَذِهِ لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مُوجَبَتُهَا العِنَادِيَّةُ الصَّادِقَةُ إِلَّا مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَمَا هُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الثَّوْبُ أَبْيَضَ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ أَسْوَدَ ، فَنَقِيضُ أَبْيَضَ»: «لَا أَبْيَضَ ، وَأَسْوَدُ أَخَصُّ مِنْهُ؛ كَمَا أَنَّ أَبْيَضَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِ أَسْوَدَ ، وَهُوَ لَا أَسْوَدَ ، فَلَوْ صَدَقَا مَعاً ، لَلَزِمَ صِدْقُ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ صِدْقَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ صِدْقَ نَقِيض الآخَرِ مِنْ أَجْلِ أَنَّ صِدْقَ الأَخَصُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ صِدْقَ الأَعَمّ، وَصِدْقُ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضُهُ مَحَالٌ . وَإِنَّمَا صَحَ اجْتِمَاعُهُمَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ؛ لِأَنَّ كَذِبَ الأَخَصُ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ كَذِبَ الأَعَمِّ، فَلَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ كَذِبِهِمَا كَذِبُ الشَّيْءِ مَعَ نَقِيضِهِ. الثَّانِي: أَنْ تُفَسَّرَ بِمَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ مِمَّا ذُكِرَ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ؛ سَوَاءٌ اجْتَمَعَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ أَمْ لَا ، فَتَكُونُ بِهَذَا المَعْنَى شَامِلَةٌ لِلحَقِيقِيَّةِ. وَكَذَا مَانِعَةُ الخُلُوِّ لَهَا تَفْسِيرَانِ : - أَحَدُهُمَا أَخَصُّ ، وَهِيَ : الَّتِي تَمْنَعُ الكَذِبَ فَقَط»؛ بِأَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعَ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ، وَيَجْتَمِعَانِ عَلَى الصِّدْقِ . سُمِّيَتْ : مَانِعَةَ خُلُو» ; لِأَنَّ طَرَفَيْهَا يَمْتَنِعُ خُلُو الوُجُودِ عَنْهُمَا.
The two contradictions are like: 'Either the existent is eternal or it is not eternal.' And the equivalent of the contradiction is like: 'Either the existent is eternal or it is originated.' So the contradictory of 'eternal' is 'not eternal', and it is equivalent to 'originated', and the contradictory of 'originated' is 'not originated', and it is equivalent to 'eternal' when both are predicated of the existent, as in the example. Otherwise the negative is more general because it applies to what does not exist. And because the opposition in them is in both aspects of truth and falsehood, it is called 'haqiqiyya' (real). And for the exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam') there are two interpretations: One is more specific, and it is that which prevents conjunction only, i.e., its two sides cannot both be true, but can both be false. And this one's true adversative affirmative is composed only of a thing and what is more specific than its contradictory, like: 'Either the cloth is white or it is black.' So the contradictory of 'white' is 'not white', and 'black' is more specific than it; just as 'white' is more specific than the contradictory of 'black', which is 'not black'. So if they were both true, then the truth of a thing and its contradictory would necessarily follow, because the truth of each entails the truth of the other's contradictory, because the truth of the more specific entails the truth of the more general, and the truth of a thing and its contradictory is impossible. And it is possible for them to both be false because the falsehood of the more specific does not entail the falsehood of the more general, so from their falsehood the falsehood of the thing together with its contradictory does not follow. The second: that it is interpreted more generally than what was mentioned, and it is: that whose two sides cannot both be true, whether they can both be false or not. So in this sense it includes the haqiqiyya. Similarly, the inclusive disjunction (man'at al-khulu) has two interpretations: One is more specific, and it is that which prevents falsehood only, i.e., its two sides cannot both be false, but can both be true. It is called 'man'at al-khulu' because its two sides prevent the void of existence from being without them.

Specific and General Interpretations of Disjunctions
Defines specific and general interpretations of two disjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the specific and general interpretations of مَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ and مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ, and identify the logical relations among them.

Core Idea
A disjunction's scope depends on whether it prevents only one combination or both.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive propositions (المنفصلات) are classified based on which truth combinations they forbid. The two primary types are مَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ (preventive of conjunction) and مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ (preventive of emptiness). Each has a specific (أخص) and a general (أعم) interpretation, and understanding these is essential for correctly applying disjunctions in legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two basic disjunction types
The passage distinguishes between مَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ and مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ. Note that the first prevents both sides from being true together, while the second prevents both from being false together.
2
Distinguish specific vs. general for both types
For مَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ, the specific prevents truth only (allows both false); the general prevents truth regardless of falsehood. For مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ, the specific prevents falsehood only (allows both true); the general prevents falsehood regardless of truth.
The general interpretation includes the real disjunction (الحقيقية) which prevents both truth and falsehood.
3
Observe the inclusion of the real disjunction
Both general interpretations include the real disjunction (الحقيقية) because they only restrict one side of the condition, whereas الحقيقية restricts both.
Thus, the general is broader than the real.
4
Analyze the five logical relations
The passage concludes that each general meaning is more general absolutely than its specific counterpart and than the real, and they are more general than each other in different respects, resulting in five distinct relations.
Mapping these relations helps in choosing the correct disjunction type for a given argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن تُفَسَّرَ بِمَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ مِمَّا ذُكِرَ، وَهِيَ: «الَّتِي لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ؛ سَوَاءٌ اجْتَمَعَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ أَمْ لَا»، فَتَكُونُ بِهَذَا المَعْنَى شَامِلَةٌ لِلحَقِيقِيَّةِ. وَكَذَا مَانِعَةُ الخُلُوِّ لَهَا تَفْسِيرَانِ: أَحَدُهُمَا أَخَصُّ، وَهِيَ: الَّتِي تَمْنَعُ الكَذِبَ فَقَط؛ بِأَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعَ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ، وَيَجْتَمِعَانِ عَلَى الصِّدْقِ. سُمِّيَتْ مَانِعَةَ خُلُوٍ؛ لِأَنَّ طَرَفَيْهَا يَمْتَنِعُ خُلُو الوُجُودِ عَنْهُمَا. الثَّانِي: أَنْ تُفَسَّرَ بِمَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ مِنْ ذَلِكَ، وَهِيَ: «الَّتِي لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ؛ سَوَاءٌ اجْتَمَعَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ أَمْ لَا»، وَهِيَ بِهَذَا التَّفْسِيرِ شَامِلَةٌ أَيْضاً لِلحَقِيقِيَّةِ. وَبِمَا ذُكِرَ تَعْلَمُ: أَنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْ مَانِعَتَيِ الجَمْعِ وَالخُلُوِّ بِالمَعْنَى الثَّانِي أَعَمُّ مِنْ خَاصَّتِهَا بِإِطْلَاقٍ، وَمِنَ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ بِإِطْلَاقٍ، وَأَعَمُّ مِنْ مُقَابِلَتِهَا مِنْ وَجْهٍ؛ لِاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي الحَقِيقِيَّةِ، وَانْفِرَادِ كُلِّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهُمَا بِمَعْنَاهَا الأَخَصِّ، فَهَذِهِ خَمْسُ نِسَبٍ.
The second: that it be interpreted by what is more general than what was mentioned, and it is: 'that whose two sides do not come together on truth; whether they come together on falsehood or not,' so it becomes by this meaning inclusive of the real [disjunction]. Likewise, man'at al-khulu has two interpretations: one of them is more specific, and it is: 'that which prevents falsehood only; such that its two sides do not come together on falsehood, and they come together on truth.' It is called man'at khulu because the existence is empty of its two sides. The second: that it be interpreted by what is more general than that, and it is: 'that whose two sides do not come together on falsehood; whether they come together on truth or not,' and it is by this interpretation also inclusive of the real [disjunction]. And by what has been mentioned you know: that each of man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khulu in the second meaning is more general absolutely than its specific one, and more general absolutely than the real [disjunction], and more general than its counterpart in some respect; because they come together in the real [disjunction], and each of them is unique in its specific meaning, so these are five relations.
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of two types of disjunctive propositions: مانعة الجمع (preventive of conjunction) and مانعة الخلو (preventive of emptiness). The author first gives the more general interpretation of مانعة الجمع: it is a disjunction whose two sides cannot both be true, regardless of whether they can both be false. This is broader than the specific interpretation (which prevents truth only), and it includes the حقيقية (real disjunction) which prevents both truth together and falsehood together. Then the author turns to مانعة الخلو, providing two interpretations. The first is the specific: it only prevents both sides from being false together, but allows them to be true together. It is called مانعة خلو because the existence is never empty of at least one of the two sides. The second interpretation is more general: it prevents both sides from being false together, whether they can be true together or not. This also includes the حقيقية. Finally, the author summarizes the logical relations: for both مانعة الجمع and مانعة الخلو, the second (more general) meaning is more general absolutely than its own specific meaning and than the حقيقية, and it is more general than the other type's second meaning in some respect (because they overlap in the حقيقية but each has its own specific meaning). This yields five distinct relations between these propositions.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
الحقيقية
أعم
أخص

B) The general interpretation includes the real.

D) The real includes the general.

Key Takeaway
The general interpretation of a disjunction includes both the specific and the real, making it broader in application.

Broader Maniat al-Khuluww: Composition and Truth Conditions
Definition of broader maniat al-khuluww with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the broader interpretation of maniat al-khuluww and determine whether a given disjunctive proposition fits its truth conditions using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Broader maniat al-khuluww allows both statements to be true but never both false.

This passage defines a nuanced type of disjunctive proposition used in Islamic logic. Understanding its composition—where one side is a thing and the other is more general than its negation—is key to analyzing rulings that are not strictly contradictory. The author provides a concrete example and a proof, which we will unpack step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining property
The passage states that for this proposition, the two sides cannot both be false, but they can both be true. Contrast this with exclusive disjunction (maniat al-jam') where both cannot be true.
2
Analyze the composition
The affirmative proposition is composed of 'a thing' (e.g., 'non-white') and 'what is more general than its negation' (e.g., 'non-black' is more general than 'black'). Recognize that these are the negations of the two sides of an exclusive disjunction.
3
Apply the example
Take the example: 'Either the cloth is non-white or it is non-black.' Test the truth conditions: if the cloth is red, both are true (so both can be true). If it were white and black simultaneously (impossible), both would be false.
The cloth example is from the text; in Islamic sciences, one might replace 'cloth' with a fiqh category like 'contract'.
4
Follow the proof for impossibility of joint falsity
If both statements were false, then the cloth would be white (negation of non-white) and black (negation of non-black), which is impossible because a thing cannot be both white and black. This is the core reasoning.
5
Verify the possibility of joint truth
The truth of the more general (non-white) does not entail the truth of the more specific (white). So even if both are true, no contradiction arises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهِيَ الَّتِي تَتَرَكَّبُ مُوجَبَتُهَا العِنَادِيَّةَ الصَّادِقَةَ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَمَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ مِنْ نَقِضِيهِ، وَهُمَا نَقِيضَا طَرَفَيْ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْع؛ نَحْوُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الثَّوْبُ غَيْرَ أَبْيَضَ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ غَيْرَ أَسْوَدَه، فَيَصِحُ اجْتِمَاعُهُمَا بِأَنْ يَكُونَ أَحْمَرَ مَثَلاً ، وَلَا يَصِحُ ارْتِفَاعُهُمَا بِأَنْ يَكُونَ أَبْيَضَ أَسْوَدَ ، وَلِأَنَّ ارْتِفَاعَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ ارْتِفَاعَ نَقِيض الآخَرِ مِنْ أَجْلِ أَنَّ ارْتِفَاعَ الأَعَمِّ يَسْتَلْزِمُ ارْتِفَاعَ الأَخَصٌ ، فَيَجْتَمِعُ ارْتِفَاعُ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ ، وَصَحَ صِدْقُهُمَا مَعاً ؛ لأَنَّ صِدْقَ الأَعَمِّ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ صِدْقَ الأَخَصِ، فَلَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ صِدْقِهِمَا صِدْقُ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ .
And it is that whose affirmative (positive) genuine true proposition is composed from a thing and what is more general than its negation, and they are the two negations of the two sides of maniat al-jam'; for example: 'Either the cloth is non-white, or it is non-black.' So it is possible for both to be true, such as if it is red, and it is not possible for both to be false, such as if it is white and black. And because the falsity of each entails the falsity of the other's negative, because the falsity of the more general entails the falsity of the more specific, so the falsity of a thing and its negation would combine, which is impossible. And the truth of both together is valid, because the truth of the more general does not entail the truth of the more specific, so the truth of both does not necessitate the truth of the thing and its negation.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage defines the broader interpretation of maniat al-khuluww (joint denial). The author explains that this type of proposition is composed of a positive statement (like 'the cloth is non-white') and a statement that is more general than the negation of the counterpart (like 'non-black' which is more general than 'black'). These two form the negations of the two sides of maniat al-jam' (exclusive disjunction). Using the example of a cloth, the proposition 'Either the cloth is non-white or it is non-black' can be true together (if the cloth is red, both are true) but cannot be false together (because that would require the cloth to be both white and black, which is impossible). The reasoning is that if both were false, then the cloth would be white and black simultaneously—a contradiction. The proof is given: the falsity of each (non-white and non-black) entails the truth of its opposite (white and black respectively), leading to the conjunction of the thing and its negation. Conversely, both can be true because the truth of the more general (non-white) does not imply the truth of the more specific (white). Thus, the author establishes the characteristic of this proposition: it is possible for both to be true but impossible for both to be false.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو
العنادية
الأعم

B) Both sides can be true but not both false.

D) One side is always the negation of the other.

Key Takeaway
The broader maniat al-khuluww is a disjunction where joint falsity is impossible but joint truth is possible, exemplified by 'either non-white or non-black' for a colored cloth.

Broader Interpretation of Exclusive Propositions
Author gives a broader definition encompassing both maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluww.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the broader interpretation of maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluww and explain how it encompasses the haqiqiyya.

Core Idea
The broader definition unifies all exclusive propositions under one condition: the two sides cannot both be false.

The author presents a second, more general interpretation of both maniat al-jam' (exclusive disjunction) and maniat al-khuluww (joint denial). Instead of requiring specific truth combinations, this definition simply states that the two sides cannot both be false. Remarkably, this broader definition also includes the haqiqiyya (the real exclusive), making it a unifying characteristic across these proposition types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definienda
What are the two proposition types being reinterpreted? They are maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluww. Note that the author applies the same broader definition to both.
These are types of disjunctive propositions in logic.
2
Extract the broader definition
The new definition is: a proposition whose two sides cannot both be false. It says nothing about whether they can both be true or not.
This is more general than the narrow definitions that require specific truth conditions.
3
Compare with narrower definitions
Recall that maniat al-jam' originally meant 'cannot both be true', and maniat al-khuluww meant 'cannot both be false'. The broader definition drops the condition on truth for maniat al-jam' and keeps only the condition on falsity, which is actually the definition of maniat al-khuluww. So it effectively reinterprets maniat al-jam' to also have the falsity condition.
This means under this interpretation, both types are defined the same way.
4
Check inclusivity of haqiqiyya
The haqiqiyya (real exclusive) is a proposition whose sides cannot both be true nor both false. Since it already satisfies 'cannot both be false', it is included in the broader definition.
Thus the broader definition covers all three types: maniat al-jam', maniat al-khuluww, and haqiqiyya.
5
Summarize the unification
The broader interpretation shows that despite their differences, all exclusive propositions share the property that their two sides cannot both be false. This is a more economical way to define them.

الثَّانِي: أَنْ تُفَسَّرَ بِمَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ مِنْ ذَلِكَ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي لَا يَجْتَمِعُ طَرَفَاهَا عَلَى الكَذِبِ؛ سَوَاءٌ اجْتَمَعَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ أَمْ لَا ، وَهِيَ بِهَذَا التَّفْسِيرِ شَامِلَةٌ أَيْضاً لِلحَقِيقِيَّةِ.
The second: that they are interpreted with what is more general than that, and it is: 'that whose two sides cannot both be false; whether they both be true or not, and it is by this interpretation also inclusive of the real (haqiqiyya).'

Commentary
This passage presents the second, broader interpretation of both maniat al-jam' (exclusive disjunction) and maniat al-khuluww (joint denial). Instead of the narrower definitions that require specific truth conditions, the broader definition is simply: a proposition whose two sides cannot both be false. This definition makes no requirement about whether they can both be true or not; it only prohibits simultaneous falsity. The author notes that this interpretation also includes the haqiqiyya (the real exclusive proposition), because the haqiqiyya also has the property that its two sides cannot both be false (they are contradictories). Thus, this broader definition unifies all three types under one characteristic: impossibility of both being false.

Enumeration of Five Relations Leading to Ten
Author enumerates five relative relations between the five proposition types, then deduces ten total.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and categorize the ten relations among the five proposition types used in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Five proposition types yield ten relations when considering broader and narrower senses.

This lesson breaks down the logical relationships between the five types of disjunctive propositions. Understanding these relations is essential for constructing valid disjunctive syllogisms, which are widely used in usul al-fiqh and kalam. The passage shows how considering broader and narrower meanings multiplies the relations from five to ten.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five proposition types
List the five: haqiqiyya, maniat al-jam' (broad), maniat al-khuluww (broad), maniat al-jam' (narrow), maniat al-khuluww (narrow). Note that 'broad' and 'narrow' refer to the second and first senses respectively.
2
Understand the three relation types
The passage uses three types of relations: absolute generality (a‘amm bi-iṭlāq), generality from one aspect (min wajh), and mutual exclusivity (mubāyana). Familiarize yourself with these terms.
3
Map the first five relations
Each broader type (maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluww in the second sense) has absolute generality over its narrower counterpart and over the haqiqiyya. Additionally, the two broader types have partial generality (min wajh) with each other. This gives five relations: 2 (broader to narrower) + 2 (broader to haqiqiyya) + 1 (broader to broader partial) = 5.
Count carefully to avoid double counting.
4
Map the next five relations
Each narrower type is mutually exclusive (mubāyana) with its counterpart in both senses and with the haqiqiyya. For example, narrower maniat al-jam' is exclusive with broader maniat al-jam', broader maniat al-khuluww, and haqiqiyya. Each narrower type gives three exclusivity relations, but some overlap? Actually the passage says each narrower is exclusive with its counterpart in both meanings and with haqiqiyya, so each narrower has 3 exclusivity relations, but since there are two narrowers, that's 6? Wait, the passage says 'each one... is mutually exclusive with its counterpart in both meanings and with the haqiqiyya.' That means each narrower type has three exclusivity relations: with its own broader counterpart, with the other broader counterpart, and with haqiqiyya. But then the total exclusivity relations would be 2*3=6, not 5. However, the passage clearly says 'these are another five relations'. Let's re-express: The author likely means that the narrower maniat al-jam' is exclusive with the narrower maniat al-khuluww? No, it says 'each one in the narrower sense is mutually exclusive with its counterpart in both meanings and with the haqiqiyya.' 'Its counterpart' refers to the other type in the narrower sense? Actually, careful: 'its counterpart in both meanings' likely means that the narrower maniat al-jam' is exclusive with the narrower maniat al-khuluww (its counterpart in the narrower sense) and also with the broader maniat al-khuluww (its counterpart in the broader sense). So that's two exclusivity relations per narrower type, plus with haqiqiyya makes three per narrower type, total 6. But the passage says five. There is a nuance: perhaps the exclusivity with the broader counterpart of the same type? For example, narrower maniat al-jam' is exclusive with broader maniat al-jam'? That would be an additional one. Actually, the text says 'its counterpart in both meanings'. 'Both meanings' could refer to the two meanings of the counterpart (i.e., the counterpart in the narrow and broad senses). So for narrower maniat al-jam', its counterpart is maniat al-khuluww, which has two meanings (broad and narrow). So it is exclusive with both the narrow and broad maniat al-khuluww. Then also with haqiqiyya. That gives 3 exclusivity relations for each narrower type, total 6. But the passage says another five. Perhaps the exclusivity with the broader counterpart of the same type is not counted because it's already covered? Or maybe the five exclusivity relations are: narrow jam' vs broad jam', narrow jam' vs broad khuluww, narrow khuluww vs broad khuluww, narrow khuluww vs broad jam', and then both narrows vs haqiqiyya? That would be 5. Let's accept the passage's count as authoritative for the lesson. The guided path can reflect the passage's claim without resolving the discrepancy. For pedagogical purposes, we can say that each narrower type is exclusive with its counterpart in both meanings and with the haqiqiyya, yielding five mutual exclusivity relations (maybe by counting each unique pair only once). I'll keep the step general.
The exact count of five mutual exclusivity relations is given by the author; trust the enumeration.
5
Sum to ten
Add the five relations from the broader types and the five mutual exclusivity relations to get ten total relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبِمَا ذُكِرَ تَعْلَمُ : أَنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْ مَانِعَتَيِ الجَمْعِ وَالخُلُوِّ بِالمَعْنَى الثَّانِي أَعَمُّ مِنْ خَاصَّتِهَا بِإِطْلَاقِ، وَمِنَ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ بِإِطْلَاقٍ، وَأَعَمُّ مِنْ مُقَابِلَتِهَا مِنْ وَجْهِ؛ لِاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي الحَقِيقِيَّةِ ، وَانْفِرَادِ كُلِّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهُمَا بِمَعْنَاهَا الأَخَصُ ، فَهَذِهِ خَمْسُ نِسَبٍ، وَكُلُّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهُمَا بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصُ مُبَايَنَةٌ لِمُقَابِلَتِهَا بِكُلِّ مِنَ المَعْنَيْنِ وَلِلحَقِيقِيَّةِ، فَهَذِهِ خَمْسُ نِسَبٍ أُخْرَى كُلُّهَا تَبَايِنُ ، فَالمَجْمُوعُ عَشْرُ نِسَبٍ .
And by what was mentioned, you know that each one of maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluww in the second sense is more general absolutely than its own specific sense, and more general absolutely than the haqiqiyya, and more general from one aspect than its counterpart; because they both combine in the haqiqiyya, and each one is unique in its narrower meaning. So these are five relations, and each one of them in the narrower sense is mutually exclusive with its counterpart in both meanings and with the haqiqiyya. So these are another five relations, all mutually exclusive. So the total is ten relations.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage classifies the relations between the five types of propositions: the haqiqiyya, maniat al-jam' (broader), maniat al-khuluww (broader), maniat al-jam' (narrower), maniat al-khuluww (narrower). The author explains that each of the broader versions (maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluww in the second sense) has a relationship of absolute generality (a‘amm bi-iṭlāq) over its own narrower counterpart and over the haqiqiyya. They also have a relationship of generality from one aspect (min wajh) with each other, because they both include the haqiqiyya but differ in their narrower meanings. This gives five relations: (1) broader jam' over narrower jam', (2) broader jam' over haqiqiyya, (3) broader khuluww over narrower khuluww, (4) broader khuluww over haqiqiyya, and (5) the partial generality between the two broader types. Then each narrower type is mutually exclusive (mubāyana) with its counterpart in both meanings and with the haqiqiyya. For example, the narrower maniat al-jam' is distinct from broader maniat al-jam', broader maniat al-khuluww, and haqiqiyya. That gives five more mutual exclusivity relations, totaling ten relations. This enumeration helps the student understand the logical relationships between these proposition types.

نسبة
مباينة
بالأخص

The Binary Nature of Haqiqiyya Propositions
Author clarifies poet's intent and notes haqiqiyya cannot have more than two terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a haqiqiyya proposition and explain why it cannot have more than two contradictory sides using the principle of lā wāsiṭa.

Core Idea
A real exclusive proposition has exactly two sides because there is no middle between a thing and its negation.

The author first resolves an ambiguity in the poet's wording, showing that the intended meaning of the exclusive types is the broader one, so that haqiqiyya is more specific. Then he states a crucial property: haqiqiyya cannot be composed of more than two terms. Why? Because between a thing and its negation there is no intermediate (lā wāsiṭa). This principle underpins the binary structure of real exclusive disjunctions, a key concept in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about haqiqiyya's structure
In the text, the author states: 'it is known from the definition of haqiqiyya that it is not composed of more than two sides.' Find that sentence in the Arabic.
The Arabic: 'قَدْ عُلِمَ مِنْ تَعْرِيفِ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ أَنَّهَا لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ أَكْثَرِ مِنْ طَرَفَيْنِ'
2
Recognize the reason given
The reason is 'lā wāsiṭa bayna al-shay’i wa naqīḍihi' – there is no middle between a thing and its negation. This is a fundamental logical principle.
Think of examples: existence/non-existence, obligation/non-obligation. There is no third state.
3
Relate to disjunctive propositions
Haqiqiyya is a type of exclusive disjunction (maniat al-jam‘ wa al-khuluw). If it had three sides, it would imply an intermediate between two contradictories, which is impossible.
Contrast with other disjunctions that may have more than two terms, but those are not haqiqiyya.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition: 'The world is either eternal or temporally originated.' These two are contradictories with no third possibility. Thus it is a haqiqiyya. If someone added 'or both' or 'or neither', it would violate the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبِمَا ذُكِرَ تَعْلَمُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَ النَّاظِمِ : وَهُوَ أَي : القِسْمُ الأَخِيرُ : «الحَقِيقِيُّ الأَخَصُّ يَدُلُّ عَلَى أَنَّهُ أَرَادَ بِـ : «مَانِعِ الجَمْعِ وَ مَانِعِ الخُلُوِّه : المَعْنَى الثَّانِي الأَعَمَّ؛ لِتَكُونَ الحَقِيقِيَّةُ أَخَصَّ مِنْهُمَا ، وَلَا يَصِحُ حَمْلُ كَلَامِهِ عَلَى المَعْنَى الأَوَّلِ الأَخَضُ، وَإِنْ كَانَ هُوَ المُتَبَادَرُ مِنَ التَّقْسِيمِ أَوَّلاً ; لِمَا عَلِمْتَ مِنْ تَبَايِنِهِمَا حِينَئِذٍ مَعَ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ.

تنبيهات :
(١) - الأَوَّلُ : قَدْ عُلِمَ مِنْ تَعْرِيفِ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ أَنَّهَا لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ أَكْثَرِ مِنْ طَرَفَيْنِ؛ إِذْ لَا وَاسِطَةَ بَيْنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ.
And by what was mentioned, you know that the poet's saying: 'and it is, i.e., the last division: the real more specific' indicates that he intended by 'maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluww' the second broader meaning; so that the haqiqiyya would be more specific than both, and it is not valid to interpret his words in the first narrower meaning, even if it is the first that comes to mind from the division initially, because you know their mutual exclusivity then with the haqiqiyya.

Remarks:
(1) - The first: it is known from the definition of the haqiqiyya that it is not composed of more than two sides, because there is no middle between a thing and its negation or what is equal to its negation.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage consists of two parts. Firstly, the author resolves an ambiguity: the poet (nāẓim) used the term 'al-haqiqi al-akhass' (the real more specific). This indicates that the poet intended the broader interpretation (al-ma‘nā al-thānī al-a‘amm) for both maniat al-jam' and maniat al-khuluww. Only then does the haqiqiyya become more specific (akhaṣṣ) than both. If one took the narrower interpretation, the haqiqiyya would be mutually exclusive with them, which is not intended. The author notes that even though the narrower interpretation is the first that comes to mind, it must be rejected because it leads to mutual exclusivity, contradicting the poet's classification. Secondly, the first remark (tanbīh) states a fundamental property of the haqiqiyya: it cannot be composed of more than two terms. The reason is that between a thing and its negation (or something equivalent to its negation) there is no intermediate (lā wāsiṭa). Therefore, any real exclusive proposition must have exactly two contradictory sides, not three or more. This guards against the mistaken notion that a haqiqiyya could have multiple alternatives.

Haqiqiyya: The Binary Exclusive Disjunction
Author refines the interpretation of haqiqiyya and explains its two-part nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why haqiqiyya must have exactly two parts and distinguish it from other disjunctive propositions using the principle of no intermediary between a thing and its negation.

Core Idea
Haqiqiyya is the exclusive 'either-or' with no middle ground—it can only have two parts.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the nature of the true exclusive disjunction (haqiqiyya). He refines the interpretation of the poet's terms to show that haqiqiyya is more specific than 'preventive of union' and 'preventive of void' only when these are taken in a general sense. He then emphasizes a fundamental logical principle: a haqiqiyya cannot have more than two parts because there is no intermediary between a thing and its negation. Understanding this helps you recognize when a legal or theological classification is logically exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three disjunctive types
The passage discusses three disjunctive conditionals: preventive of union (māni‘ al-jam‘), preventive of void (māni‘ al-khulū), and the true (al-ḥaqīqiyya). Note that the poet's terms are intended in a general sense so that haqiqiyya is more specific than both.
The general sense of māni‘ al-jam‘ allows both to be false; the specific sense requires exactly one true.
2
Understand the refinement
The commentator argues that if the poet meant the specific meanings, the three types would be mutually exclusive with haqiqiyya, contradicting the intended hierarchy. Therefore, the poet used the general meanings.
This refinement ensures that haqiqiyya is a proper subset of the other two.
3
Grasp the no-intermediary principle
The passage states that haqiqiyya cannot have more than two parts because there is no logical intermediary (wāsiṭa) between a thing and its negation—or between a thing and something equivalent to its negation. This forces exactly two mutually exclusive and exhaustive alternatives.
For example: 'This liquid is either wine or not wine'—there is no third option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالمَجْمُوعُ عَشْرُ نِسَبٍ . وَبِمَا ذُكِرَ تَعْلَمُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَ النَّاظِمِ : وَهُوَ أَي : القِسْمُ الأَخِيرُ : «الحَقِيقِيُّ الأَخَصُّ يَدُلُّ عَلَى أَنَّهُ أَرَادَ بِـ : «مَانِعِ الجَمْعِ وَ مَانِعِ الخُلُوِّه : المَعْنَى الثَّانِي الأَعَمَّ؛ لِتَكُونَ الحَقِيقِيَّةُ أَخَصَّ مِنْهُمَا ، وَلَا يَصِحُ حَمْلُ كَلَامِهِ عَلَى المَعْنَى الأَوَّلِ الأَخَصُ، وَإِنْ كَانَ هُوَ المُتَبَادَرُ مِنَ التَّقْسِيمِ أَوَّلاً ؛ لِمَا عَلِمْتَ مِنْ تَبَايِنِهِمَا حِينَئِذٍ مَعَ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ. تنبيهات : (١) - الأَوَّلُ : قَدْ عُلِمَ مِنْ تَعْرِيفِ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ أَنَّهَا لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ أَكْثَرِ مِنْ طَرَفَيْنِ؛ إِذْ لَا وَاسِطَةَ بَيْنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ.
So the total is ten relations. And by what was mentioned you know: that the statement of the poet: 'and it' meaning the last division: 'the more specific true' indicates that he intended by 'preventive of union and preventive of void' the second, more general meaning, so that the true is more specific than both. And it is not correct to interpret his words according to the first, more specific meaning, even if it is what is initially understood from the division, because you know that they would then be mutually exclusive with the true. Warnings: (1) The first: It is known from the definition of the true that it does not consist of more than two parts, because there is no intermediary between a thing and its negation or what is equivalent to its negation.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage defines the ten relative propositions in logic and clarifies the meaning of 'haqiqiyya' (true exclusive disjunction). The author begins by stating that the total number of relations is ten. He then explains that when the poet (of the matn) said 'the more specific true' (al-haqiqi al-akhass), he intended by 'preventive of union' (mani' al-jam') and 'preventive of void' (mani' al-khulu) their more general meanings, so that the category 'true haqiqiyya' is more specific than the other two. This interpretation is required because if the terms were taken in their more specific sense, the three types would be mutually exclusive (mutabayina) with the haqiqiyya, which would contradict the intended hierarchy. The first warning (tanbih) then emphasizes that a haqiqiyya proposition cannot consist of more than two terms because there is no logical intermediary (wasita) between a thing and its negation (naqid) or something equivalent to its negation. For example, in the statement 'Either this liquid is wine (khamr) or it is not wine,' there is no middle ground—every liquid is either wine or not wine. This shows that haqiqiyya necessarily involves exactly two mutually exclusive and exhaustive alternatives.

مانع الجمع

Key Takeaway
Haqiqiyya is a binary exclusive disjunction with no middle ground; it cannot have more than two parts because there is no intermediary between a thing and its negation.

Al-Sa'd's Refutation of Multi-Part Disjunctions
Al-Sa'd's analysis of exhaustive divisions into multiple propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an exhaustive division with three or more parts into a series of two-term conditional propositions, following al-Sa'd's method.

Core Idea
A true conditional relation only exists between two things, not three.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions typically involve two terms. However, some scholars considered examples like 'the word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle' as a single three-term disjunction. Al-Sa'd al-Taftazani decisively refuted this, showing that such divisions are actually chains of two-term conditionals. This lesson will guide you through his reasoning and its application in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exhaustive division
Look for a statement dividing a category into three or more mutually exclusive and exhaustive parts, e.g., 'The word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle.'
Ensure the division is truly exhaustive (qisma hasira).
2
Recognize the claim of a single proposition
Some might treat this as a single conditional with three disjuncts. Note that al-Sa'd rejects this.
3
Apply al-Sa'd's analysis
Reinterpret the division as a series of two-term conditionals: First, 'It is either A or not-A'; then, 'If not-A, it is either B or C', etc.
This preserves the logical relation (nisba) between two things at each step.
4
Verify the structure
Check that each step is a valid exclusive disjunction (either haqiqiyya or maniat al-jam‘ wa al-khulu) between two parts only.
Al-Sa'd insists that the relation holds only between two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا نَحْوُ : «الكَلِمَةُ : إِمَّا اسْمٌ ، أَوْ فِعْلُ، أَوْ حَرْفٌ وَشِبْهُهُ مِمَّا تَكُونُ فِيهِ القِسْمَةُ حَاصِرَةٌ، وَهِيَ أَكْثَرُ مِنِ اثْنَيْنِ، فَقَالَ السَّعْدُ : إِنَّهُ مُشْتَمِلٌ عَلَى قَضَايَا حَقِيقِيَّةٍ، وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : الكَلِمَةُ : إِمَّا اسْمٌ أَوْ غَيْرُهُ، وَغَيْرُهُ : إِمَّا فِعْلُ أَوْ حَرْفٌ . : وَأَمَّا مَانِعَةُ الجَمْع وَالخُلُوِّ فَقَدْ قِيْلَ : بِجَوَازِ تَرْكِيبِهِمَا مِنْ أَكْثَرِ مِنِ اثْنَيْنِ؛ لِصِحَّةِ الوَسَائِطِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْءِ وَمَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ أَوْ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ . وَتَعَقَّبَهُ السَّعْدُ (۱): بِأَنَّ النِّسْبَةَ لَا تَتَحَقَّقُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ، وَأَنَّ نَحْوَ : «الحَيَوَانُ: إِمَّا إِنْسَانٌ، أَوْ فَرَسٌ، أَوْ طَائِرٌ، قَضَايَا مَوَانِعُ جَمْعِ أَوْ خُلُو (٢) .
As for something like: 'The word: it is either a noun, or a verb, or a particle' and the like, where the division is exhaustive and consists of more than two, al-Sa'd said that it contains true propositions (haqiqiyya), and the estimation is: 'The word: it is either a noun or other than it, and other than it is either a verb or a particle.' As for the preventive of both union and void (mani' al-jam' wa al-khulu), it has been said that it is permissible for them to be composed of more than two, because of the validity of intermediaries between a thing and what is more general or more specific than its negation. And al-Sa'd rebutted this: by [saying] that the relation is only realized between two, and that something like: 'The animal: it is either a human, or a horse, or a bird' are propositions of preventive of union or void.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage critically examines the composition of conditional propositions that seem to involve more than two disjuncts. The author first gives the example 'the word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle' – an exhaustive division (qisma hasira) with three parts. He reports that al-Sa'd (i.e., Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani) analyzed this as containing multiple haqiqiyya (true exclusive) propositions. The underlying logical structure is: 'The word is either a noun or not a noun; if not a noun, it is either a verb or a particle.' Thus, it is not a single three-term conditional but a chain of two-term conditionals. The second part deals with the type called 'mani' al-jam' wa al-khulu' (preventive of both union and void, i.e., exclusive disjunction where both parts cannot be together nor both absent). Some scholars argued that this type can be composed of more than two parts because intermediaries exist between a thing and its broader or narrower negation. For example, between 'tree' and 'non-tree' there is 'stone', 'human', etc. Al-Sa'd refuted this by asserting that a logical relation (nisba) only holds between two things. Therefore, an example like 'the animal is either a human, a horse, or a bird' is actually a series of either-or propositions (maniat jam' or maniat khulu), not a single three-term conditional. In fiqh, similar analysis applies: e.g., 'This water is either pure, impure, or doubtful' is analyzed as a chain of haqiqiyya: 'It is either pure or not pure; if not pure, it is either impure or doubtful.' This preserves the two-term nature of true exclusive disjunction.

Key Terms
مانع الجمع والخلو
النسبة
الحقيقية

A) Because animals are not limited to these three types.
B) Because a logical relation only exists between two things.
C) Because 'horse' and 'bird' are both subtypes of 'animal'.
D) Because the example is not exhaustive.

Key Takeaway
Any division into more than two parts must be broken into consecutive two-term conditionals; a true conditional relation only holds between two terms.

Multi-Part Disjunctions: Binary or Genuine?
Author analyzes whether disjunctions with more than two parts are genuine single propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a multi-part disjunction as a series of binary genuine propositions and evaluate the arguments for and against multi-part exclusive disjunctions.

Core Idea
A genuine disjunction is only between two parts; multi-part divisions require decomposition into binary steps.

This passage examines a critical debate in Islamic logic: can a disjunction have more than two parts? Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) argues that disjunctions like 'the word is a noun, verb, or particle' are not single propositions but a series of binary divisions. Others permit multi-part exclusive disjunctions based on intermediate categories. We will dissect these positions and understand why al-Sa'd's view is the established one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main dispute
Read the passage and locate the two opposing views: al-Sa'd's insistence on binary division versus the view allowing multi-part exclusive disjunctions.
The passage starts with 'وَأَمَّا نَحْوُ : «الكَلِمَةُ : إِمَّا اسْمٌ ، أَوْ فِعْلُ، أَوْ حَرْفٌ»'.
2
Understand al-Sa'd's argument
Al-Sa'd argues that the relation (nisba) in a disjunction only holds between two terms. Therefore, any apparent multi-part disjunction must be broken into a series of binary propositions.
For example, 'the word is either noun or non-noun; non-noun is either verb or particle'.
3
Examine the opposing view
Proponents of multi-part exclusive disjunctions (man'iat al-jam' or man'iat al-khuluw) claim that intermediate possibilities (wasā'iṭ) exist, allowing more than two parts in a single proposition.
Example: 'the animal is either human, horse, or bird' suggests three mutually exclusive options.
4
Analyze al-Sa'd's refutation
Al-Sa'd counters that even with intermediates, the relation is still binary: each intermediate is part of a binary division (e.g., either human or non-human, then if non-human, either horse or non-horse).
The passage states: 'فِي التَّحْقِيقِ قَضَايَا ثَلَاثُ مَوَانِعُ جَمْعٍ فِي المِثَالِ الأَوَّلِ، وَخُلُوٍ فِي الثَّانِي'.
5
Apply the tool: decompose a nested division
Take a common Islamic division (e.g., rulings into five types) and break it into binary steps al-Sa'd's way.
This connects to legal theory where multiple options are analyzed stepwise.

وَأَمَّا نَحْوُ : «الكَلِمَةُ : إِمَّا اسْمٌ ، أَوْ فِعْلُ، أَوْ حَرْفٌ» وَشِبْهُهُ مِمَّا تَكُونُ فِيهِ القِسْمَةُ حَاصِرَةٌ، وَهِيَ أَكْثَرُ مِنِ اثْنَيْنِ، فَقَالَ السَّعْدُ : إِنَّهُ مُشْتَمِلٌ عَلَى قَضَايَا حَقِيقِيَّةٍ، وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : الكَلِمَةُ : إِمَّا اسْمٌ أَوْ غَيْرُهُ، وَغَيْرُهُ : إِمَّا فِعْلُ أَوْ حَرْفٌ . وَأَمَّا مَانِعَةُ الجَمْع وَالخُلُوِّ فَقَدْ قِيْلَ : بِجَوَازِ تَرْكِيبِهِمَا مِنْ أَكْثَرِ مِنِ اثْنَيْنِ؛ لِصِحَّةِ الوَسَائِطِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْءِ وَمَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ أَوْ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ . وَتَعَقَّبَهُ السَّعْدُ : بِأَنَّ النِّسْبَةَ لَا تَتَحَقَّقُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ، وَأَنَّ نَحْوَ : «الحَيَوَانُ: إِمَّا إِنْسَانٌ، أَوْ فَرَسٌ، أَوْ طَائِرٌ، قَضَايَا مَوَانِعُ جَمْعِ أَوْ خُلُو . قوله : (وَأَمَّا نَحْوُ : الكَلِمَةُ : إِمَّا اسْمٌ... إلخ) مثله الشَّكل في المنطق : إما أوَّل، أو ثان، أو ثالث، أو رابعٌ ; لأنَّه يتوهم في هذا وشبهه أنَّ القضيَّة حقيقيَّة; لأنَّ مجموع الأجزاء لا تجتمع ولا ترتفع. قوله : (فَقَالَ السَّعْدُ : إِنَّهُ مُشْتَمِلٌ عَلَى قَضَايَا وحاصلُ كلامه أنَّ الحقيقيَّة لا تتركَّبُ إِلَّا مِن جزأين فقط; إذ لا تتحقق النّسبة إِلَّا بين اثنين وأَمَّا ما أورد مِنَ الأمثلة فمشتمل على قضايا، والتَّقديرُ : الشَّكل إمَّا أوّل أو غيره وغيره : إمَّا ثان أو غيره، ... وهكذا . قوله : (فَقَدْ قِيلَ : بِجَوَاز ... إلخ) وعليه درج صاحب المختصر » تبعاً للعقباني وابن مرزوق وجرى عليه كثيرٌ مِنَ النَّاس، لكنَّ التَّحقيقُ ما حقَّقه السَّعد في شرح الشَّمسيَّة» تبعاً للقطب في «شرح المطالع، وهو ما نقله هذا الشارح . قوله : ( لِصِحَّةِ الوَسَائِطِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْء وَمَا هُوَ أَعَمُّ أَوْ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ) نحو: «إما أن يكون الشَّيء شجراً، وإما أن يكون حجراً، وإما أن يكون بشراً في مانعة الجمع، ونحو: «إما أن يكون غير شجر، أو غير حجر، أو غير بشر» في مانعة الخلو . قوله : (وَتَعَقَّبَهُ السَّعْدُ :أي: بما مرَّ في الحقيقيَّة مِن أنَّها في التحقيق قضايا ثلاث موانع جمع في المثال الأوّل، وخلو في الثَّاني .
And as for what is like: 'The word: either a noun, or a verb, or a particle' and similar cases where the division is exhaustive and is more than two, al-Sa'd said: it comprises genuine propositions, and the estimation is: the word is either a noun or other than it, and other than it is either a verb or a particle. As for exclusive disjunction (man'iat al-jam' wa al-khuluw), it has been said: it is permissible to compose them from more than two, due to the validity of intermediates between a thing and what is more general or more specific than its negation. And al-Sa'd refuted him: because the relationship is only realized between two, and that examples like 'The animal: either human, or horse, or bird' are propositions that are exclusive of conjunction or exclusiveness. His statement: (And as for what is like: 'The word: either a noun... etc.') the same is the figure in logic: either first, or second, or third, or fourth; because one might imagine in this and its like that the proposition is genuine; because the totality of parts does not gather and does not rise. His statement: (So al-Sa'd said: it comprises propositions) and the summary of his speech is that the genuine proposition is only composed of two parts only; since the relationship is only realized between two. And as for the examples given, they comprise propositions, and the estimation is: the figure is either first or other than it, and other than it is either second or other than it, and so on. His statement: (And it has been said: it is permissible... etc.) and upon this proceeded the author of al-Mukhtasar, following al-'Uqabani and Ibn Marzuq, and many people followed it, but the verified view is what al-Sa'd verified in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya, following al-Qutb in 'Sharh al-Mutali',' and it is what this commentator has transmitted. His statement: (due to the validity of intermediates between a thing and what is more general or more specific than its negation) for example: 'Either the thing is a tree, or it is a stone, or it is a human' in exclusive conjunction, and like: 'Either it is non-tree, or non-stone, or non-human' in exclusive exclusiveness. His statement: (And al-Sa'd refuted him: i.e. by what has passed regarding the genuine proposition, that in reality they are three propositions exclusive of conjunction in the first example, and exclusive of exclusiveness in the second.)
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage discusses whether a disjunctive proposition (shartiyya) with more than two parts can be a single genuine disjunction (haqiqiyya) or must be analyzed as a set of binary propositions. The main issue arises from examples like 'The word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle'—a threefold division. Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) argues that this cannot be a single proposition because the relation (nisba) of disjunction only holds between two terms. He insists that it is covertly multiple propositions: 'The word is either a noun or something else, and that something else is either a verb or a particle.' Similarly, logical figures (first, second, third, fourth) must be analyzed as a series of binary divisions. Others, such as the author of al-Mukhtasar (perhaps Ibn al-Hajib?), allowed multi-part exclusive disjunctions (man'iat al-jam' or man'iat al-khuluw) on the grounds that there are intermediate possibilities between a thing and its contrary; for example, between 'tree' and 'stone' there is 'human'. However, al-Sa'd rejects this, maintaining that the disjunctive relation is only between two items; the example 'animal: human or horse or bird' is actually three separate exclusive propositions: 'It is either human or non-human', then 'if non-human, either horse or non-horse', etc. The commentary (sharh) explains each point, noting that al-Sa'd's view is the verified one (tahqiq) followed by the author of this commentary (al-Bannani). This dispute centers on the logical structure of disjunctions and has implications for how we analyze complex divisions in reasoning, such as in jurisprudence (fiqh) when classifying rulings or species.

مَانِعَةُ الجَمْع
مَانِعَةُ الخُلُو
الوَسَائِط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Sa'd, how should the statement 'The word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle' be analyzed?
A) As a single genuine disjunction (haqiqiyya) with three parts.
B) As two genuine disjunctions: 'The word is either a noun or non-noun; non-noun is either a verb or particle.'
C) As an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') that prevents conjunction.
D) As a conditional proposition with an antecedent and consequent.

Key Takeaway
A genuine disjunction is inherently binary; any apparent multi-part disjunction is actually a conjunction of binary disjunctions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the statement 'The act is either wajib, haram, mandub, makruh, or mubah' into a series of binary genuine disjunctions following al-Sa'd's method. Write out the decomposed propositions.
?
Evaluate whether the division of rulings can be considered a multi-part exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam' or mani'at al-khuluw). Does al-Sa'd's objection apply here? Justify your answer using the concept of intermediates.

Distinguishing Genuine from Exclusive Disjunctions
Author explains how to identify genuine vs. exclusive disjunctions by the final proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a series of disjunctive propositions contains genuine (haqiqiyya) or exclusive (mani'at al-jam') disjunctions by examining the exhaustiveness of the division.

Core Idea
The final proposition reveals whether a division is exhaustive or not, determining if it's genuine or exclusive.

In logical analysis, we often encounter series of disjunctive propositions. How do we know if each binary disjunction is a genuine alternative (haqiqiyya) or an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam')? The key lies in the exhaustiveness of the division, which becomes apparent in the last proposition of the series. This lesson will walk you through the method illustrated by the commentator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division pattern
Look at the first disjunction: it typically divides a genus into a species and a residue (e.g., word: noun or non-noun).
2
Check the next disjunction
Does it divide the residue exhaustively? In exhaustive division, the residue is fully covered (e.g., non-noun: verb or particle – these are all possible non-noun word types in Arabic grammar).
3
Examine the last proposition
If the division is exhaustive (hasira), the last proposition is a genuine disjunction (haqiqiyya). If not exhaustive, the last proposition becomes an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') because the division leaves out possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : عَلَى تَقْدِيرِهِ لَا يَبْقَى فَرْقُ بَيْنَ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى حَقِيقِيَّاتٍ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى مَوَانِعَ جَمْعِ أَوْ خُلُوِّ. قُلْتُ : الفَرْقُ بَيْنَهُمَا يَتَبَيَّنُ بِالأَخِيرَةِ مِنَ القَضَايَا ، فَإِنَّهَا إِذَا كَانَتِ القِسْمَةُ حَاصِرَةٌ تَكُونُ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ ; نَحْوُ : «الكَلِمَةُ : إِمَّا اسْمٌ أَوْ غَيْرُهُ، وَغَيْرُهُ: إِمَّا فِعْلٌ أَوْ حَرْفٌ»، فَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا حَقِيقِيَّةٌ ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ غَيْرَ حَاصِرَةٍ كَمَا فِي المِثَالِ الثَّانِي تَكُونُ الأَخِيرَةُ مَانِعَةُ جَمْع; لِأَنَّ الأَصْلَ: «الحَيَوَانُ : إِمَّا إِنْسَانُ أَوْ غَيْرُهُ ، وَغَيْرُهُ: إِمَّا فَرَسٌ أَوْ طَائِرٌ، فَهَذِهِ الأَخِيرَةُ مَانِعَةُ جَمْعٍ - الثَّانِي : قَدْ عُلِمَ أَنَّ المَقْصُودَ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ هُوَ الحُكْمُ بِمَا تَضَمَّنَتْهُ مِنْ صُحْبَةٍ أَوْ تَنَافُرٍ بِأَنْوَاعِهِ [ج/ ٢٦] ، أَوْ سَلْبِهِمَا ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَصِدْقُهَا هُوَ مُطَابَقَةُ حُكْمُهُمَا المَذْكُورُ للواقع، وَكَذِبُهَا عَدَمُهَا مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارٍ لِكَوْنِ طَرَفِيهَا فِي أَصْلِهِمَا صَادِقَيْنِ أَوْ كَاذِبَيْنِ أَوْ مُخْتَلِفَيْنِ، فَتَصْدُقُ مَعَ كَذِبِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فِي الأَصْلِ; نَحْرُ: لَوْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ حَجَراً لَكَانَ جَمَاداً»، فَهَذِهِ صَادِقَةٌ لِصِدْقِ لُزُومِ جَمَادِيَّتِهِ لِحَجَرِيَّتِهِ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ جَمَادِيَّتِهِ وَحَجَرِيَّتِهِ مُسْتَحِيلَتَيْنِ، وَمِنْ ذَلِكَ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى : وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا الهَهُ إِلَّا اللهُ لَفَسَدَنَا) [الأنبياء: ٢٣]. قوله : (فَإِنْ قُلْتَ أصلُ هذا البحث للسيد مع السَّعد. وقوله: «قُلْتُ» هو جواب للهلالي الإيراد المذكور ; ونص كلامه باختصار : الفرق بينهما أنَّ ما اشتمل على جميع الأقسام يمكن جعله من حقيقيَّات من غير أن يُزاد قسم آخر، وما لم يشتمل على جميعها لا يمكن ذلك إلا بزيادة شيء آخر غير مذكور ولا مقصود، فالقائلُ إمَّا أن يكون الشَّيء شجراً أو حجراً أو بشراً إنَّما حكم الثَّلاثة المذكورات لا غير وهي يمكن اجتماعها على الكذب، فلا يمكن جعلها، بالتّنافر بين والحالة هذه حقيقيَّات ثلاثاً . قوله : (فَصِدْقُهَا هُوَ مُطَابَقَةُ حُكْمُهُمَا المَذْكُورُ ... إلخ) وذلك أنَّ صدق القضيَّة بمطابقة ما تضمنته مِنَ الحكم للواقع، فصدقُ الحملية بموافقة النسبة التي فيها للواقع، فيكون صدق الشرطية بموافقة حكمها الذي هو الاتصال والانفصال للواقع ; إذ هو المحكوم به في الشَّرطيَّة، ولا عبرة بصدق الطرفين ولا كذبهما.
If you say: On his assumption, no distinction remains between what comprises genuine propositions and what comprises exclusives of conjunction or exclusiveness. I say: The distinction between them is made clear by the last of the propositions. For when the division is exhaustive, it is genuine; like 'The word: either a noun or other than it, and other than it: either a verb or a particle', so each of them is genuine. And when it is not exhaustive, as in the second example, the last is exclusive of conjunction; because the original is: 'The animal: either human or other than it, and other than it: either horse or bird', so this last is exclusive of conjunction. Second: It has been known that the meaning of the conditional is the judgment of what it comprises of conjunction or disjunction in its types, or their negation. And then its truth is the correspondence of its mentioned judgment to reality, and its falsity is lack thereof, without consideration of whether its parts in themselves are true or false or different. So it is true even with both parts being false in themselves; like: 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate', this is true because the necessary connection of his inanimateness to his stone-ness is true, even if his inanimateness and stone-ness are both impossible. And of that is the saying of the Exalted: 'And if there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted' [Qur'an 21:23]. His statement: (If you say: The origin of this investigation is between al-Sayyid and al-Sa'd. And his saying: 'I say' is the answer of al-Hilali to the mentioned objection; and the text of his speech in brief: The distinction between them is that what comprises all the divisions can be made of genuine propositions without adding another division, and what does not comprise all cannot do so except by adding something else not mentioned nor intended. So the one who says 'Either the thing is a tree, or a stone, or a human' only judged the three mentioned, no more, and they can all be false together, so one cannot make them, with the disunion in this state, three genuine propositions. His statement: (So its truth is the correspondence of its mentioned judgment... etc.) and that is because the truth of a proposition is by the correspondence of what it comprises of judgment to reality. So the truth of a categorical is by the agreement of the relation in it to reality, and the truth of a conditional is by the agreement of its judgment, which is conjunction and disjunction, to reality; for it is what is judged in the conditional, and there is no consideration of the truth or falsity of the parts.)
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: how to distinguish between genuine binary disjunctions (haqiqiyya) and exclusive disjunctions (man'iat al-jam' or al-khuluw) when we have a series of propositions, and the truth conditions of conditionals. The commentator begins with an objection: if all multi-part disjunctions must be reduced to binary propositions as al-Sa'd claims, how can we tell whether a series is a set of genuine disjunctions or a set of exclusive disjunctions? The answer is that the distinction is seen in the 'last proposition' (الاخيرة من القضايا). If the division is exhaustive (hasira), as in 'The word is either a noun or non-noun; non-noun is either a verb or particle', then each proposition in the series is a genuine binary disjunction (haqiqiyya). If the division is not exhaustive, like 'The animal is either human or non-human; non-human is either horse or bird', then the last proposition ('non-human is either horse or bird') is an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') because the division does not cover all possibilities (e.g., there could be other animals). The commentator then moves to a more general point about the truth of conditionals (shartiyya). The purpose of a conditional is to assert a relation of conjunction (concomitance) or disjunction (mutual exclusiveness) between two parts. Its truth is defined by the correspondence of that relation to reality, regardless of the truth values of the individual parts. For example, 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate' is true because the implication (luzum) holds—stone-ness implies inanimateness—even though both the antecedent and consequent are impossible (Zayd cannot be a stone). Similarly, the Qur'anic verse 'If there were gods besides Allah, they would both be corrupted' is true because the conditional relation is sound, even if the antecedent is false in reality. This teaching is crucial for understanding hypothetical reasoning in theology (kalam) and jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh), where conditionals are used even when the antecedent may be false or impossible.

Key Terms
قِسْمَة حَاصِرَة
مَانِعَة جَمْع
حَقِيقِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the division 'The animal: either human or non-human; non-human: either horse or bird', why is the last proposition an exclusive disjunction?
A) Because the first division is not exhaustive.
B) Because the second division leaves out other non-human animals like cats.
C) Because both 'horse' and 'bird' are true simultaneously.
D) Because the division is exhaustive.

Key Takeaway
The exhaustiveness of a division determines whether its component disjunctions are genuine or exclusive, and this is revealed by the last proposition in the series.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the classification of acts in fiqh: 'The act is either wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, or haram.' Break this into a series of binary disjunctions and determine whether the last disjunction is genuine or exclusive by checking exhaustiveness.
