Truth Conditions of Conditionals
Author explains the truth conditions of conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth of a conditional proposition based on the relation (luzum or in'ikas) between its parts, not the truth of the parts themselves.

Core Idea
A conditional is true if the relation between antecedent and consequent holds in reality, even if both parts are false or impossible.

Many beginners think a conditional like 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate' is false because Zayd is not a stone. However, Islamic logicians emphasize that the truth of a conditional depends solely on the truth of the implication (luzum) between the parts. This lesson unpacks that principle with examples from the Qur'an and everyday reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core judgment
Determine whether the conditional asserts conjunction (ittisal), disjunction (infisal), or their negation. The truth depends on this relation.
2
Ignore the truth values of parts
Set aside whether the antecedent and consequent are true or false in reality. Their truth is irrelevant for the conditional's truth.
3
Evaluate the relation
Check if the relation between parts holds in reality. For a conditional of conjunction, ask: does the antecedent necessarily imply the consequent? For disjunction, ask: are they mutually exclusive? If yes, the conditional is true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : عَلَى تَقْدِيرِهِ لَا يَبْقَى فَرْقُ بَيْنَ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى حَقِيقِيَّاتٍ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى مَوَانِعَ جَمْعِ أَوْ خُلُوِّ. قُلْتُ : الفَرْقُ بَيْنَهُمَا يَتَبَيَّنُ بِالأَخِيرَةِ مِنَ القَضَايَا ، فَإِنَّهَا إِذَا كَانَتِ القِسْمَةُ حَاصِرَةٌ تَكُونُ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ ; نَحْوُ : «الكَلِمَةُ : إِمَّا اسْمٌ أَوْ غَيْرُهُ، وَغَيْرُهُ: إِمَّا فِعْلٌ أَوْ حَرْفٌ»، فَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا حَقِيقِيَّةٌ ، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ غَيْرَ حَاصِرَةٍ كَمَا فِي المِثَالِ الثَّانِي تَكُونُ الأَخِيرَةُ مَانِعَةُ جَمْع; لِأَنَّ الأَصْلَ: «الحَيَوَانُ : إِمَّا إِنْسَانُ أَوْ غَيْرُهُ ، وَغَيْرُهُ: إِمَّا فَرَسٌ أَوْ طَائِرٌ، فَهَذِهِ الأَخِيرَةُ مَانِعَةُ جَمْعٍ - الثَّانِي : قَدْ عُلِمَ أَنَّ المَقْصُودَ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ هُوَ الحُكْمُ بِمَا تَضَمَّنَتْهُ مِنْ صُحْبَةٍ أَوْ تَنَافُرٍ بِأَنْوَاعِهِ [ج/ ٢٦] ، أَوْ سَلْبِهِمَا ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَصِدْقُهَا هُوَ مُطَابَقَةُ حُكْمُهُمَا المَذْكُورُ للواقع، وَكَذِبُهَا عَدَمُهَا مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارٍ لِكَوْنِ طَرَفِيهَا فِي أَصْلِهِمَا صَادِقَيْنِ أَوْ كَاذِبَيْنِ أَوْ مُخْتَلِفَيْنِ، فَتَصْدُقُ مَعَ كَذِبِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فِي الأَصْلِ; نَحْرُ: لَوْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ حَجَراً لَكَانَ جَمَاداً»، فَهَذِهِ صَادِقَةٌ لِصِدْقِ لُزُومِ جَمَادِيَّتِهِ لِحَجَرِيَّتِهِ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ جَمَادِيَّتِهِ وَحَجَرِيَّتِهِ مُسْتَحِيلَتَيْنِ، وَمِنْ ذَلِكَ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى : وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا الهَهُ إِلَّا اللهُ لَفَسَدَنَا) [الأنبياء: ٢٣]. قوله : (فَإِنْ قُلْتَ أصلُ هذا البحث للسيد مع السَّعد. وقوله: «قُلْتُ» هو جواب للهلالي الإيراد المذكور ; ونص كلامه باختصار : الفرق بينهما أنَّ ما اشتمل على جميع الأقسام يمكن جعله من حقيقيَّات من غير أن يُزاد قسم آخر، وما لم يشتمل على جميعها لا يمكن ذلك إلا بزيادة شيء آخر غير مذكور ولا مقصود، فالقائلُ إمَّا أن يكون الشَّيء شجراً أو حجراً أو بشراً إنَّما حكم الثَّلاثة المذكورات لا غير وهي يمكن اجتماعها على الكذب، فلا يمكن جعلها، بالتّنافر بين والحالة هذه حقيقيَّات ثلاثاً . قوله : (فَصِدْقُهَا هُوَ مُطَابَقَةُ حُكْمُهُمَا المَذْكُورُ ... إلخ) وذلك أنَّ صدق القضيَّة بمطابقة ما تضمنته مِنَ الحكم للواقع، فصدقُ الحملية بموافقة النسبة التي فيها للواقع، فيكون صدق الشرطية بموافقة حكمها الذي هو الاتصال والانفصال للواقع ; إذ هو المحكوم به في الشَّرطيَّة، ولا عبرة بصدق الطرفين ولا كذبهما.
If you say: On his assumption, no distinction remains between what comprises genuine propositions and what comprises exclusives of conjunction or exclusiveness. I say: The distinction between them is made clear by the last of the propositions. For when the division is exhaustive, it is genuine; like 'The word: either a noun or other than it, and other than it: either a verb or a particle', so each of them is genuine. And when it is not exhaustive, as in the second example, the last is exclusive of conjunction; because the original is: 'The animal: either human or other than it, and other than it: either horse or bird', so this last is exclusive of conjunction. Second: It has been known that the meaning of the conditional is the judgment of what it comprises of conjunction or disjunction in its types, or their negation. And then its truth is the correspondence of its mentioned judgment to reality, and its falsity is lack thereof, without consideration of whether its parts in themselves are true or false or different. So it is true even with both parts being false in themselves; like: 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate', this is true because the necessary connection of his inanimateness to his stone-ness is true, even if his inanimateness and stone-ness are both impossible. And of that is the saying of the Exalted: 'And if there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted' [Qur'an 21:23]. His statement: (If you say: The origin of this investigation is between al-Sayyid and al-Sa'd. And his saying: 'I say' is the answer of al-Hilali to the mentioned objection; and the text of his speech in brief: The distinction between them is that what comprises all the divisions can be made of genuine propositions without adding another division, and what does not comprise all cannot do so except by adding something else not mentioned nor intended. So the one who says 'Either the thing is a tree, or a stone, or a human' only judged the three mentioned, no more, and they can all be false together, so one cannot make them, with the disunion in this state, three genuine propositions. His statement: (So its truth is the correspondence of its mentioned judgment... etc.) and that is because the truth of a proposition is by the correspondence of what it comprises of judgment to reality. So the truth of a categorical is by the agreement of the relation in it to reality, and the truth of a conditional is by the agreement of its judgment, which is conjunction and disjunction, to reality; for it is what is judged in the conditional, and there is no consideration of the truth or falsity of the parts.)
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: how to distinguish between genuine binary disjunctions (haqiqiyya) and exclusive disjunctions (man'iat al-jam' or al-khuluw) when we have a series of propositions, and the truth conditions of conditionals. The commentator begins with an objection: if all multi-part disjunctions must be reduced to binary propositions as al-Sa'd claims, how can we tell whether a series is a set of genuine disjunctions or a set of exclusive disjunctions? The answer is that the distinction is seen in the 'last proposition' (الاخيرة من القضايا). If the division is exhaustive (hasira), as in 'The word is either a noun or non-noun; non-noun is either a verb or particle', then each proposition in the series is a genuine binary disjunction (haqiqiyya). If the division is not exhaustive, like 'The animal is either human or non-human; non-human is either horse or bird', then the last proposition ('non-human is either horse or bird') is an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') because the division does not cover all possibilities (e.g., there could be other animals). The commentator then moves to a more general point about the truth of conditionals (shartiyya). The purpose of a conditional is to assert a relation of conjunction (concomitance) or disjunction (mutual exclusiveness) between two parts. Its truth is defined by the correspondence of that relation to reality, regardless of the truth values of the individual parts. For example, 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate' is true because the implication (luzum) holds—stone-ness implies inanimateness—even though both the antecedent and consequent are impossible (Zayd cannot be a stone). Similarly, the Qur'anic verse 'If there were gods besides Allah, they would both be corrupted' is true because the conditional relation is sound, even if the antecedent is false in reality. This teaching is crucial for understanding hypothetical reasoning in theology (kalam) and jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh), where conditionals are used even when the antecedent may be false or impossible.

Key Terms
الشَّرْطِيَّة
لُزُوم
صُحْبَة
تَنَافُر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the conditional 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate' considered true, even though Zayd is not a stone?
A) Because the antecedent is true.
B) Because the consequent is true.
C) Because the implication (luzum) between stone-ness and inanimateness is true.
D) Because it is an exclusive disjunction.

Key Takeaway
A conditional's truth depends solely on the correspondence of its judgment (conjunction or disjunction) to reality, not on the truth values of its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional: 'If the Qur'an were the speech of a human, it would contain inconsistencies.' Determine its truth by examining the relation between the antecedent and consequent, not the actual truth of the antecedent.

Truth Conditions of the Mutasila Conditional
Author classifies truth conditions of mutasila propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three true cases and four false cases of the mutasila luzumiyya and apply them to arguments from Kalam.

Core Idea
A conditional's truth depends on logical connection, not truth values of parts.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the truth conditions of the mutasila (conditional) proposition. He explains that the truth of a conditional is not simply a matter of the truth of its antecedent and consequent; rather, it hinges on the real implication between them. This understanding is critical for evaluating conditional arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the implication condition
Note that the mutasila luzumiyya is true only when the antecedent truly implies the consequent, not merely when both parts are true or false. The commentator emphasizes that the parts themselves are not truth-bearers; only the whole compound is.
2
Identify the three true cases
From the passage: the mutasila is true when (a) both parts are true, (b) both are false, or (c) the antecedent is false and the consequent is true. For example: 'If Zayd is a human, he is an animal' (both true).
3
Identify the four false cases
The mutasila is false when (a) both parts are true but no real implication, (b) both false but no implication, (c) antecedent true and consequent false, (d) antecedent false and consequent true but no implication. Example for (c): 'If Zayd is an animal, he is a stone.'
4
Apply to a Kalam argument
Take the conditional: 'If the world is temporally originated, it has a creator.' Since the implication holds, this conditional is true even if the antecedent is false (e.g., for someone who denies temporality). Determine its truth condition.

Source Text

وَمَعَ كَذِبِ المُقَدَّم فَقَط ; نَحْوُ : لَوْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ فِي السَّمَاءِ مَا نَجَا مِنَ المَوْتِ»، وَلَا تَخْفَى أَمْثِلَةُ بَقِيَّةِ الأَقْسَامِ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ وَفِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ. وَإِنَّمَا قَيَّدْنَا بِـ «الأَصْلِ» ; لِأَنَّ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لَا يُوصَفَانِ بِالكَذِبِ حَالَ كَوْنِهِمَا فِي القَضِيَّةِ ; لأَنَّ جُزْءَ القَضِيَّةِ لَا يُوْصَفُ بِصِدْقٍ وَلَا كَذِبٍ، افْهَمْ.
قصارة -
قوله : (وَمَعَ كَذِبِ المُقَدَّمِ فَقَط) اقتصر على المثال ولم يأت بمثال عكسه، وهو ما تألف من مقدَّم صادق وتالي كاذب ; لكون الصدق فيه محالاً ; لاستدعائه المحال، وهو صدق الكاذب وكذب الصادق; لأنَّ صدق الملزوم يستلزم صدق اللازم، وكذب اللازم يستلزم كذب الملزوم. قوله : (وَلَا تَخْفَى أَمْثِلَةُ بَقِيَّةِ الأَقْسَامِ) بيان ذلك :
- أنَّ المتّصلة الصادقة تتركَّب مِن صادقين; نحو: «إن كان زيد إنساناً كان حيواناً»، ومن كاذبين كما مرَّ عنِ الشَّارح، ومِن مقدَّم كاذب وتال صادق; كما مر، دون العكس.
والمتّصلة الكاذبة : تكذب عن كاذبين; نحو: إن كان زيد حماراً كان حجراً»، وعن صادقين; نحو: كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقاً كان الحمار ناهقاً حيث لا علاقة، وعن مقدَّم صادق وتال كاذب; نحو : «إن كان زيد حيواناً كان حجراً»، وعن عكسه; نحو: إن كان زيد حجراً كان ناطقاً». فقد بان لك أنَّ المتّصلة اللُّزوميّة تكذب عن أقسام أربعة، وتصدق عن ثلاث.
- وأما المنفصلة :
فالحقيقيَّة : تصدق عن صادق وكاذب ; نحو: «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً»، وتكذب عن صادقين نحو : إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً وإما أن يكون ناطقاً، وعن كاذبين ; نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد حجراً أو شجراً».
ومانعة الجمع: تصدق عن كاذبين كالمثال المذكور قبل وعن صادق وكاذب; نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو حماراً»، وتكذب عن صادقين; نحو: «إمَّا أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو ناطقاً».
ومانعة الخلو : تصدقُ عن صادقين; نحو: «إما أن يكون الحَيَوان متحرِّكاً أو نامياً، وعن صادق وكاذب ; نحو: «إما أن يكون الحيوان متحرِّكاً أو جامداً»، وتكذب عن كاذبين; نحو: «إما أن يكون الحجر ناطقاً أو متحرّكاً بالإرادة.
And along with the falsity of the antecedent only, such as: 'If Zayd were in the sky, he would not escape death.' And the examples of the remaining divisions are not hidden in the mutasila and in the munfasila. We only restricted with 'the original' because the two parts are not described as false while they are in the proposition; because a part of the proposition is not described as true or false. Understand.
Margin: His statement: 'and along with the falsity of the antecedent only' – he confined to the example and did not bring the example of its opposite, which is composed of a true antecedent and a false consequent; because truth in it is impossible, because it would require the impossible, which is the truth of the false and the falsity of the true; because the truth of the implicans necessitates the truth of the implicatum, and the falsity of the implicatum necessitates the falsity of the implicans. His statement: 'and the examples of the remaining divisions are not hidden' – its explanation: The true mutasila is composed of two true [parts], such as: 'If Zayd is a human, he is an animal'; and of two false [parts] as mentioned by the commentator; and of a false antecedent and a true consequent, as mentioned, but not the opposite. And the false mutasila: it is false from two false, such as: 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is a stone'; and from two true, such as: 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying' where there is no relation; and from a true antecedent and a false consequent, such as: 'If Zayd is an animal, he is a stone'; and from its opposite, such as: 'If Zayd is a stone, he is rational.' It is thus clear that the implicative mutasila is false in four cases and true in three.
As for the munfasila: The haqiqiyya: it is true from true and false, such as: 'Either this number is even or odd'; and it is false from two true, such as: 'Either Zayd is a human or he is rational'; and from two false, such as: 'Either Zayd is a stone or a tree.'
Mani'at al-jam': it is true from two false as the previously mentioned example, and from true and false, such as: 'Either Zayd is a human or a donkey'; and it is false from two true, such as: 'Either Zayd is a human or rational.'
Mani'at al-khuluw: it is true from two true, such as: 'Either the animal is moving or growing'; and from true and false, such as: 'Either the animal is moving or inanimate'; and it is false from two false, such as: 'Either the stone is rational or moving by will.'
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the truth conditions of conditional propositions (mutasila and munfasila) from a commentary on a logical text. The passage begins by discussing the case where a conditional is true with a false antecedent only, using the example 'If Zayd were in the sky, he would not escape death.' The commentator notes that the other cases are not hidden. Then it clarifies why we restrict to 'the original': because the parts of a conditional are not independently truth-bearing; truth and falsity apply only to the whole proposition. The marginal note ('قصارة') explains why the author only gave the example of false antecedent and not the opposite (true antecedent and false consequent): such a case is impossible in a true conditional because truth of the antecedent necessitates truth of the consequent, and falsity of the consequent necessitates falsity of the antecedent. The passage then enumerates the four cases of false mutasila luzumiyya and the three true cases, with examples from logical and everyday reasoning. For the disjunctive (munfasila), it classifies three types: haqiqiyya (exclusive disjunction), mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction), and mani'at al-khuluw (preventing emptiness). Each type's truth and falsity conditions are given with examples. All examples are drawn from classification of animals, numbers, etc., which are typical in Islamic logic. The lesson helps students understand that the truth of a conditional is not simply about the truth of its parts but about the connection between them.

المقدَّم
التالي

Key Takeaway
The mutasila luzumiyya is false when the antecedent is true and the consequent is false, assuming a real implication; otherwise it is false in three additional cases where the implication is absent.

Truth Conditions of the Munfasila Disjunction
Author classifies truth conditions of munfasila propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunction (haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khuluw) and determine their truth conditions with examples from Fiqh.

Core Idea
Disjunctions differ in whether they allow both true, both false, or only one true.

This passage classifies disjunctive propositions (munfasila) into three types, each with distinct truth conditions. Understanding these types is crucial for evaluating disjunctive arguments in Islamic jurisprudence, where options are often presented as mutually exclusive or exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of disjunction
The passage names: haqiqiyya (exclusive disjunction), mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction), and mani'at al-khuluw (preventing emptiness). Each has different conditions for truth and falsity.
2
Learn the truth conditions for haqiqiyya
Haqiqiyya is true when exactly one disjunct is true (e.g., 'Either this number is even or odd'), and false when both are true or both false.
3
Learn the truth conditions for mani'at al-jam'
This type is true when at least one part is false (so it allows both false but forbids both true). Example: 'Either Zayd is a human or a donkey' is true because he cannot be both.
4
Learn the truth conditions for mani'at al-khuluw
This type is true when at least one part is true (so it allows both true but forbids both false). Example: 'Either the animal is moving or growing' is true because it must be one of these.
5
Apply to a Fiqh example
Consider the legal ruling: 'Either this contract is valid or invalid.' Classify this disjunction and determine its truth condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَعَ كَذِبِ المُقَدَّم فَقَط ; نَحْوُ : لَوْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ فِي السَّمَاءِ مَا نَجَا مِنَ المَوْتِ»، وَلَا تَخْفَى أَمْثِلَةُ بَقِيَّةِ الأَقْسَامِ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ وَفِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ. وَإِنَّمَا قَيَّدْنَا بِـ «الأَصْلِ» ; لِأَنَّ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لَا يُوصَفَانِ بِالكَذِبِ حَالَ كَوْنِهِمَا فِي القَضِيَّةِ ; لأَنَّ جُزْءَ القَضِيَّةِ لَا يُوْصَفُ بِصِدْقٍ وَلَا كَذِبٍ، افْهَمْ.
قصارة -
قوله : (وَمَعَ كَذِبِ المُقَدَّمِ فَقَط) اقتصر على المثال ولم يأت بمثال عكسه، وهو ما تألف من مقدَّم صادق وتالي كاذب ; لكون الصدق فيه محالاً ; لاستدعائه المحال، وهو صدق الكاذب وكذب الصادق; لأنَّ صدق الملزوم يستلزم صدق اللازم، وكذب اللازم يستلزم كذب الملزوم. قوله : (وَلَا تَخْفَى أَمْثِلَةُ بَقِيَّةِ الأَقْسَامِ) بيان ذلك :
- أنَّ المتّصلة الصادقة تتركَّب مِن صادقين; نحو: «إن كان زيد إنساناً كان حيواناً»، ومن كاذبين كما مرَّ عنِ الشَّارح، ومِن مقدَّم كاذب وتال صادق; كما مر، دون العكس.
والمتّصلة الكاذبة : تكذب عن كاذبين; نحو: إن كان زيد حماراً كان حجراً»، وعن صادقين; نحو: كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقاً كان الحمار ناهقاً حيث لا علاقة، وعن مقدَّم صادق وتال كاذب; نحو : «إن كان زيد حيواناً كان حجراً»، وعن عكسه; نحو: إن كان زيد حجراً كان ناطقاً». فقد بان لك أنَّ المتّصلة اللُّزوميّة تكذب عن أقسام أربعة، وتصدق عن ثلاث.
- وأما المنفصلة :
فالحقيقيَّة : تصدق عن صادق وكاذب ; نحو: «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً»، وتكذب عن صادقين نحو : إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً وإما أن يكون ناطقاً، وعن كاذبين ; نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد حجراً أو شجراً».
ومانعة الجمع: تصدق عن كاذبين كالمثال المذكور قبل وعن صادق وكاذب; نحو: «إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو حماراً»، وتكذب عن صادقين; نحو: «إمَّا أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو ناطقاً».
ومانعة الخلو : تصدقُ عن صادقين; نحو: «إما أن يكون الحَيَوان متحرِّكاً أو نامياً، وعن صادق وكاذب ; نحو: «إما أن يكون الحيوان متحرِّكاً أو جامداً»، وتكذب عن كاذبين; نحو: «إما أن يكون الحجر ناطقاً أو متحرّكاً بالإرادة.
And along with the falsity of the antecedent only, such as: 'If Zayd were in the sky, he would not escape death.' And the examples of the remaining divisions are not hidden in the mutasila and in the munfasila. We only restricted with 'the original' because the two parts are not described as false while they are in the proposition; because a part of the proposition is not described as true or false. Understand.
Margin: His statement: 'and along with the falsity of the antecedent only' – he confined to the example and did not bring the example of its opposite, which is composed of a true antecedent and a false consequent; because truth in it is impossible, because it would require the impossible, which is the truth of the false and the falsity of the true; because the truth of the implicans necessitates the truth of the implicatum, and the falsity of the implicatum necessitates the falsity of the implicans. His statement: 'and the examples of the remaining divisions are not hidden' – its explanation: The true mutasila is composed of two true [parts], such as: 'If Zayd is a human, he is an animal'; and of two false [parts] as mentioned by the commentator; and of a false antecedent and a true consequent, as mentioned, but not the opposite. And the false mutasila: it is false from two false, such as: 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is a stone'; and from two true, such as: 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying' where there is no relation; and from a true antecedent and a false consequent, such as: 'If Zayd is an animal, he is a stone'; and from its opposite, such as: 'If Zayd is a stone, he is rational.' It is thus clear that the implicative mutasila is false in four cases and true in three.
As for the munfasila: The haqiqiyya: it is true from true and false, such as: 'Either this number is even or odd'; and it is false from two true, such as: 'Either Zayd is a human or he is rational'; and from two false, such as: 'Either Zayd is a stone or a tree.'
Mani'at al-jam': it is true from two false as the previously mentioned example, and from true and false, such as: 'Either Zayd is a human or a donkey'; and it is false from two true, such as: 'Either Zayd is a human or rational.'
Mani'at al-khuluw: it is true from two true, such as: 'Either the animal is moving or growing'; and from true and false, such as: 'Either the animal is moving or inanimate'; and it is false from two false, such as: 'Either the stone is rational or moving by will.'
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the truth conditions of conditional propositions (mutasila and munfasila) from a commentary on a logical text. The passage begins by discussing the case where a conditional is true with a false antecedent only, using the example 'If Zayd were in the sky, he would not escape death.' The commentator notes that the other cases are not hidden. Then it clarifies why we restrict to 'the original': because the parts of a conditional are not independently truth-bearing; truth and falsity apply only to the whole proposition. The marginal note ('قصارة') explains why the author only gave the example of false antecedent and not the opposite (true antecedent and false consequent): such a case is impossible in a true conditional because truth of the antecedent necessitates truth of the consequent, and falsity of the consequent necessitates falsity of the antecedent. The passage then enumerates the four cases of false mutasila luzumiyya and the three true cases, with examples from logical and everyday reasoning. For the disjunctive (munfasila), it classifies three types: haqiqiyya (exclusive disjunction), mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction), and mani'at al-khuluw (preventing emptiness). Each type's truth and falsity conditions are given with examples. All examples are drawn from classification of animals, numbers, etc., which are typical in Islamic logic. The lesson helps students understand that the truth of a conditional is not simply about the truth of its parts but about the connection between them.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
الحقيقيَّة
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

D) Both true and both false

Key Takeaway
Each type of munfasila has unique truth conditions: haqiqiyya requires exactly one true, mani'at al-jam' forbids both true, and mani'at al-khuluw forbids both false.

The Nature of Conditional Propositions: Logicians vs. Grammarians
Author contrasts logical and grammatical interpretations and presents al-Sayyid's reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical and grammatical interpretations of conditional propositions and explain al-Sayyid's resolution that they are in agreement.

Core Idea
The whole conditional or just the consequent? Al-Sayyid shows both views agree on the linking.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange: al-Sa'd contrasts the logical and grammatical perspectives on conditionals. For logicians, the entire conditional is a declarative statement about implication; for grammarians, only the consequent is declarative. Then al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani refutes the apparent opposition and argues that both perspectives are compatible when we understand that what matters is the linking (rabt) between parts. This debate is essential for correctly analyzing conditional statements in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two perspectives
Find in the Arabic where al-Sa'd describes the logicians' view and the grammarians' view.
Look for phrases like 'باعتبار أهل المنطق' and 'باعتبار أهل العربية'.
2
Understand the logical interpretation
For logicians, both parts are divested of truth-value; the whole conditional is a judgment of implication (luzum).
The subject is the condition, and the predicated is the consequent.
3
Understand the grammatical interpretation
For grammarians, only the consequent (jaza') is khabar; the condition (shart) is a qualifier, like a temporal circumstance.
They interpret 'If you come, I honor you' as 'I honor you at the time of your coming'.
4
Examine al-Sayyid's refutation
He argues they agree because conditionals can be true even if parts are false, as long as the linking matches reality.
This shows the grammatical view also intends a relation, not just a temporal qualifier.
5
Apply to Qur'anic examples
Consider the verses: 'If his shirt is torn from the front, then she is truthful' and 'If there were gods besides Allah, both would have been corrupted.'
In both, the truth of the conditional depends on the linking, not the truth of the parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها
لَكِنْ ذَكَرَ السَّعْدُ أَنَّ كَوْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ انْخَلَعَا عَنْ مَعْنَى الخَبَرِيَّةِ، وَصَارَ مَجْمُوعُهُمَا هُوَ الخَبَرُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِاعْتِبَارِ أَهْلِ المَنْطِقِ، وَأَمَّا بِاعْتِبَارِ أَهْلِ العَرَبِيَّةِ فَالخَبَرُ عِنْدَهُمْ هُوَ الجَزَاءُ فَقَط ، وَهُوَ المُحْتَمِلُ لِلصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ.
وَأَمَّا الشَّرْطُ فَهُوَ قَيْدٌ لِلخَبَرِ فَقَط ، كَمَا يُقَيَّدُ بِالطَّرْفِ، فَمَعْنَى «إِنْ جِئْتَنِي أَكْرَمْتُكَ» عَلَى الاعْتِبَارِ المَنْطِقِيِّ : الحُكْمُ بِلُزُومِ الإِكْرَامِ لِلْمَجِيءِ، وَعَلَى اعْتِبَارِ أَهْلِ العَرَبِيَّةِ : الحُكْمُ بِحُصُولِ الإِكْرَامِ مِنَ المُتَكَلِّمِ مُقَيَّدٌ بِوَقتِ المَجِيْءِ; كَأَنَّهُ قَالَ: «أَكْرِمُكَ وَقتَ مَجِيْنِكَ». وَرَدَّهُ السَّيِّدُ فِي حَوَاشِي المُطَوَّلِ، وَحَقَّقَ : أَنَّ اعْتِبَارَ أَهْلِ العَرَبِيَّةِ مُوَافِقُ لِاعْتِبَارِ المَنَاطِقَةِ ; لِصِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ مَعَ تَخَلُّفِ مَدْلُولِ كُلِّ مِنْ طَرَفِيهَا أَوْ أَحَدِهِمَا إِذَا كَانَ الرَّبْطُ مُوَافِقاً لِلْوَاقِع، وَإِنَّ الرَّبْطَ هُوَ المَقْصُودُ فِي العَرَبِيَّةِ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ; نَحْوُ : ﴿إِن كَانَ قَمِيصُهُ قُدَّ مِن قَبْلِ فَصَدَقَتْ ﴾ [يوسف: (٢٦]، ﴿وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا الهَهُ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَنَا﴾ [الأنبياء: ٢٣] .
قصارة
وما ذكر كلُّه خاص بالموجبات، وَأَمَّا السَّوالب في المتّصلات والمنفصلات فعلى العكس; أي : تصدق فيما تكذب فيه الموجبات وتكذب فيما تصدق فيه، والله تعالى أعلم. قوله : (لَكِنْ ذَكَرَ السَّعْدُ) صَرَّح بذلك في المطوّل»، والتلويح»، و«شرح الشَّمسيَّة»، وما ذكره من أنَّ اعتبار أهل اللسان على خلاف اعتبار أهل الميزان هو ظاهر كلام القزويني ومتبوعه، والحق ما حققه السَّيِّد، على ما سيأتي .
:
قوله : (الحُكْمُ بِلُزُومِ الإِكْرَامِ لِلْمَجِيْءٍ) أي: فيكون المحكوم عليه هو الشرط، والمحكوم به هو الجزاء، ومفهوم القضيَّة: الحكم بلزوم الجزاء للشَّرط، فتصدقُ القضيَّةُ بمطابقة الحكم باللُّزوم للواقع، وتكذب بعدم ذلك، فكلُّ مِنَ الطَّرفين قدِ انخلع عن معنى الخبرية، واحتمال الصدق والكذب .
أو

Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings
But al-Sa'd mentioned that the two parts in the mutasila are divested of the meaning of being a declarative statement, and their sum becomes the declaration, only from the perspective of the logicians. As for the perspective of the Arabic grammarians, the declarative statement in their view is only the consequent, and it is what admits truth and falsity. And as for the condition, it is merely a qualifier for the statement, just as it is qualified by a circumstance. So the meaning of 'If you come to me, I will honor you' according to the logical perspective: the judgment of the implication of honor for coming. And according to the perspective of the Arabic grammarians: the judgment of the occurrence of honor from the speaker qualified by the time of coming, as if he said: 'I honor you at the time of your coming.' Al-Sayyid refuted this in his glosses on al-Mutawwal, and verified that the perspective of the Arabic grammarians is in agreement with the perspective of the logicians, because the conditional is true even when the denotation of each of its parts or one of them is missing, if the linking is in accordance with reality, and that the linking is what is intended in Arabic from conditionals, such as: {If his shirt is torn from the front, then she is truthful} [Yusuf: 26], and {If there were therein gods besides Allah, then both would have been corrupted} [al-Anbiya': 23].
Margin:
All that was mentioned is specific to affirmatives. As for the negatives in mutasilas and munfasilas, it is the opposite; i.e., they are true where the affirmatives are false and false where the affirmatives are true. And Allah knows best. His statement: (But al-Sa'd mentioned) – he stated that in al-Mutawwal, al-Talwih, and Sharh al-Shamsiyya, and what he mentioned that the perspective of the people of the language is contrary to the perspective of the people of the scale [logic] is apparent from the speech of al-Qazwini and his followers, but the truth is what al-Sayyid verified, as will come later.
:
His statement: (the judgment of the implication of honor for coming) i.e., the subject of judgment is the condition, and the predicated is the consequent, and the meaning of the proposition is the judgment of implication of the consequent for the condition. Thus the proposition is true by the correspondence of the judgment of implication with reality, and false by its absence, so each of the two parts is divested of the meaning of being a declarative statement and the possibility of truth and falsity.
Or
His statement: (And al-Sayyid refuted it in his glosses on al-Mutawwal) i.e., and likewise in Sharh al-Miftah and others. Its explanation: When the judgment of the consequent is qualified by a time or another qualifier, its truth is by the realization of its judgment in that time or qualifier, and its falsity is by its absence in it or with it. So if you say: 'Strike Zayd on Friday' or 'standing', then for its truth it is necessary that your striking of him and the realization of that qualifier with it are both realized. So if you do not strike him or you strike him on other than Friday or in other than the standing state, it is false. So if you say: 'If Zayd strikes me, I strike him', if its meaning were 'I strike him at the time of his striking me', it would not be true unless the striking from you with that qualifier is realized. Then if we suppose...
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the nature of conditional propositions, specifically whether the entire conditional or just the consequent carries the truth value. The author contrasts two perspectives: the logical view (ahl al-mantiq) that the entire conditional expresses a judgment of implication (luzum) between the condition (shart) and the consequent (jaza'), and the grammatical view (ahl al-'arabiyya) that only the consequent is the declarative statement (khabar) and the condition is a mere qualifier (qayd). The logician interprets 'If you come, I honor you' as a judgment of implication of honor for coming; the grammarian interprets it as a promise of honor qualified by the time of coming. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani refutes the grammatical view and shows that it agrees with the logical view, because conditionals are true even when the parts are false if the linking (rabt) matches reality. He cites Qur'anic examples from Yusuf and al-Anbiya'. The marginal note adds that the entire discussion pertains to affirmative conditionals; negatives are opposite. Then the commentary paraphrases al-Sa'd's original claim and al-Sayyid's refutation. The explanation emphasizes that in the logical view, both parts are divested of truth-value because the judgment is of implication. The final part expands on why the grammatical interpretation fails: if the consequent were temporally qualified, the conditional would be false in cases where the implication holds but the consequent is not realized at that specific time, which is not the case. This lesson helps students understand the precise meaning of conditionals in logic and how they differ from ordinary language usage.

Key Terms
الخَبَر
الجزاء
الشّرط
اللّزوم
الرَّبط

Key Takeaway
The key insight is that conditional propositions express a relation of implication (luzum) or linking (rabt), which is what both logicians and grammarians ultimately intend.

Rule for Negative Conditionals
Rule for negative conditionals: truth-value reversal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that negative conditionals are true precisely when their affirmative counterparts are false, using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Negatives flip truth: true where affirmatives are false.

This lesson centers on a concise but critical rule for negative conditional propositions. The author clarifies that the previous discussions of conditional truth-conditions applied only to affirmatives. For negatives—both conjunctive and disjunctive—the rule reverses: a negative conditional is true exactly when the corresponding affirmative is false, and false when the affirmative is true. Mastering this rule is essential for correctly evaluating compound propositions in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the domain
Note that the rule specifically addresses negative conditionals (sawālib) as opposed to affirmative conditionals (mawjibāt). The previous discussion applies only to affirmatives.
2
Understand the reversal
The rule states: negatives are true where affirmatives are false, and false where affirmatives are true. This is a complete opposite truth-condition relation.
3
Apply to a concrete example
Consider the affirmative conditional: 'If the sale is valid, then the contract is binding.' If in a particular case this statement is false (e.g., the sale is valid but the contract is not binding), then the negative 'It is not the case that if the sale is valid, then the contract is binding' is true.
4
Generalize to both types of conditionals
The rule applies to both muttasila (conjunctive) and munfasila (disjunctive) conditionals. The reversal holds regardless of the specific type of connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر كلُّه خاص بالموجبات، وأمَّا السَّوالب في المتّصلات والمنفصلات فعلى العكس؛ أي : تصدق فيما تكذب فيه الموجبات وتكذب فيما تصدق فيه، والله تعالى أعلم.
All that was mentioned is specific to affirmatives. As for negatives in conditionals (muttasilat and munfasilat), it is the opposite; i.e., they are true where affirmatives are false, and false where affirmatives are true. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage introduces a brief but important rule for negative conditional propositions. The author clarifies that the previous discussion about the truth-conditions of conditionals (specifically the compatibility of logician and grammarian views) applies only to affirmative conditionals (mawjibāt). For negative conditionals (sawālib)—both conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila)—the truth-conditions are reversed: a negative conditional is true precisely when the corresponding affirmative conditional is false, and false when the affirmative is true. For example, if the affirmative 'If Zayd comes, Amr leaves' is false because the connection does not hold, then the negative 'It is not the case that if Zayd comes, Amr leaves' would be true. Conversely, if the affirmative is true, the negative is false. This rule is fundamental for understanding logical negation in compound propositions.

Key Terms
السوالب
الموجبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) True
B) False
C) Undetermined
D) Depends on whether Zayd comes

Key Takeaway
The truth-conditions of negative conditionals are the exact opposite of their affirmative counterparts—true where the affirmative is false, false where it is true.

Two Interpretations of Conditional Propositions
Author presents competing interpretations of conditionals by logicians and grammarians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logician's view of a conditional as a single judgment of implication and the grammarian's view as a qualified assertoric statement.

Core Idea
Logicians treat the whole conditional as implication; grammarians treat only the consequent as assertoric.

When you read a conditional like 'If you come, I will honor you', how is it judged true or false? Logicians and grammarians have different answers. This lesson clarifies their distinct perspectives, setting the stage for a reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties and their objects of analysis
The logicians (ahl al-manṭiq) and grammarians (ahl al-ʿarabiyya) analyze the same conditional sentence but focus on different aspects. Note that both are interpreting the same linguistic form.
The passage attributes the logician view to al-Saʿd al-Taftazānī.
2
Extract the logician's interpretation
According to al-Saʿd, logicians see the whole conditional as a single khabar (assertoric statement) where both antecedent and consequent are stripped of independent truth-value. The judgment is about implication (luzūm) of consequent by antecedent. For 'If you come, I honor you', the meaning is a judgment that honor is implied by coming.
In this view, truth depends on whether the implication relation matches reality, not on actual events.
3
Extract the grammarian's interpretation
For grammarians, only the consequent (al-jazā) is the khabar and carries truth-value. The antecedent (shart) is merely a qualifier (qayd) similar to a temporal or circumstantial qualifier. So 'If you come, I honor you' means 'I honor you at the time of your coming' — a qualified assertion about the occurrence of honor.
In this view, truth depends on whether the qualified event (honor-at-time-of-coming) actually occurs.
4
Contrast the two views on the condition of truth
For logicians, truth depends on whether the implication relation matches reality; actual occurrence of events is irrelevant. For grammarians, truth depends on whether the qualified event occurs. This difference sets up the problem that al-Sayyid will resolve.
Reflect on why a conditional like 'If you come, I will honor you' might be considered true even if you never come.

Source Text

لَكِنْ ذَكَرَ السَّعْدُ أَنَّ كَوْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ انْخَلَعَا عَنْ مَعْنَى الخَبَرِيَّةِ، وَصَارَ مَجْمُوعُهُمَا هُوَ الخَبَرُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِاعْتِبَارِ أَهْلِ المَنْطِقِ، وَأَمَّا بِاعْتِبَارِ أَهْلِ العَرَبِيَّةِ فَالخَبَرُ عِنْدَهُمْ هُوَ الجَزَاءُ فَقَط ، وَهُوَ المُحْتَمِلُ لِلصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ. وَأَمَّا الشَّرْطُ فَهُوَ قَيْدٌ لِلخَبَرِ فَقَط ، كَمَا يُقَيَّدُ بِالطَّرْفِ، فَمَعْنَى إِنْ جِئْتَنِي أَكْرَمْتُكَ» عَلَى الاعْتِبَارِ المَنْطِقِيِّ : الحُكْمُ بِلُزُومِ الإِكْرَامِ لِلْمَجِيء، وَعَلَى اعْتِبَارِ أَهْلِ العَرَبِيَّةِ : الحُكْمُ بِحُصُولِ الإِكْرَامِ مِنَ المُتَكَلِّمِ مُقَيَّدٌ بِوَقتِ المَجِيْء؛ كَأَنَّهُ قَالَ: «أَكْرِمُكَ وَقتَ مَجِيْنِكَ». وَرَدَّهُ السَّيِّدُ فِي حَوَاشِي المُطَوَّلِ، وَحَقَّقَ : أَنَّ اعْتِبَارَ أَهْلِ العَرَبِيَّةِ مُوَافِقُ لِاعْتِبَارِ المَنَاطِقَةِ ؛ لِصِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ مَعَ تَخَلُّفِ مَدْلُولِ كُلِّ مِنْ طَرَفِيهَا أَوْ أَحَدِهِمَا إِذَا كَانَ الرَّبْطُ مُوَافِقاً لِلْوَاقِع، وَإِنَّ الرَّبْطَ هُوَ المَقْصُودُ فِي العَرَبِيَّةِ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ؛ نَحْوُ : ﴿إِن كَانَ قَمِيصُهُ قُدَّ مِن قَبْلِ فَصَدَقَتْ ﴾ [يوسف: (٢٦]، ﴿وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا الهَهُ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَنَا) [الأنبياء: ٢٣] . قوله : (الحُكْمُ بِلُزُومِ الإِكْرَامِ لِلْمَجِيْءٍ) أي: فيكون المحكوم عليه هو الشرط، والمحكوم به هو الجزاء، ومفهوم القضيَّة: الحكم بلزوم الجزاء للشَّرط، فتصدقُ القضيَّةُ بمطابقة الحكم باللُّزوم للواقع، وتكذب بعدم ذلك، فكلُّ مِنَ الطَّرفين قدِ انخلع عن معنى الخبرية، واحتمال الصدق والكذب . قوله : (وَرَدَّهُ السَّيِّدُ فِي حَوَاشِي المُطَوَّلِ) أي : وكذا في شرح المفتاح وغيرهما . وبيانُه : أَنَّ الجزاء إذا قيد حكمه بزمن أو قيد آخر، كان صدقه بتحقق حكمه في ذلك الزمان، ذلك القيد وكذبه بعدمه فيه أو معه، فإذا قلت: «اضرب زيداً مع يوم الجمعة، أو : قائماً»، فلا بد في صدقه تحقق من ضربك إيَّاه، وتحقق ذلك القيد معه، فإذا لم تضربه أو ضربته في غير يوم الجمعة، أو في غير حال القيام كان كاذباً ، فإذا قلت : إن ضربني زيد ضربته»، فلو كان معناه: ضربته وقت ضربه إيَّاي» لم يكن صادقاً ؛ إلَّا إذا تحقق الضَّرب منك مع ذلك القيد، فإذا فرض انتفاء القيد ؛ أعني : وقت ضربه إيَّاك لم يكن الضَّرب المقيَّد به واقعاً، فيكون الخبرُ الدَّالُّ على وقوعه كاذباً ؛ سواء وجد منك الضَّرب في غير ذلك الوقت، أم لم يوجد، وذلك باطل قطعاً؛ لأنَّه إذا لم يضربك ولم تضربه وكنت بحيث إن ضربك ضربته عدَّ كلامك هذا صادقاً عرفاً ولغة. وكذا إن قلت : إن جئتني، جئتني أكرمتك»، لم يشتبه على من له أدنى تمييز أنَّ هذا الكلام قد يكون صادقاً، وإن لم يوجد شيءٌ مِنَ المجيء والإكرام أصلاً، ولو كان أكرمتك كلاماً خبريا قيّد بقيد، لوجب أن يكون صدقه بتحقق الإكرام له مع ما قيد به ؛ إذ صدق الخبر بتحقق مضمونه مقيّداً أو مطلقاً ؛ ألا ترى أنَّ قولك : سأكرمك راكباً» لا يُتصوّر صدقه إلَّا بتحقق الإكرام مع قيد الركوب . فظهر أنَّ الحكم الإخباري متعلّق بارتباط أحدِ الطَّرفين بالآخر، لا بالنسبة بين أجزاء الجزاء، وأنَّ ما ذهب إليه الميزانيون لا يخالف كلام أهل العربيَّة، كيف وهم بصدد بيان مفهومات القضايا المستعملة في العرف، وقد صرّح النحويون بأنَّ كلم المجازات تدلُّ على سببيَّة الأَوَّل ومسببيَّة الثَّاني، وفيه إشارة إلى أنَّ المقصود هو الارتباط بين الشَّرط والجزاء.
But al-Sa'd mentioned that the two terms in the conditional (muttasila) are stripped of the meaning of assertoric predication, and their combination becomes the assertoric statement according to the logicians. As for the grammarians, the assertoric statement with them is only the consequent, and it is what admits truth and falsehood. The antecedent is merely a qualifier for the statement, just as it is qualified by a term. Thus the meaning of 'If you come to me, I will honor you' according to the logical consideration is: the judgment of implication of honor by coming. According to the grammarians' consideration: the judgment of the occurrence of honor from the speaker qualified by the time of coming, as if he said: 'I honor you at the time of your coming.' al-Sayyid refuted him in his glosses on al-Mutawwal, and established that the grammarians' consideration is compatible with that of the logicians, because the conditional is true even if the denotation of each of its terms or one of them is lacking, as long as the connection (rabt) matches reality. And the connection is what is intended in Arabic in conditionals, such as: 'If his shirt is torn from the front, then she has told the truth' (Yusuf 26) and 'If there were in them gods other than Allah, they would have been corrupted' (Anbiya 23). (Commentary:) The statement 'the judgment of implication of honor by coming' means: the subject of judgment is the antecedent, and the predicate is the consequent, and the concept of the proposition is the judgment of the entailment of the consequent by the antecedent. Thus the proposition is true if the judgment of entailment matches reality, and false otherwise. So each of the two terms is stripped of the meaning of assertoric predication and the possibility of truth and falsehood. (Commentary:) The statement 'al-Sayyid refuted him in his glosses on al-Mutawwal' means: and likewise in Sharh al-Miftah and others. Its explanation: if the consequent's judgment is qualified by time or another qualifier, its truth is by the realization of its judgment in that time and that qualifier, and its falsehood by its non-existence in it or with it. If you say 'Strike Zayd on Friday' or 'standing', then for its truth it is necessary that your striking him occurs and that the qualifier occurs with it. If you do not strike him, or strike him on a day other than Friday, or not while standing, it is false. If you say 'If Zayd strikes me, I strike him', if its meaning were 'I strike him at the time of his striking me', it would not be true unless your striking occurs with that qualifier. If the qualifier is absent—i.e., the time of his striking you—then the striking qualified by it does not occur, so the statement indicating its occurrence would be false, whether your striking occurred at another time or not. This is clearly false, because if he did not strike you and you did not strike him, but you are such that if he struck you you would strike him, this statement is considered true by custom and language. Similarly, if you say 'If you come to me, I will honor you'—it is not ambiguous to anyone with the slightest discernment that this statement may be true even if nothing of the coming or honoring occurred at all. If 'I honor you' were an assertoric statement qualified by a qualifier, its truth would require the realization of the honor together with what it is qualified by, because the truth of a statement is by the realization of its content, whether qualified or absolute. Do you not see that your saying 'I will honor you riding' is not conceivable as true except with the realization of honor together with the qualifier of riding. Thus it is evident that the assertoric judgment is related to the connection of one of the terms with the other, not to the relation between parts of the consequent, and that what the logicians held does not contradict the words of the grammarians, especially since they are concerned with explaining the concepts of propositions used in custom. The grammarians explicitly state that the particles of conditionals indicate the causality of the first and the effect of the second, and in that is an indication that the intention is the connection between the antecedent and the consequent.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical reconciliation between two competing interpretations of conditional propositions: that of the logicians (ahl al-manṭiq) and that of the grammarians (ahl al-ʿarabiyya). The author begins by presenting al-Saʿd al-Taftazānī's claim that according to logicians, the whole conditional (qadiyya shartiyya) is a single assertoric statement (khabar) where both antecedent (sharṭ) and consequent (jazā) are stripped of independent truth-values, while according to grammarians, only the consequent is the assertoric statement, and the antecedent is merely a qualifier (qayd) on it. al-Saʿd illustrates this with the example 'If you come to me, I will honor you': for logicians, it means a judgment of implication (luzūm) of honor by coming; for grammarians, it means a qualified judgment of occurrence of honor at the time of coming. The author then reports that al-Sayyid al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī refuted al-Saʿd in his glosses on al-Mutawwal, establishing that the two views are actually compatible. The key argument is that a conditional can be true even when neither the antecedent nor the consequent is realized, as long as the connection (rabṭ) between them corresponds to reality. This is supported by Qurʾānic examples: the conditional about Yuusuf's shirt and the verse about multiple gods. The author then provides a detailed commentary (sharḥ) on the logicians' view, clarifying that the judgment is about entailment, not about the actual occurrence of the parts. He then expands on al-Sayyid's reconciliation with an example: if you say 'If Zayd strikes me, I strike him', and Zayd never strikes you and you never strike him, the statement is still considered true by custom and language because the connection is preserved. This shows that the assertoric judgment is about the connection between antecedent and consequent, not about the parts of the consequent. The author concludes that the logicians' view does not contradict the grammarians', since grammarians also intend the connection when they describe conditionals.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sayyid's reconciliation, when is a conditional proposition 'If P then Q' true?
A) When both P and Q are actually true.
B) When the consequent Q occurs in time.
C) When the connection (rabṭ) between P and Q matches reality, regardless of whether P or Q occur.
D) When the antecedent P is true.

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition is true when the connection between its antecedent and consequent corresponds to reality, regardless of whether the parts themselves occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Qurʾānic conditional 'If there were in them gods other than Allah, they would have been corrupted' (al-Anbiya 22). Explain why this conditional is true according to al-Sayyid's reconciliation, even though there are not actually multiple gods.
?
Examine a conditional statement from usul al-fiqh: 'If the prayer is valid, then the purification is valid.' Identify whether logicians or grammarians would interpret this differently and why.

Propositions

Al-Sayyid's Reconciliation: The Connection (Rabt) as the Core
Author provides detailed reconciliation with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the conditional 'If you come, I will honor you' can be true even if neither coming nor honoring occurs, based on the concept of rabt.

Core Idea
The truth of a conditional lies in the fit between the connection and reality, not in the actual events.

In the previous lesson, we saw the two interpretations. Now al-Sayyid deepens the reconciliation with a concrete example: 'If you come, I will honor you.' This example shows that the conditional can be true even when nothing happens, as long as the implication holds.

Guided Path
1
Recall the grammarian's interpretation as a qualified assertion
Grammarians interpret 'If you come, I will honor you' as 'I will honor you at the time of your coming' – a qualified statement about honor at a specific time. Truth would require that the qualified event (honor-at-time-of-coming) actually occur.
In this view, the antecedent is a condition on the occurrence of the consequent.
2
Identify the problem: the conditional can be true without occurrence
If the grammarian's view were strictly correct, the conditional could not be true if neither coming nor honor occurs. But custom and language hold it true if the connection holds. For example, if someone says 'If you come, I will honor you' and you never come, we still consider the statement true if the speaker would have honored you had you come.
This shows that truth does not depend on actual events.
3
Analyze al-Sayyid's key example: 'If Zayd strikes me, I strike him'
This conditional is considered true even if Zayd never strikes, because the speaker would retaliate if struck. The truth depends on the disposition to retaliate, i.e., the connection (rabt). If the meaning were 'I strike him at the time of his striking me', it would be false if no striking occurs, which is absurd.
Al-Sayyid uses this to show that the connection, not the qualified event, is what matters.
4
Generalize to the role of rabt
The rabt (connection) is the relation of necessary implication (luzum). Whether the events occur is irrelevant to the truth of the conditional. This aligns with the logicians' view that the whole proposition is about implication.
The conditional is about the link, not the actuality of the individual parts.
5
Verify with Qur'anic examples
The verses about Yusuf (shirt torn from front implies truth) and about multiple gods (if multiple gods existed, corruption would follow) are conditionals whose truth depends solely on the connection matching reality, not on the actual states of affairs.
These examples show that the Qur'an uses conditionals to assert relationships, not to report events.

بِاعْتِبَارِ أَهْلِ العَرَبِيَّةِ فَالخَبَرُ عِنْدَهُمْ هُوَ الجَزَاءُ فَقَط ، وَهُوَ المُحْتَمِلُ لِلصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ. وَأَمَّا الشَّرْطُ فَهُوَ قَيْدٌ لِلخَبَرِ فَقَط ، كَمَا يُقَيَّدُ الحكم باللُّزوم للواقع، وتكذب بعدم ذلك، فكلُّ مِنَ الطَّرفين قدِ انخلع عن معنى الخبرية، واحتمال الصدق والكذب . قوله : (وَرَدَّهُ السَّيِّدُ فِي حَوَاشِي المُطَوَّلِ) أي : وكذا في شرح المفتاح وغيرهما . إيَّاه، وتحقق ذلك القيد معه، فإذا لم تضربه أو ضربته في غير يوم الجمعة، أو في غير حال القيام كان كاذباً ، فإذا قلت : إن ضربني زيد ضربته»، فلو كان معناه: ضربته وقت ضربه إيَّاي» لم يكن صادقاً ؛ إلَّا إذا تحقق الضَّرب منك مع ذلك القيد، فإذا فرض انتفاء القيد ؛ أعني : وقت ضربه إيَّاك لم يكن الضَّرب المقيَّد به واقعاً، فيكون الخبرُ الدَّالُّ على وقوعه كاذباً ؛ سواء وجد منك الضَّرب في غير ذلك الوقت، أم لم يوجد، وذلك باطل قطعاً؛ لأنَّه إذا لم يضربك ولم تضربه وكنت بحيث إن ضربك ضربته عدَّ كلامك هذا صادقاً عرفاً ولغة. وكذا إن قلت : إن جئتني، جئتني أكرمتك»، لم يشتبه على من له أدنى تمييز أنَّ هذا الكلام قد يكون صادقاً، وإن لم يوجد شيءٌ مِنَ المجيء والإكرام أصلاً، ولو كان أكرمتك كلاماً خبريا قيّد بقيد، لوجب أن يكون صدقه بتحقق الإكرام له مع ما قيد به ؛ إذ صدق الخبر بتحقق مضمونه مقيّداً أو مطلقاً ؛ ألا ترى أنَّ قولك : سأكرمك راكباً» لا يُتصوّر صدقه إلَّا بتحقق الإكرام مع قيد الركوب . فظهر أنَّ الحكم الإخباري متعلّق بارتباط أحدِ الطَّرفين بالآخر، لا بالنسبة بين أجزاء الجزاء، وأنَّ ما ذهب إليه الميزانيون لا يخالف كلام أهل العربيَّة، كيف وهم بصدد بيان مفهومات القضايا المستعملة في العرف، وقد صرّح النحويون بأنَّ كلم المجازات تدلُّ على سببيَّة الأَوَّل ومسببيَّة الثَّاني، وفيه إشارة إلى أنَّ المقصود هو الارتباط بين الشَّرط والجزاء.
But al-Sa'd mentioned that the two terms in the conditional (muttasila) are stripped of the meaning of assertoric predication, and their combination becomes the assertoric statement according to the logicians. As for the grammarians, the assertoric statement with them is only the consequent, and it is what admits truth and falsehood. The antecedent is merely a qualifier for the statement, just as it is qualified by a term. Thus the meaning of 'If you come to me, I will honor you' according to the logical consideration is: the judgment of implication of honor by coming. According to the grammarians' consideration: the judgment of the occurrence of honor from the speaker qualified by the time of coming, as if he said: 'I honor you at the time of your coming.' al-Sayyid refuted him in his glosses on al-Mutawwal, and established that the grammarians' consideration is compatible with that of the logicians, because the conditional is true even if the denotation of each of its terms or one of them is lacking, as long as the connection (rabt) matches reality. And the connection is what is intended in Arabic in conditionals, such as: 'If his shirt is torn from the front, then she has told the truth' (Yusuf 26) and 'If there were in them gods other than Allah, they would have been corrupted' (Anbiya 23). (Commentary:) The statement 'the judgment of implication of honor by coming' means: the subject of judgment is the antecedent, and the predicate is the consequent, and the concept of the proposition is the judgment of the entailment of the consequent by the antecedent. Thus the proposition is true if the judgment of entailment matches reality, and false otherwise. So each of the two terms is stripped of the meaning of assertoric predication and the possibility of truth and falsehood. (Commentary:) The statement 'al-Sayyid refuted him in his glosses on al-Mutawwal' means: and likewise in Sharh al-Miftah and others. Its explanation: if the consequent's judgment is qualified by time or another qualifier, its truth is by the realization of its judgment in that time and that qualifier, and its falsehood by its non-existence in it or with it. If you say 'Strike Zayd on Friday' or 'standing', then for its truth it is necessary that your striking him occurs and that the qualifier occurs with it. If you do not strike him, or strike him on a day other than Friday, or not while standing, it is false. If you say 'If Zayd strikes me, I strike him', if its meaning were 'I strike him at the time of his striking me', it would not be true unless your striking occurs with that qualifier. If the qualifier is absent—i.e., the time of his striking you—then the striking qualified by it does not occur, so the statement indicating its occurrence would be false, whether your striking occurred at another time or not. This is clearly false, because if he did not strike you and you did not strike him, but you are such that if he struck you you would strike him, this statement is considered true by custom and language. Similarly, if you say 'If you come to me, I will honor you'—it is not ambiguous to anyone with the slightest discernment that this statement may be true even if nothing of the coming or honoring occurred at all. If 'I honor you' were an assertoric statement qualified by a qualifier, its truth would require the realization of the honor together with what it is qualified by, because the truth of a statement is by the realization of its content, whether qualified or absolute. Do you not see that your saying 'I will honor you riding' is not conceivable as true except with the realization of honor together with the qualifier of riding. Thus it is evident that the assertoric judgment is related to the connection of one of the terms with the other, not to the relation between parts of the consequent, and that what the logicians held does not contradict the words of the grammarians, especially since they are concerned with explaining the concepts of propositions used in custom. The grammarians explicitly state that the particles of conditionals indicate the causality of the first and the effect of the second, and in that is an indication that the intention is the connection between the antecedent and the consequent.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical reconciliation between two competing interpretations of conditional propositions: that of the logicians (ahl al-manṭiq) and that of the grammarians (ahl al-ʿarabiyya). The author begins by presenting al-Saʿd al-Taftazānī's claim that according to logicians, the whole conditional (qadiyya shartiyya) is a single assertoric statement (khabar) where both antecedent (sharṭ) and consequent (jazā) are stripped of independent truth-values, while according to grammarians, only the consequent is the assertoric statement, and the antecedent is merely a qualifier (qayd) on it. al-Saʿd illustrates this with the example 'If you come to me, I will honor you': for logicians, it means a judgment of implication (luzūm) of honor by coming; for grammarians, it means a qualified judgment of occurrence of honor at the time of coming. The author then reports that al-Sayyid al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī refuted al-Saʿd in his glosses on al-Mutawwal, establishing that the two views are actually compatible. The key argument is that a conditional can be true even when neither the antecedent nor the consequent is realized, as long as the connection (rabṭ) between them corresponds to reality. This is supported by Qurʾānic examples: the conditional about Yuusuf's shirt and the verse about multiple gods. The author then provides a detailed commentary (sharḥ) on the logicians' view, clarifying that the judgment is about entailment, not about the actual occurrence of the parts. He then expands on al-Sayyid's reconciliation with an example: if you say 'If Zayd strikes me, I strike him', and Zayd never strikes you and you never strike him, the statement is still considered true by custom and language because the connection is preserved. This shows that the assertoric judgment is about the connection between antecedent and consequent, not about the parts of the consequent. The author concludes that the logicians' view does not contradict the grammarians', since grammarians also intend the connection when they describe conditionals.

Key Terms
المتصلة
الجزاء
الربط
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the conditional 'If you come, I will honor you' can be true even when:
A) You come but I don't honor you
B) You don't come but I honor you
C) Neither coming nor honoring occurs, as long as the implication holds
D) Only if both coming and honoring occur

Key Takeaway
Conditionals are true when the connection between antecedent and consequent holds, regardless of the occurrence of the events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional statement of your own, like 'If I study, I will succeed.' Then evaluate: Is it true if you never study but would have succeeded if you did? Explain using the concept of rabt.
?
Look at the Qur'anic conditional 'And if We will, We could have raised a warner in every village' (al-Furqan 51). Why is this conditional true despite no warner being raised? Use al-Sayyid's reasoning.

Two Usages of Conditionals in Logic and Arabic
Muhaqqiq presents two usages of conditionals in Arabic: as restricted news (al-Sa'd) and as connection (al-Sayyid).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two usages of conditional statements described by al-Sa'd and al-Sayyid al-Sharif, and identify which is more common.

Core Idea
Conditionals function either as restricted news or as statements of implication; the latter is more frequent.

The passage introduces a key distinction in how conditionals work in Arabic. Some conditionals report a fact restricted by a condition; others state a necessary connection. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, as it affects how conditionals are used in proofs and definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional examples
Find the two conditional sentences in the Arabic text: one about sunrise and giving a dirham, another about being human and animal.
The first example is from everyday speech, the second from logical implication.
2
Understand al-Sa'd's view
Al-Sa'd holds that in 'If the sun rises, I give you a dirham', the apodosis 'I give you a dirham' is a piece of news restricted (muqayyad) by the condition.
This treats the conditional as a restricted report.
3
Understand al-Sayyid's view
Al-Sayyid holds that in 'If this is a human, it is an animal', the conditional expresses a connection (irtibat) between the antecedent and consequent, not a restricted report.
This treats the conditional as a logical implication.
4
Note frequency
The muhaqqiq states that al-Sayyid's usage (connection) occurs more often in Arabic.
5
Recognize the commentator's acceptance
The commentator does not reject this distinction; he only rejects its forced application to the juristic dispute.
The two usages are both legitimate and serve different functions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَجَابَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ : بِأَنَّ كُلَّا مِنَ الِاسْتِعْمَالَيْنِ وَارِدٌ فِي العَرَبِيَّةِ، فَنَحْوُ : إِذَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ أُعْطِيكَ دِرْهَماً جَارٍ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرَهُ السَّعْدُ مِنَ الإِخْبَارِ بِمَدْلُولِ الجَزَاءِ مُقَيَّداً بِطُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ، وَنَحْوُ : «إِذَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَانَا جَارٍ عَلَى مَا لِلسَّيِّدِ؛ لَّا أَنَّ الاِسْتِعْمَالَ الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ السَّيِّدُ أَكْثَرُ وُقُوعاً، وَالكَلَامُ فِي المَسْأَلَةِ طَوِيلُ الذَّيْلِ، فَلْيُنْظَرْ مَحَلِّهِ، وَاللَّهُ تَعَالَى الْمُوَفِّقُ . وَمَا ذَكَرَهُ مِنْ بِنَاءِ خِلَافِ الْإِمَامَيْنِ عَلَى تِلْكَ التَّفْرِقَةِ لَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَّعَسُّفِ، وَأَنَّهُ لَا وَجْهَ لَهُ، وَالظَّاهِرُ أَنَّ الْخِلَافَ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى أَنَّهُ هَلْ يُشْتَرَطُ مِلْكُ الْعِصْمَةِ حَالَ التَّعْلِيقِ وَعَلَيْهِ الشَّافِعِيُّ، أَوْ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ وَعَلَيْهِ الْحَنَفِيُّ، وَاللَّهُ تَعَالَى أَعْلَمُ.
And some investigators responded: that each of the two usages occurs in Arabic. So expressions like 'If the sun rises, I give you a dirham' runs on what al-Sa'd mentioned, of informing about the meaning of the apodosis restricted by the sunrise. And expressions like 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' runs on what al-Sayyid [holds] of connection; except that the usage that al-Sayyid mentioned occurs more frequently. And the discussion of the issue is long-tailed, so let it be seen in its place, and God Almighty is the Granter of success. And what he mentioned of building the disagreement of the two imams on that distinction, it is not hidden how forced it is, and that it has no basis. The apparent [truth] is that the disagreement is built on whether ownership of the marital bond is required at the time of the conditional pronouncement – al-Shafi'i is upon that – or it is not required – the Hanafi is upon that. And God Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of an earlier claim that linked a logical-grammatical distinction to a juristic disagreement between Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i. The investigator (muhaqqiq) first identifies two usages of conditional sentences in Arabic: one where the apodosis is a piece of news restricted by the condition (e.g., 'If the sun rises, I give you a dirham') – this follows al-Sa'd's analysis; the other where the conditional expresses a connection (irtibat) between two propositions, conveying that whenever the antecedent holds, the consequent follows (e.g., 'If this is a human, it is an animal') – this follows al-Sayyid al-Sharif's analysis. The muhaqqiq notes that the latter usage is more common, but the matter is extensive. Then the commentator (likely Bannani) directly critiques the attempt to ground the Hanafi/Shafi'i difference on these two usages. He states it is forced and baseless; the real basis is simply whether ownership of the marital bond (milk al-'isma) is required at the time of pronouncing the conditional divorce. This is a straightforward juristic point, not rooted in logical theory.

Key Terms
الشرطية
الجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the muhaqqiq, which usage of conditionals occurs more frequently in Arabic?
A) The usage described by al-Sa'd (restricted news).
B) The usage described by al-Sayyid (connection).
C) Both are equally common.
D) The muhaqqiq does not state a preference.

Key Takeaway
Conditionals can function as restricted news or as statements of implication; recognizing the type is essential for correct logical and juristic analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional: 'If someone prays without purity, the prayer is invalid.' Does this fit al-Sa'd's analysis (restricted news) or al-Sayyid's (connection)? Explain why and how the analysis affects the fiqh ruling.
?

Critiquing a Forced Link: Logic vs. Juristic Dispute
Commentator critiques the forced linkage of logical usages to a juristic dispute, offering a fiqh-based alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a forced ('ta'assuf') linkage between a logical distinction and a juristic disagreement, and state the correct basis of the Hanafi/Shafi'i dispute on conditional divorce.

Core Idea
The Hanafi/Shafi'i disagreement on conditional divorce is not about logical usages but about the requirement of milk al-'isma at the time of pronouncement.

The commentator firmly rejects an attempt to base the Hanafi/Shafi'i difference on the two usages of conditionals. He calls it forced and baseless. Instead, he identifies the real juristic principle: whether ownership of the marital bond (milk al-'isma) must be present when the conditional divorce is pronounced. This lesson shows how to distinguish between logical analysis and independent juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's criticism
Identify the sentence where the commentator dismisses the linkage as 'forced' (ta'assuf) and 'having no face' (la wajh lahu).
This is a strong rejection, indicating logical analysis is being misapplied.
2
Identify the forced link
The forced link is that the Hanafi/Shafi'i dispute over conditional divorce is based on the distinction between the two usages of conditionals (restricted news vs. connection).
3
Extract the commentator's alternative basis
The commentator states the true basis: whether milk al-'isma is required at the time of the conditional pronouncement (ta'liq).
This is a purely juristic point, not a logical one.
4
Contrast logical and juristic reasoning
The two usages of conditionals are about semantics and logic; the juristic dispute is about legal conditions for divorce. They are independent domains.
Conflating them is a category error.
5
Apply the lesson to other contexts
Recognize that critiques of forced linkages (qiyas ma'a al-fariq) are common in usul al-fiqh where logical distinctions are imported inappropriately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَجَابَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ : بِأَنَّ كُلَّا مِنَ الِاسْتِعْمَالَيْنِ وَارِدٌ فِي العَرَبِيَّةِ، فَنَحْوُ : إِذَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ أُعْطِيكَ دِرْهَماً جَارٍ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرَهُ السَّعْدُ مِنَ الإِخْبَارِ بِمَدْلُولِ الجَزَاءِ مُقَيَّداً بِطُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ، وَنَحْوُ : «إِذَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَانَا جَارٍ عَلَى مَا لِلسَّيِّدِ؛ لَّا أَنَّ الاِسْتِعْمَالَ الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ السَّيِّدُ أَكْثَرُ وُقُوعاً، وَالكَلَامُ فِي المَسْأَلَةِ طَوِيلُ الذَّيْلِ، فَلْيُنْظَرْ مَحَلِّهِ، وَاللَّهُ تَعَالَى الْمُوَفِّقُ . وَمَا ذَكَرَهُ مِنْ بِنَاءِ خِلَافِ الْإِمَامَيْنِ عَلَى تِلْكَ التَّفْرِقَةِ لَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَّعَسُّفِ، وَأَنَّهُ لَا وَجْهَ لَهُ، وَالظَّاهِرُ أَنَّ الْخِلَافَ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى أَنَّهُ هَلْ يُشْتَرَطُ مِلْكُ الْعِصْمَةِ حَالَ التَّعْلِيقِ وَعَلَيْهِ الشَّافِعِيُّ، أَوْ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ وَعَلَيْهِ الْحَنَفِيُّ، وَاللَّهُ تَعَالَى أَعْلَمُ.

So expressions like 'If the sun rises, I give you a dirham' runs on what al-Sa'd mentioned, of informing about the meaning of the apodosis restricted by the sunrise. Hashya Critique: All Conditionals Express Connection
Hashya writer argues against earlier muhaqqiq's distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the hashya writer's critique of the muhaqqiq's distinction and apply his uniform interpretation of conditionals to examples from Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Every conditional, even factual ones, is about logical dependence, not mere time.

In this passage, a hashya writer challenges a distinction made by earlier investigators (muhaqqiqun) who divided conditionals into temporal and connective types. The writer insists that all conditionals uniformly express a relation of dependence (irtibat) between protasis and apodosis. By examining this critique, you will learn how to identify and defend a unified analysis of conditional statements, a key skill in both logic and Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Distinction Under Attack
Find the two types of conditionals that the muhaqqiq distinguished: one allegedly temporal (like 'I give you a dirham at sunrise') and one connective (like 'If the sun rises, I give you a dirham' as per al-Sayyid). The hashya writer rejects this split.
The muhaqqiq's distinction is not explicitly stated in the passage but is inferred from the hashya writer's objection.
2
Extract the Objection's Basis
The writer gives three reasons: (1) no evidence (لا دليل) for the distinction, (2) no explicit example from al-Sa'd to support it, and (3) the conditional particle 'idha' is inherently conditional, not temporal.
The particle 'idha' contains the meaning of condition (shart), which suspends the apodosis on the protasis.
3
Understand the Uniform Reinterpretation
The writer reinterprets 'If the sun rises, I give you a dirham' as expressing connection: the giving is conditioned on the sunrise, which is a confirmed event. The contrast with a pure temporal statement ('at the time of sunrise') shows the conditional adds dependence.
The writer uses the difference between conditional and temporal phrasing to support his point.
4
Apply the Uniform Approach to a New Example
Test the writer's claim on a conditional from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'If you are in a state of janaba, then perform ghusl.' Show that it is best interpreted as a conditional connection (obligation depends on the condition) rather than a mere temporal report.
This example comes from Quran 5:6 and is discussed in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَأَجَابَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ) قد نقل ياسين هذا التفصيل في «حاشية مختصر السَّعد» وفيه نظر؛ إذ لا دليل على هذا التَّفصيل، ولم يوجد مثال صريح فيما قاله السعد، ولا يحتمل أن يكون وارداً على ما قاله السَّيِّد، فليحمل الباب كله على نهج واحد، فنحو: «إذا طلعت الشمس أعطيك درهماً جارٍ أيضاً على ما للسَّيِّد مِنَ الارتباط، والمقصود به : تحقيق إعطاء الدرهم لتعليقه على ما هو محقق، وهو طلوع الشَّمس، ويدلُّك على صحة ما ذكرناه ما نجده من الفرق بينه وبين قولك: «أعطيك درهماً وقت طلوع الشمس». وأمَّا إن إذا معمولة لما في جوابها من فعل وشبهه، فلا يقتضي أن تكون كسائر القيود مِنَ الظرفية وغيرها، كيف وهي متضمنة لمعنى الشَّرط، ولا معنى للشَّرط إلَّا تعليق حصول مضمون جملة على حصول مضمون أخرى، فكلتا الجملتين جزء كلام لا محالة.
His statement: '(And some investigators responded)' – Yaseen has transmitted this distinction in his Hashiya on Mukhtasar al-Sa'd, but it is questionable, for there is no evidence for this distinction, and no explicit example is found in what al-Sa'd said, nor is it plausible that it applies to what al-Sayyid said. So the entire chapter should be carried on a single path. So expressions like 'If the sun rises, I give you a dirham' also run on what al-Sayyid [holds] of connection; and the intention with it is to confirm the giving of the dirham by conditioning it on something confirmed, namely the rising of the sun. And what supports the correctness of what we have mentioned is the difference we find between it and your saying 'I give you a dirham at the time of sunrise'. As for 'idha', it is governed by a verb or verb-like element in its response, so it does not necessitate that it be like other adverbial or other restrictions, especially since it contains the meaning of condition; and the meaning of condition is nothing but the suspending of the occurrence of the content of one sentence on the occurrence of the content of another. So both sentences are unquestionably parts of a single utterance.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage is a hashya (super-commentary) that refines the earlier muhaqqiq's distinction between two types of conditionals. The writer argues that the distinction lacks evidence and is not supported by al-Sa'd's own examples. Instead, all conditional sentences should be interpreted uniformly on the model of al-Sayyid, i.e., as expressing a connection (irtibat) between the antecedent and consequent. Even the example 'If the sun rises, I give you a dirham' – which the muhaqqiq had assigned to al-Sa'd's analysis – is reinterpreted: the intention is to confirm the gift by tying it to a certain event, not to report a restricted event. The writer emphasizes that the conditional particle 'idha' is inherently conditional, not merely adverbial, because it suspends the occurrence of the response on the occurrence of the condition. Therefore, both the condition and the apodosis are integral parts of the utterance. This critique effectively collapses the two usages into one: every conditional asserts a relation of dependence.

C) The muhaqqiq's examples are grammatically incorrect.
D) The muhaqqiq ignored the role of 'idha' as a particle.
