How Conditionals Are Particularized: Makhsusa and Ghayr Makhsusa
Division of conditionals into particular and non-particular

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a particular conditional from a non-particular one and explain the basis of its particularity in both connected and disconnected forms.

Core Idea
Conditionals become particular when their implication or opposition is tied to a specific time or circumstance.

The author introduces a crucial division of conditional propositions: some are particular (makhsusa) and some are not. This mirrors the division of categoricals, but the basis of particularity is different. Understanding this distinction prevents us from treating conditional statements as universally applicable when they are actually context-restricted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Recognize that conditionals are either connected (mutasila) or disconnected (munfasila). Connected conditionals express implication (luzum); disconnected conditionals express opposition ('inad).
2
Recall the basis of particularity in categoricals
In categorical propositions, particularity (khusus) is based on the subject being particular (e.g., 'Zayd' or 'some humans').
3
Apply the analogy to conditionals
For a connected conditional, particularity means the implication (luzum) holds only in a specific state or time. For a disconnected conditional, particularity means the opposition ('inad) holds only in a specific state or time.
Example: 'If it is morning, the sun has risen' is particular because the implication is restricted to that time.
4
Test with a fiqh example
Consider 'If a person is travelling, he may shorten the prayer.' This is a particular connected conditional because the permission to shorten is restricted to the state of travel. Without particularity, the ruling would apply universally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ : اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ مُتَّصِلَةٌ أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ تَنْقَسِمُ أَوَّلاً إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : مَحْصُوصَةٍ في وَغَيْرِهَا . قوله : (الثَّالِثُ : اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ مُتَّصِلَةٌ أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ تَنْقَسِمُ أَشار بهذا التنبيه إلى بيان أنَّ أقسام الشَّرطيَّة كأقسام الحمليَّة، فتكون مخصوصة كما تكون الحمليّة مخصوصة، وإن كان خصوص الحملية بكون موضوعها جزئياً، وخصوص الشَّرطيَّة بأن يخصَّ اللُّزوم في المتصلة، والعناد في المنفصلة بحالة أو زمان معيَّن وعليه : فما كان ينبغي للناظم إغفال هذا التقسيم، وقد أشار شيخنا سيدي حمدون في «خريدته» بقوله :
The third [section]: Know that the conditional, connected or disconnected, divides firstly into two divisions: particular and non-particular. His statement: (The third: Know that the conditional, connected or disconnected, divides...) He indicated by this note to clarify that the divisions of the conditional are like the divisions of the categorical. Thus it is particularized just as the categorical is particularized, except that the particularity of the categorical is by its subject being particular, and the particularity of the conditional is by specifying the implication (luzum) in the connected and the opposition ('inad) in the disconnected to a specific state or time. Therefore, the poet should not have omitted this division, and our master Sayyid Hamdun indicated it in his 'Kharida' by saying:
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage introduces the third section of the work, which deals with the division of conditional propositions into particular (makhsusa) and non-particular. The main text briefly states that both connected (mutasila) and disconnected (munfasila) conditionals are divided in this way. The hashya then expands on this: the commentator notes that the purpose of this section is to show that the divisions of conditionals mirror those of categoricals (hamliyya). Just as a categorical can be particularized by having a particular subject (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'), a conditional can be particularized by restricting the implication (luzum) in a connected conditional or the opposition ('inad) in a disconnected conditional to a specific circumstance (hal) or time (zaman). For example, the conditional 'If it is morning, the sun has risen' is particular because the implication holds only at that time. The commentator then notes a critique: the original poet (al-nazim) of the Sullam should not have omitted this important division, and he points out that his teacher Hamdun did mention it in his work 'al-Kharida'. This illustrates how later commentators fill gaps in the matn.

Conditional propositions: restriction and quantification
Author classifies conditional propositions into restricted/unrestricted and then into universal/particular/indefinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any conditional proposition as restricted or unrestricted and determine its quantification (universal, particular, or indefinite).

Core Idea
A conditional's universality is about the generality of the entailment, not the universality of its parts.

We now move to a systematic classification of conditional propositions. Just as categorical propositions are divided by subject quantifier, conditionals are divided by whether the entailment or incompatibility is tied to a specific time or state, and then by how general that relation is across all possible situations. This distinction is crucial for understanding how conditionals function in Kalam and Fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
First, determine if it is conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila) or disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila). The rest of the classification applies to both.
2
Check for restriction
Is the entailment (luzum) or incompatibility (inad) restricted to a specific time (e.g., 'today') or state (e.g., 'while riding')? If yes, it is makhsusa; if not, it is ghayr makhsusa.
The author warns that 'restricted' here does not mean the same as in categorical propositions.
3
Determine quantification
For both restricted and unrestricted conditionals, decide if the entailment holds for all possible situations consistent with the antecedent (kulliyya), for some situations (juz'iyya), or is unspecified (muhmala).
Universality is about the judgment, not the antecedent or consequent.
4
Apply the rule with an example
Consider 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal.' This is universal because the entailment (animality from humanity) holds for every possible situation (e.g., Zayd laughing, sitting, etc.). The antecedent 'Zayd is a human' is not universal, but the judgment is.
Compare with 'If Zayd is a human, he is an animal' – this is indefinite (muhmala) because the scope is unspecified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳) - الثَّالِثُ : اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ مُتَّصِلَةٌ أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ تَنْقَسِمُ أَوَّلاً إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : مَخْصُوصَةٍ وَغَيْرِهَا .
فَالمَخْصُوصَةُ هِيَ : «الَّتِي قُيِّدَ لُزُومُهَا أَوْ عِنَادُهَا بِزَمَنٍ مُعَيَّنٍ ، أَوْ حَالَةٍ مُعَيَّنَةٍ ؛ نَحْوُ : اليَوْمَ أَوْ رَاكِباً أَكْرَمْتُهُ، وَنَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الإِنْسَانُ وَهُوَ مُكَلَّفٌ جَاءَنِي أَحَدٌ اليَوْمَ أَوْ وَقْتَ تَكْلِيفِهِ مُطِيعاً أَوْ عَاصِياً».
وَغَيْرُ المَخْصُوصَةِ هِيَ : «الَّتِي لَا يَتَقَيَّدُ لُزُومُهَا أَوْ عِنَادُهَا بِمَا ذُكِرَ».
وَلَمَّا كَانَتِ المَخْصُوصَةُ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ لَيْسَتْ بِمَعْنَى المَخْصُوصَةِ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّةِ، صَحْ أَنْ تَقْبَلَ مِنَ الأَقْسَامِ مَا لَا تَقْبَلُهُ المَخْصُوصَةُ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّةِ ، فَتَنْقَسِمُ كُلٌّ مِنَ المَخْصُوصَةِ وَغَيْرِ المَخْصُوصَةِ إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَام : كُلِّيَّةٍ، وَجُزْئِيَّةٍ، وَمُهْمَلَةٍ .
وَهَذِهِ الأَقْسَامُ لَاحِقَةٌ لَهَا بِحَسَبِ تَعْمِيمِ اللُّزُومِ وَالعِنَادِ، وَعَدَم تَعْمِيمِهِمَا، فَهَذِهِ سِتَّةُ أَقْسَامٍ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، وَمِثْلُهَا فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ، وَكُلٌّ مِنْهَا : إِمَّا مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ .

وَإِنْ تَكُنْ شَرْطِيَّةٌ مَخْصُوصَه بِحَالَةٍ أَوْ زَمَن مَخصُوصه أي : تسمى بذلك .

قوله : (وَهَذِهِ الْأَقْسَامُ لَاحِقَةٌ لَهَا بِحَسَبِ تَعْمِيمِ اللُّزُومِ ... إلخ) أي : تعميم اللُّزوم والعناد في جميع الأوضاع الممكنة إن كانت موجبة، وتعميم سلب لزومها أو عنادها في جميع تلك الأحوال إن كانت سالبة.
فمعنى كلِّيَّة اللُّزوميَّة المتّصلة: إثبات لزوم تاليها لمقدَّمها ، أو سلبه عن جميع الأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدَّم، فإذا قلنا : كلَّما كان زيد إنساناً، كان حيواناً أردنا : أنَّ لزوم حيوانية زيد لإنسانيَّته ثابت مع كلِّ وضع يمكن أن يجامع إنسانيته من كونه ضاحكاً أو كاتباً، قاعداً أو قائماً، كائناً في أي زمان وفي أي مكان.
فكلِّيَّةُ الشَّرطيَّة ليست لأجل أنَّ مقدَّمها أو تاليها كلّيّ، بل كلّيَّتها بحسب الحكم الذي فيها، وإن كان مقدَّمُها وتاليها شخصيين، كما أنَّ كلّيّة الحملية ليست بحسب كلّيّة الموضوع أو المحمول، بل باعتبار كلية الحكم.
قوله : (فَهَذِهِ سِتَّةُ أَقْسَامٍ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ) :
مثال المخصوصة الكلَّيَّة : كلَّما جئتني راكباً أكرمتك، والجزئية : قد يكون إذا جئتني راكباً أكرمتك، والمهملة نحو : إن جئتني راكباً أكرمتك .
(3) The third [point]: Know that the conditional, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, is first divided into two divisions: restricted and unrestricted. The restricted is: that whose entailment or incompatibility is restricted to a specific time or a specific state; for example: 'Today' or 'while riding, I honored him', and like: 'Either the human while he is responsible, someone came to me today or at the time of his responsibility, obedient or disobedient.' And the unrestricted is: that whose entailment or incompatibility is not restricted by what was mentioned. And since the restricted conditional is not in the same sense as the restricted categorical, it is valid for it to accept among the divisions what the restricted categorical does not accept. So each of the restricted and unrestricted divides into three divisions: universal, particular, and indefinite. And these divisions apply to them according to the generalization of entailment and incompatibility and the non-generalization of them. So these are six divisions in the conjunctive, and the like in the disjunctive. And each of them is either affirmative or negative. And if there is a conditional restricted to a state or a specific time, it is called that. His saying: (And these divisions apply to them according to the generalization of entailment...) i.e., the generalization of entailment and incompatibility over all possible situations if the proposition is affirmative, and the generalization of the negation of its entailment or incompatibility over all those states if it is negative. So the meaning of universality of the conjunctive entailment proposition is: the affirmation of the entailment of its consequent from its antecedent, or its negation, over all possible situations that can coexist with the antecedent. When we say: 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal', we mean that the entailment of Zayd's animality from his humanity is affirmed with every situation that can accompany his humanity, whether he is laughing or writing, sitting or standing, being in any time and any place. So the universality of the conditional is not because its antecedent or consequent is universal; rather its universality is according to the judgment in it, even if its antecedent and consequent are particular, just as the universality of the categorical is not according to the universality of the subject or predicate, but according to the universality of the judgment. His saying: (So these are six divisions in the conjunctive): Example of the restricted universal: 'Whenever you come to me riding, I honor you'; and the particular: 'Sometimes when you come to me riding, I honor you'; and the indefinite: 'If you come to me riding, I honor you.'
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of conditional propositions (shartiyya). The author begins stating the third point: conditional propositions, whether conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila), are first divided into two categories: restricted (makhsusa) and unrestricted (ghayr makhsusa). The restricted conditional is one where the entailment (luzum) or incompatibility (inad) is tied to a specific time or state. Examples are given: 'Today I honored him' (with 'today' as time restriction) or 'while riding I honored him' (with state restriction) for conditional propositions, and a disjunctive example: 'Either the human while he is responsible, someone came to me today or at the time of his responsibility, obedient or disobedient.' The unrestricted is the opposite: entailment or incompatibility is not restricted. The author then notes an important point: the restricted conditional is not the same as the restricted categorical (hamliyya). In categorical propositions, restriction pertains to the subject being particular; in conditional propositions, restriction pertains to the conditional relation. Therefore, the restricted conditional accepts further divisions that the restricted categorical does not. Specifically, both the restricted and unrestricted conditionals can be further divided into three types: universal (kulliyya), particular (juz'iyya), and indefinite (muhmala). These divisions are based on whether the entailment or incompatibility is universalized over all possible situations or not. Thus, there are six divisions in the conjunctive (three for restricted, three for unrestricted) and likewise for the disjunctive. Each of these can be affirmative or negative. Then a gloss explains the definition: 'his saying: (And these divisions apply...) means the generalization of entailment and incompatibility over all possible situations if affirmative, and the generalization of the negation of entailment or incompatibility over all those states if negative.' The author elaborates with an example: 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal' – the entailment holds in every possible situation that can accompany Zayd's humanity, e.g., whether he is laughing, writing, etc. Thus, the universality of the conditional is not based on the universality of its parts (the antecedent or consequent may be particular) but on the universality of the judgment. Similarly, the universality of a categorical proposition is not based on the universality of its subject or predicate but on the universality of the judgment. Finally, examples are given: the restricted universal: 'Whenever you come to me riding, I honor you'; the restricted particular: 'Sometimes when you come to me riding, I honor you'; the restricted indefinite: 'If you come to me riding, I honor you.'

غَيْر المَخْصُوصَة

Key Takeaway
Conditionals are classified by restriction (time/state) and by the generality of the entailment judgment, not by the universality of their parts.

Quantifiers for Conditional Propositions
Bannani enumerates quantifiers for conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier type (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, indefinite) for any given conjunctive or disjunctive conditional proposition from an Islamic science example.

Core Idea
Conditionals have dedicated quantifiers: kullamā for universal conjunctive, dā'iman for universal disjunctive, and specific particles for negation and particularity.

Just as categorical propositions have quantifiers (kull, ba'd), conditional propositions also require quantifiers to indicate universality or particularity. Al-Bannānī, in his commentary on al-Sullam, fills a gap left by the author by systematically listing the quantifiers for conjunctive (sharṭiyya muttaṣila) and disjunctive (sharṭiyya munfaṣila) conditionals. This lesson will equip you to recognize and use these quantifiers in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
First, determine whether the proposition is conjunctive (using 'if...then' or similar) or disjunctive (using 'either...or'). The quantifier system differs: for conjunctives, the universal affirmative is 'kullamā'; for disjunctives, it is 'dā'iman'.
Conjunctives indicate companionship (ṣuḥba); disjunctives indicate opposition ('inād).
2
Find the quantifier word or its absence
Look for words like 'kullamā', 'mahmā', 'dā'iman', 'laysa al-battah', 'qad yakūn', 'qad lā yakūn', 'laysa kullamā', 'laysa dā'iman', or simply the bare tools 'in', 'idhā', 'law' (for conjunctives) or 'immā' (for disjunctives).
The absence of a quantifier indicates the indefinite (muhmala) form.
3
Determine quantity and quality
Use the following mapping: 'kullamā/mahmā' = universal affirmative conjunctive; 'dā'iman' = universal affirmative disjunctive; 'laysa al-battah' = universal negative both; 'qad yakūn' = particular affirmative both; 'qad lā yakūn' = particular negative both; 'laysa kullamā' = particular negative conjunctive only; 'laysa dā'iman' = particular negative disjunctive only; no quantifier = indefinite.
The universal affirmative means the relation (companionship or opposition) holds in all cases; particular means it holds in some cases; negative indicates the relation does not hold.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take an example from the text: 'qad yakūn' (particular affirmative) in 'qad yakūn idhā kāna al-insānu mu'minan najā min 'adhābi al-qabr' (It may be that if the person is a believer, he is saved from the punishment of the grave). This is a particular conjunctive conditional. Verify that it affirms companionship in some cases, not all.
The examples are drawn from Kalam and theology, reinforcing the relevance to Islamic sciences.

باب في القضايا وأحكامها. لهي أربع وعشرون. والمعتبر في إيجاب الشرطية وسلبها هو ما حكم به فيها من الصحبة بين الطرفين : أو العناد؛ سواء كان الطرفان إيجابيين، أو سلبين، أو مختلفين. وسور الإيجاب الكلي في المتصلة: «كلما ومهما» [قصارة] وغير المخصوصة الكلية؛ نحو: «كلما جئتني أكرمتك» بحذف القيد، والجزئية: «قد يكون ... إلخ»، والمهملة : «إن جئتني أكرمتك». والمنفصلات المخصوصة الكلية ؛ نحو: «دائماً إما أن تكون وأنت حي عالماً أو جاهلاً»، والجزئية: «قد يكون إما أن تكون وأنت حي عالماً أو جاهلاً»، والمهملة؛ نحو: «إما أن تكون وأنت حي عالماً أو جاهلاً». وغير المخصوصة كلية ; نحو: «دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو فرداً»، وجزئية : «قد يكون إما أن يكون العدد ... إلخ»، ومهملة نحو: «إما أن يكون العدد». قوله: (فهي أربع وعشرون) هذا من غير اعتبار اللزوم والاتفاق، وإن اعتبر كانت ثمانية وأربعين، وإن اعتبرت في المنفصلة اعتبار منع الجمع والخلو، أو أحدهما زادت على ذلك. قوله: (سواء كان الطرفان إيجابيين) أي: فتكون موجبة عن إيجابيين ولا إشكال؛ نحو: «كلما كانت الشمس طالعة، فالنهار موجود»، وتكون موجبة عن سالبتين ; نحو: «كلما لم يكن الشيء حيواناً، لم يكن ناطقاً»، وتكون سالبة عن موجبتين; نحو: «ليس البتة إذا كان الشيء حيواناً، كان حجراً». قوله: (وسور الإيجاب الكلي) لما لم يتعرض الناظم رحمه الله تعالى لأسوار الشرطية، أشار لها الشارح هنا في هذا التنبيه، وكان من حق الناظم التعرض لها كما تعرض لأسوار الحملية، وقد أشار لسور المتصلة شيخنا سيدي حمدون بقوله: والسور: «كلما» و: «ليس كلما» وقد يكون، وشبيه علما. ولِسور المنفصلة بقوله: والسور: «دائماً» و «ليس البتة» وقد يكون، وشبيه نلته. وقد تكون: «وما في معناهما، نحو: «كلما كان الموجود جائزاً كان حادثاً، و: «مهما كان الشيء غنياً عن الفاعل كان قديماً»، و «متى كان حادثاً كان مفتقراً إلى محدث». وسور الإيجاب الكلي في المنفصلة: «دائماً»، وما في معناه؛ نحو: «دائماً : إما أن يكون الموجود قديماً، وإما أن يكون حادثاً». وسور السلب الكلي في المتصلة وفي المنفصلة معاً: «ليس البتة»; نحو: «ليس البتة إذا كان الموجود حادثاً، كان غنياً عن الفاعل، ونحو: «ليس البتة : إما أن يكون الشيء قديماً، وإما أن يكون غنياً عن الفاعل». وسور الإيجاب الجزئي لهما معاً: «قد يكون»; كقولنا في المتصلة: «قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان مؤمناً نجا من عذاب القبر، وفي المنفصلة: «قد يكون : إما أن يكون المرء مطيعاً أو عاصياً». وسور السلب الجزئي لهما معاً: «قد لا يكون»، وللمتصلة فقط: «ليس كلما»، وللمنفصلة فقط: «ليس دائماً»; تقول في المتصلة: «قد لا يكون إذا كان مؤمناً نجا من عذاب القبر، و: «ليس كلما كان مؤمناً نجا من عذاب القبر»، وفي المنفصلة: «قد لا يكون : إما أن يكون الإنسان مطيعاً، وإما أن يكون عاصياً»، و: «ليس دائماً : إما أن يكون الإنسان مطيعاً، وإما أن يكون عاصياً». وإهمال الشرطية: أن تجرد أداة الاتصال، وهي: «لو، وإن، وإذا»، وأداة الانفصال لفظة «إما» من كل ما يدل على تعميم أو تبعيض ; نحو: «إن كان حيواناً كان إنساناً». قوله: (وما في معناهما) أي: من كل لفظ دال على عموم اللزوم في الأزمنة والأوضاع والأحوال كـ: «متى». قوله: «دائماً، وما في معناه» أي: من كل لفظ دال على عموم العناد للأزمنة والأوضاع والأحوال; نحو: «أبداً إما أن يكون الجسم حيواناً أو جماداً». قوله: (وللمتصلة فقط: «ليس كلما») وذلك أنك إذا قلت: كلما كان كذا، كان كذا، فهو إيجاب كلي، فإذا قلت: ليس كلما كان معناه: رفع الإيجاب الكلي لا محالة، وإذا ارتفع الإيجاب الكلي تحقق السلب الجزئي.
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. They are twenty-four. The consideration in the affirmation of a conditional and its negation is what is judged therein of companionship between the two parts or opposition; whether the two parts are affirmative, negative, or different. The universal affirmative quantifier in the conjunctive: 'kullamā' and 'mahmā' [shortened?], and the unrestricted universal: like 'Whenever you come to me, I honor you' by dropping the restriction; the particular: 'qad yakūn... etc.'; the indefinite: 'If you come to me, I honor you.' For the specific universal disjunctives: like 'Always: either you are, while you are alive, knowledgeable or ignorant'; the particular: 'It may be that either you are, while alive, knowledgeable or ignorant'; the indefinite: like 'Either you are, while alive, knowledgeable or ignorant.' The unrestricted universal: like 'Always: either the number is even or odd'; the particular: 'It may be that either the number is... etc.'; the indefinite: like 'Either the number is...' His saying: (They are twenty-four) without considering entailment and coincidence; if considered, they become forty-eight; and if considered in the disjunctive considering mutual exclusion and non-vacancy, or one of them, they increase beyond that. His saying: (Whether the two parts are affirmative) i.e., it becomes affirmative from two affirmatives without problem; like: 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists'; and it becomes affirmative from two negatives; like: 'Whenever a thing is not an animal, it is not rational'; and it becomes negative from two affirmatives; like: 'Not at all: if a thing is an animal, it is a stone.' His saying: (And the universal affirmative quantifier) Since the author did not address the quantifiers of conditionals, the commentator pointed to them in this note; and it was the author's right to address them as he addressed the quantifiers of categoricals. Our master Sayyidī Ḥamdūn has indicated the quantifiers for the conjunctive by his saying: The quantifier: 'kullamā' and 'laysa kullamā', and 'qad yakūn', and its like are known. And for the quantifiers of the disjunctive by his saying: The quantifier: 'dā'iman' and 'laysa al-battah', and 'qad yakūn', and its like we have. And 'what is in their meaning', like: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it is originated', and: 'Whatever thing is independent of the agent, it is eternal', and: 'Whenever it is originated, it is in need of an originator.' The universal affirmative quantifier in the disjunctive: 'dā'iman', and what is in its meaning; like: 'Always: either the existent is eternal, or it is originated.' The universal negative quantifier in both the conjunctive and disjunctive: 'laysa al-battah'; like: 'Not at all: if the existent is originated, it is independent of the agent', and like: 'Not at all: either the thing is eternal, or it is independent of the agent.' The particular affirmative quantifier for both: 'qad yakūn'; like our saying in the conjunctive: 'It may be that if the person is a believer, he is saved from the punishment of the grave'; and in the disjunctive: 'It may be: either the person is obedient or disobedient.' The particular negative quantifier for both: 'qad lā yakūn'; and for the conjunctive only: 'laysa kullamā'; and for the disjunctive only: 'laysa dā'iman'; you say in the conjunctive: 'It may not be that if he is a believer, he is saved from the punishment of the grave', and: 'Not every time he is a believer, he is saved from the punishment of the grave'; and in the disjunctive: 'It may not be: either the person is obedient or disobedient', and: 'Not always: either the person is obedient or disobedient.' The indefinite of the conditional: that the tool of connection be stripped, which is 'law', 'in', and 'idhā', and the tool of disjunction, the word 'immā', from everything that indicates generalization or particularization; like: 'If it is an animal, it is a human.' His saying: (And what is in their meaning) i.e., from every word that indicates the generality of entailment in times, situations, and states, like 'matā'. His saying: ('dā'iman', and what is in its meaning) i.e., from every word that indicates the generality of opposition in times, situations, and states; like: 'Ever: either the body is an animal or an inanimate object.' His saying: (And for the conjunctive only: 'laysa kullamā') That is because when you say: 'Every time it is such, it is such', it is a universal affirmative; so when you say: 'Not every time', its meaning is the removal of the universal affirmative without doubt, and when the universal affirmative is removed, the particular negative is realized.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage from al-Bannānī's commentary on al-Sullam al-Munawraq defines the quantifiers (sūr) for conditional propositions (sharṭiyyāt). It begins by stating that there are twenty-four types of propositions (a total from categorical and conditional). The author then explains that in conditionals, the truth condition is based on the relation between the two parts: either companionship (ṣuḥba) for conjunctives or opposition ('inād) for disjunctives, regardless of whether the parts themselves are affirmative or negative. Next, the passage lists the universal affirmative quantifier for conjunctives: 'kullamā' (whenever) and 'mahmā' (whatever), and their synonyms, with examples like 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' It then distinguishes between specific and unrestricted (ghayr makhṣūṣa) forms, where the quantifier is explicit or implied. For disjunctives, the universal affirmative quantifier is 'dā'iman' (always) plus 'immā...aw' (either...or). The passage then covers universal negative ('laysa al-battah'), particular affirmative ('qad yakūn'), particular negative ('qad lā yakūn' and 'laysa kullamā' for conjunctives, 'laysa dā'iman' for disjunctives), and the indefinite form (where the quantifier is dropped, e.g., 'in' for conjunctives and 'immā' for disjunctives without universal particles). The commentary (qāla) clarifies that the author (al-Sullam) did not originally include quantifiers for conditionals, so al-Bannānī adds them, citing his teacher Sayyidī Ḥamdūn's verses. The passage explains that each quantifier indicates generality of entailment or opposition across times, situations, and states. It also clarifies that 'laysa kullamā' in conjunctives is equivalent to a particular negative, since it negates the universal affirmative. All examples are rooted in Islamic sciences: existential claims about the sun and day, animal and rational, believer and salvation, etc.

Propositions

7 min
Universal Quantifiers for Conditionals
Universal quantifiers for conditionals and disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the universal affirmative and universal negative quantifiers in both conditional and disjunctive propositions.

Core Idea
Universal quantifiers for conditionals assert that the link holds or fails without exception.

In this passage, the author specifies the quantifiers (sūr) for conditional propositions. For universal statements, the conditional uses 'kullamā' and the disjunctive uses 'dā'imān' for affirmative; both share 'laysa al-battata' for negative. These tools are essential for formulating precise theological arguments about necessity and contingency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the statement is a conditional (muttasila) with 'if-then' or a disjunctive (munfasila) with 'either-or'.
The connector 'law', 'in', or 'idhā' signals muttasila; 'immā' signals munfasila.
2
Recognize universal affirmative quantifiers
For muttasila, the quantifier is 'kullamā' (whenever). For munfasila, it is 'dā'imān' (always). Look for these words before the connective.
Example from kalam: 'Kullamā kāna al-mawjūdu jā'izan kāna ḥādithan' (Whenever the existent is contingent, it is originated).
3
Recognize universal negative quantifiers
Both types share the quantifier 'laysa al-battata' (absolutely not). It negates the entire conditional or disjunctive assertion.
Example: 'Laysa al-battata idhā kāna al-mawjūdu ḥādithan kāna ghaniyyan 'an al-fā'il' (Absolutely not: if the existent is originated, then it is independent of the agent).
4
Test with a new example
Given a statement, determine its quantifier and whether it is universal affirmative or negative.
Practice: 'Kullamā kāna al-insānu mu'minan najā min 'adhābi al-qabri' is universal affirmative conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا، نَحْوُ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ الْمَوْجُودُ جَائِزًا كَانَ حَادِثًا، وَ: «مَهُمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ غَنِيًّا عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ كَانَ قَدِيمًا»، وَمَتَى كَانَ حَادِثًا كَانَ مُفْتَقِرًا إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ. وَسُورُ الْإِيجَابِ الْكُلِّيِّ فِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ: «دَائِمًا»، وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُ؛ نَحْوُ: «دَائِمًا: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَوْجُودُ قَدِيمًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَادِثًا». وَسُورُ السَّلْبِ الْكُلِّيِّ فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ مَعًا: «لَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ»؛ نَحْوُ: «لَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَوْجُودُ حَادِثًا، كَانَ غَنِيًّا عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ»، وَنَحْوُ: «لَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ قَدِيمًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ غَنِيًّا عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ». وَسُورُ الْإِيجَابِ الْجُزْئِيِّ لَهُمَا مَعًا: «قَدْ يَكُونُ»؛ كَقَوْلِنَا فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ: «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ الْإِنْسَانُ مُؤْمِنًا نَجَا مِنْ عَذَابِ الْقَبْرِ»، وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ: «قَدْ يَكُونُ: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَرْءُ مُطِيعًا أَوْ عَاصِيًا». وَسُورُ السَّلْبِ الْجُزْئِيِّ لَهُمَا مَعًا: «قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ»، وَلِلْمُتَّصِلَةِ فَقَطْ: «لَيْسَ كُلَّمَا»، وَلِلْمُنْفَصِلَةِ فَقَطْ: «لَيْسَ دَائِمًا»؛ تَقُولُ فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ: قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا نَجَا مِنْ عَذَابِ الْقَبْرِ، وَ: «لَيْسَ كُلَّمَا كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا نَجَا مِنْ عَذَابِ الْقَبْرِ»، وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ: «قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْإِنْسَانُ مُطِيعًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ عَاصِيًا»، وَ: «لَيْسَ دَائِمًا: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْإِنْسَانُ مُطِيعًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ عَاصِيًا». وَإِهْمَالُ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ: أَنْ تُجَرَّدَ أَدَاةُ الِاتِّصَالِ، وَهِيَ لَوْ، وَإِنْ، وَإِذَا»، وَأَدَاةُ الِانْفِصَالِ لَفْظَةُ «إِمَّا» مِنْ كُلِّ مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمٍ أَوْ تَبْعِيضٍ؛ نَحْوُ: «إِنْ كَانَ حَيَوَانًا كَانَ إِنْسَانًا»، وَ: «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَيَوَانًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرَسًا». وَالْمُهْمَلَةُ فِي قُوَّةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ.
And what is in their meaning, such as: 'Whenever the existent is contingent, it is originated,' and: 'Whenever a thing is independent of the agent, it is eternal,' and: 'Whenever it is originated, it is in need of an originator.' And the universal affirmative quantifier for the disjunctive: 'always,' and what is in its meaning; such as: 'Always: either the existent is eternal or it is originated.' And the universal negative quantifier for both the conditional and the disjunctive: 'absolutely not'; such as: 'Absolutely not: if the existent is originated, then it is independent of the agent,' and such as: 'Absolutely not: either the thing is eternal or it is independent of the agent.' And the particular affirmative quantifier for both: 'sometimes'; as our saying in the conditional: 'Sometimes: if a person is a believer, he is saved from the punishment of the grave,' and in the disjunctive: 'Sometimes: either the man is obedient or disobedient.' And the particular negative quantifier for both: 'sometimes not,' and for the conditional only: 'not whenever,' and for the disjunctive only: 'not always'; you say in the conditional: 'sometimes not: if he is a believer, he is saved from the punishment of the grave,' and: 'not whenever he is a believer is he saved from the punishment of the grave,' and in the disjunctive: 'sometimes not: either the man is obedient or disobedient,' and: 'not always: either the man is obedient or disobedient.' And absoluteness of the conditional: that the connector of the conditional, which is 'if, 'if' (law, in, idhā), and the connector of the disjunctive, the word 'either', are stripped of every indicator of generality or particularity; such as: 'If it is an animal, it is a human,' and: 'Either it is an animal or a horse.' And the absolute [proposition] has the force of the particular.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage is a classificatory and definitional expansion from a commentary on al-Sullam. The author systematically lists the quantifiers (sūr) for conditional propositions—both the conditional (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). He covers four logical quantifiers: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. For each, he provides example propositions drawn from kalam arguments: for example, 'Whenever the existent is contingent, it is originated' illustrates universal affirmative for the conditional, and 'Always: either the existent is eternal or originated' does the same for the disjunctive. He also notes special quantifiers: 'not whenever' is unique to the conditional, and 'not always' to the disjunctive. Finally, he explains 'absoluteness' (ihmāl): when the conditional connectors (law, in, idhā for muttasila; immā for munfasila) are used without any quantifier, the proposition is called 'absolute' (muhmala) and has the logical force of a particular proposition. The examples given—'If it is an animal, it is a human' and 'Either it is an animal or a horse'—are deliberately false but serve to illustrate the logical form stripped of truth conditions. The overall aim is to equip the student with the precise quantificational tools needed to analyze conditional statements in theological and legal reasoning.

دَائِمًا
لَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ

Universal quantifiers in conditionals—'kullamā' for affirmative conditional, 'dā'imān' for affirmative disjunctive, and 'laysa al-battata' for negative in both—enable precise universal claims in theological reasoning.

Construct a universal affirmative conditional proposition using an Islamic example (e.g., about obligation and reward). Write it in Arabic with the correct quantifier.

Identify the quantifier in the following statement from fiqh: 'Laysa al-battata idhā kāna al-mā'u najisan kāna ṭāhiran.' Is it universal negative? For which type of proposition?

Particular Quantifiers for Conditionals
Particular quantifiers for conditionals and disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply particular affirmative and negative quantifiers in conditional and disjunctive propositions, including their special forms.

Core Idea
Particular quantifiers indicate that the conditional or disjunctive link holds or fails in some instances.

The passage continues with particular quantifiers. For both types, 'qad yakūn' marks particular affirmative and 'qad lā yakūn' marks particular negative. However, each type also has a unique quantifier: 'laysa kullamā' for conditionals and 'laysa dā'imān' for disjunctives. These distinctions are crucial for expressing partial exceptions in theological and legal statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particular affirmative quantifier
Both muttasila and munfasila use 'qad yakūn' (sometimes) before the proposition. It indicates the link holds in at least one case.
Example: 'Qad yakūn idhā kāna al-insānu mu'minan najā min 'adhābi al-qabri' (Sometimes if a person is a believer, he is saved from punishment of the grave).
2
Identify the particular negative quantifier
The common quantifier is 'qad lā yakūn' (sometimes not). Additionally, for muttasila only: 'laysa kullamā' (not whenever); for munfasila only: 'laysa dā'imān' (not always).
Example for muttasila: 'Laysa kullamā kāna mu'minan najā min 'adhābi al-qabri' (Not whenever he is a believer is he saved...).
3
Distinguish common and exclusive quantifiers
Note that 'qad lā yakūn' works for both, but 'laysa kullamā' is exclusive to conditionals and 'laysa dā'imān' to disjunctives.
This specialization shows the logical structure differs slightly between the two proposition types.
4
Test with a new example
Given a statement, decide if it is particular affirmative or negative, and if exclusive quantifiers apply.
Practice: 'Qad lā yakūn: immā an yakūna al-insānu muṭī'an aw 'āṣiyan' is particular negative disjunctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُمَا، نَحْوُ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ الْمَوْجُودُ جَائِزًا كَانَ حَادِثًا، وَ: «مَهُمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ غَنِيًّا عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ كَانَ قَدِيمًا»، وَمَتَى كَانَ حَادِثًا كَانَ مُفْتَقِرًا إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ. وَسُورُ الْإِيجَابِ الْكُلِّيِّ فِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ: «دَائِمًا»، وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُ؛ نَحْوُ: «دَائِمًا: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَوْجُودُ قَدِيمًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَادِثًا». وَسُورُ السَّلْبِ الْكُلِّيِّ فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ مَعًا: «لَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ»؛ نَحْوُ: «لَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَوْجُودُ حَادِثًا، كَانَ غَنِيًّا عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ»، وَنَحْوُ: «لَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ قَدِيمًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ غَنِيًّا عَنِ الْفَاعِلِ». وَسُورُ الْإِيجَابِ الْجُزْئِيِّ لَهُمَا مَعًا: «قَدْ يَكُونُ»؛ كَقَوْلِنَا فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ: «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ الْإِنْسَانُ مُؤْمِنًا نَجَا مِنْ عَذَابِ الْقَبْرِ»، وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ: «قَدْ يَكُونُ: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَرْءُ مُطِيعًا أَوْ عَاصِيًا». وَسُورُ السَّلْبِ الْجُزْئِيِّ لَهُمَا مَعًا: «قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ»، وَلِلْمُتَّصِلَةِ فَقَطْ: «لَيْسَ كُلَّمَا»، وَلِلْمُنْفَصِلَةِ فَقَطْ: «لَيْسَ دَائِمًا»؛ تَقُولُ فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ: قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا نَجَا مِنْ عَذَابِ الْقَبْرِ، وَ: «لَيْسَ كُلَّمَا كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا نَجَا مِنْ عَذَابِ الْقَبْرِ»، وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ: «قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْإِنْسَانُ مُطِيعًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ عَاصِيًا»، وَ: «لَيْسَ دَائِمًا: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْإِنْسَانُ مُطِيعًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ عَاصِيًا». وَإِهْمَالُ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ: أَنْ تُجَرَّدَ أَدَاةُ الِاتِّصَالِ، وَهِيَ لَوْ، وَإِنْ، وَإِذَا»، وَأَدَاةُ الِانْفِصَالِ لَفْظَةُ «إِمَّا» مِنْ كُلِّ مَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى تَعْمِيمٍ أَوْ تَبْعِيضٍ؛ نَحْوُ: «إِنْ كَانَ حَيَوَانًا كَانَ إِنْسَانًا»، وَ: «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَيَوَانًا، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرَسًا». وَالْمُهْمَلَةُ فِي قُوَّةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ.
And what is in their meaning, such as: 'Whenever the existent is contingent, it is originated,' and: 'Whenever a thing is independent of the agent, it is eternal,' and: 'Whenever it is originated, it is in need of an originator.' And the universal affirmative quantifier for the disjunctive: 'always,' and what is in its meaning; such as: 'Always: either the existent is eternal or it is originated.' And the universal negative quantifier for both the conditional and the disjunctive: 'absolutely not'; such as: 'Absolutely not: if the existent is originated, then it is independent of the agent,' and such as: 'Absolutely not: either the thing is eternal or it is independent of the agent.' And the particular affirmative quantifier for both: 'sometimes'; as our saying in the conditional: 'Sometimes: if a person is a believer, he is saved from the punishment of the grave,' and in the disjunctive: 'Sometimes: either the man is obedient or disobedient.' And the particular negative quantifier for both: 'sometimes not,' and for the conditional only: 'not whenever,' and for the disjunctive only: 'not always'; you say in the conditional: 'sometimes not: if he is a believer, he is saved from the punishment of the grave,' and: 'not whenever he is a believer is he saved from the punishment of the grave,' and in the disjunctive: 'sometimes not: either the man is obedient or disobedient,' and: 'not always: either the man is obedient or disobedient.' And absoluteness of the conditional: that the connector of the conditional, which is 'if, 'if' (law, in, idhā), and the connector of the disjunctive, the word 'either', are stripped of every indicator of generality or particularity; such as: 'If it is an animal, it is a human,' and: 'Either it is an animal or a horse.' And the absolute [proposition] has the force of the particular.

Commentary
This passage is a classificatory and definitional expansion from a commentary on al-Sullam. The author systematically lists the quantifiers (sūr) for conditional propositions—both the conditional (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). He covers four logical quantifiers: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. For each, he provides example propositions drawn from kalam arguments: for example, 'Whenever the existent is contingent, it is originated' illustrates universal affirmative for the conditional, and 'Always: either the existent is eternal or originated' does the same for the disjunctive. He also notes special quantifiers: 'not whenever' is unique to the conditional, and 'not always' to the disjunctive. Finally, he explains 'absoluteness' (ihmāl): when the conditional connectors (law, in, idhā for muttasila; immā for munfasila) are used without any quantifier, the proposition is called 'absolute' (muhmala) and has the logical force of a particular proposition. The examples given—'If it is an animal, it is a human' and 'Either it is an animal or a horse'—are deliberately false but serve to illustrate the logical form stripped of truth conditions. The overall aim is to equip the student with the precise quantificational tools needed to analyze conditional statements in theological and legal reasoning.

قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ
لَيْسَ كُلَّمَا
لَيْسَ دَائِمًا

Key Takeaway
Particular quantifiers—'qad yakūn' (affirmative) and 'qad lā yakūn' (negative)—are common, but 'laysa kullamā' (conditional only) and 'laysa dā'imān' (disjunctive only) provide nuanced negation.

Absoluteness (Ihmāl) in Conditionals
Definition and logical force of absolute conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize an absolute (muhmala) conditional or disjunctive proposition and explain its logical force as particular.

Core Idea
An absolute conditional lacks a quantifier and functions logically as a particular proposition.

The passage concludes by explaining 'ihmāl' (absoluteness): when the conditional connectors (law, in, idhā for muttasila; immā for munfasila) are used without any quantifier, the proposition is called 'muhmala'. Despite its unqualified appearance, it has the logical force of a particular proposition—meaning it applies in at least one case but not necessarily all. This is a key nuance for interpreting unquantified statements in theological and legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Define 'absolute' (muhmala) proposition
A conditional or disjunctive proposition is absolute when its connector is stripped of any quantifier like 'kullamā', 'dā'imān', or 'qad yakūn'.
Example from passage: 'In kāna ḥayawānan kāna insānan' (If it is an animal, it is a human) – no quantifier.
2
Identify the connector and absence of quantifier
Look for the conditional connectors 'law', 'in', 'idhā' for muttasila, or 'immā' for munfasila, and check there is no preceding quantifier word.
Second example: 'Immā an yakūna ḥayawānan wa-immā an yakūna farasan' (Either it is an animal or a horse) – no quantifier.
3
Understand the logical force
Such absolute propositions are treated as particular (juz'iyya) in logical force, meaning they assert at least one instance but not universal coverage.
The author states: 'وال مهملة في قوة الجزئية' (the absolute (proposition) has the force of the particular).
4
Apply the rule
When encountering an unquantified conditional in a kalam or fiqh text, remember it is not universal but particular unless context demands otherwise.
This prevents overinterpretation of absolute statements as universal.

Kullamā and Mahmā as Conditional Quantifiers
Author clarifies the original and logical use of 'kullamā' and 'mahmā' as quantifiers for conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the original categorical usage of 'kullamā' from its logical conditional usage and identify appropriate examples of each.

Core Idea
Logicians repurpose 'kullamā' and 'mahmā' from general quantifiers into conditional adverbs.

In this note, the commentator explains a crucial terminological shift: words like 'kullamā' and 'mahmā' have a natural-language use as universal quantifiers for categorical propositions, but logicians intentionally repurpose them as conditional connectives. Understanding this shift prevents confusion when analyzing conditional propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two uses of kullamā
First, note that 'kullamā' originally expresses universality over individuals, forming a categorical proposition (e.g., 'Everything in the world is perishable'). Second, it can also be used as a temporal adverb, acquiring adverbial force from the 'mā' that refers to time, thus becoming a conditional connective meaning 'whenever'.
The shift hinges on the adverbial nature of the 'mā' attached to 'kull'.
2
Identify the grammatical mechanism
The passage explains that 'kullamā' acquires ẓarfiyya (adverbiality) from 'mā' that falls on time (al-ḥīn). This turns it into a connective particle that requires a condition (sharṭ) and a response (jawāb).
This is a linguistic transformation, not a logical one—it is how the word functions in Arabic grammar.
3
Analyze the Quranic example
Examine the verse: 'Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it' (5:64). Here, 'kullamā' is not quantifying individuals but linking a repeated condition (kindling fire) to its result (Allah extinguishing it). This illustrates the logical usage.
The Quranic citation serves as an authoritative illustration of the conditional usage.
4
Extend the analysis to mahmā
Similarly, 'mahmā' originally is an indefinite noun for non-rational things ('whatever'), but logicians imbue it with the meaning of condition and use it as an adverb for universal conditionals, equivalent to 'whenever'.
The logical use of 'mahmā' parallels that of 'kullamā' in conditional propositions.

تَنْبِيهٌ: تَقَدَّمَ مِنْ أَسْوَارِ الْمُتَّصِلَةِ: «كُلَّمَا»، وَالْأَصْلُ فِيهَا: أَنْ تَكُونَ لِعُمُومِ الْأَفْرَادِ، فَتَكُونُ الْقَضِيَّةُ مَعَهَا حَمْلِيَّةً، نَحْوُ: «كُلَّمَا هُوَ فِي الدُّنْيَا نَافِذٌ»، وَقَدْ تَكُونُ ظَرْفًا؛ لِاكْتِسَابِهَا الظَّرْفِيَّةَ مِنْ مَا الْوَاقِعَةِ عَلَى الْحِينِ، فَتُسْتَعْمَلُ اسْتِعْمَالَ أَدَوَاتِ الرَّبْطِ، وَتَقْتَضِي شَرْطًا وَجَوَابًا؛ نَحْوُ: ﴿كُلَّمَا أَوْقَدُوا نَارًا لِلْحَرْبِ أَطْفَأَهَا اللَّهُ﴾ [المائدة: 64]، وَهَذَا مُرَادُ الْمُنَاطِقَةِ. وَاسْتَعْمَلُوا أَيْضًا لِلْمُتَّصِلَةِ «مَهْمَا»، وَهِيَ فِي الْأَصْلِ: اسْمٌ مُبْهَمٌ لِغَيْرِ الْعَاقِلِ، أُشْرِبَتْ مَعْنَى الشَّرْطِ؛ نَحْوُ: ﴿وَمَهْمَا تَأْتِنَا بِهِ﴾ [الأعراف: 132] الْآيَةَ، وَأَهْلُ الْمَنْطِقِ يَسْتَعْمِلُونَهَا ظَرْفًا.
Note: Preceded from the quantifiers of the conditional: 'kullamā'. Its origin is that it is for the universality of individuals, so the proposition with it is categorical, such as: 'Everything that is in the world is perishable.' And sometimes it is an adverb, because it acquires adverbiality from 'mā' that falls on time, so it is used like a connective particle, and it requires a condition and a response; such as: 'Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it' [5:64]. And this is what the logicians intend. They also use for the conditional 'mahmā', which originally is an indefinite noun for non-rationals, imbued with the meaning of condition; such as: 'Whatever sign you bring us' [7:132] the verse. And the logicians use it as an adverb.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbīh) from the commentary addressing a previous point about quantifiers of the conditional. The author explains that the word 'kullamā' has two distinct uses. Originally, in language, it expresses generality over individuals (like 'every' in categorical propositions: 'Everything in the world is perishable' is a categorical universal). But logicians (al-munātiqa) use it differently: they take 'kullamā' as a temporal adverb (ẓarf) that functions as a conditional connective, meaning 'whenever' or 'every time'. It acquires this adverbial sense because the 'mā' attached to it refers to time (al-ḥīn). Thus it becomes a particle that joins a condition and a result, as in the Quranic verse 'Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it' (5:64). Likewise, 'mahmā' is discussed: originally it is an indefinite noun for non-rational beings (like 'whatever thing'), but logicians imbue it with the meaning of condition and use it as an adverb for universal conditionals. The purpose of this note is to prevent confusion between the categorical and conditional uses of these words—a crucial distinction for correctly analyzing propositions in logic. The examples from the Quran serve as authoritative illustrations of the logical usage.

Redefining 'Kullama' and 'Mahma' as Conditional Quantifiers
Explanation of quantifier shift for conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical redefinition of 'kullama' and 'mahma' as quantifiers for universal conditional propositions and distinguish their usage from ordinary Arabic.

Core Idea
Logicians repurpose ordinary Arabic words into precise logical quantifiers for conditionals.

In logic, the same word can serve different functions depending on its technical definition. The Arabic terms 'kullama' and 'mahma' originally refer to generality of individuals (categorical) or are ambiguous nouns. However, logicians transform them into temporal adverbs that function as quantifiers for universal conditional propositions. This lesson unpacks that shift, showing how a word's logical meaning can diverge from its ordinary usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ordinary meaning of 'kullama'
In Arabic, 'kullama' originally indicates generality of individuals or serves as a temporal adverb meaning 'whenever'. The passage shows that when used for individuals, the proposition is categorical (e.g., 'Everything in the world is perishing').
2
Recognize the logical redefinition
Logicians adopt 'kullama' as a quantifier for universal conditionals, treating it as a temporal adverb that requires a condition and a response. The Quranic example (al-Ma'idah 64) illustrates this: 'Whenever they kindle a fire for war, Allah extinguishes it.'
The 'ma' in 'kullama' is taken to refer to time, giving it adverbial force.
3
Examine the shift for 'mahma'
Originally, 'mahma' is an ambiguous noun for non-rational beings, imbued with conditional meaning (e.g., 'Whatever you bring us'). Logicians use it as an adverb of time, making it a quantifier for universal conditionals.
Al-Sa'd explains that 'mahma' was transferred from quantifying individuals (categorical) to quantifying times and states (conditional).
4
Contrast with ordinary usage
Note the shift: in ordinary language, these words are not primarily quantifiers for conditionals. The logician's redefinition is a deliberate technical move, criticized by some grammarians, but essential for precise logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنْبِية:
تَقَدَّمَ مِنْ أَسْوَارِ المُتَّصِلَةِ : كُلَّمَا»، وَالأَصْلُ فِيهَا : أَنْ تَكُونَ لِعُمُومِ الأَفْرَادِ، فَتَكُونُ القَضِيَّةُ مَعَهَا حَمْلِيَّةٌ، نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا هُوَ فِي الدُّنْيَا نَافِذ»، وَقَدْ تَكُونُ ظَرْفاً ؛ لِاكْتِسَابِهَا الظَّرْفِيَّةَ مِنْ مَا الوَاقِعَةِ عَلَى الجِيْنِ ، فَتُسْتَعْمَلُ اسْتِعْمَالَ أَدَوَاتِ الرَّبْطِ، وَتَقْتَضِي شَرْطاً وَجَوَاباً ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا أَوْقَدُواْ نَارًا لِلْحَرْبِ أَطْفَاهَا اللهُ ﴾ [المائدة : ٦٤] ، وَهَذَا مُرَادُ المَنَاطِقَةِ .
وَاسْتَعْمَلُوا أَيْضاً لِلْمُتَّصِلَةِ مَهْمَا، وَهِيَ فِي الأَصْلِ: اسْمٌ مُبْهَم لِغَيْرِ العَاقِلِ أُشْرِبَتْ مَعْنَى الشَّرْطِ ؛ نَحْوُ : ﴿مَهمَا تَأْتِنَا بِهِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۳۲] الآيَةَ، وَأَهْلُ المَنْطِقِ يَسْتَعْمِلُونَهَا ظرفاً.
وَقال السعد: «مهما» بحسب اللغة إِنَّما هي لعموم الأفراد حتّى تصلح سوراً للكلِّيَّة الحمليّة، وهم قد نقلوها إلى عموم الأوضاع؛ أي: الأزمان والأحوال وجعلوها سوراً للكلّيَّة المتّصلة. اهـ.
A note: Previously from the quantifiers of the conditional: 'kullama' (whenever), and its original is that it be for the generality of individuals, so the proposition with it becomes categorical, like: 'Everything that is in the world is perishing.' And it may be an adverb of time, because it acquires adverbial force from 'ma' which falls on time, so it is used like connective tools and requires a condition and a response, like: 'Whenever they kindled a fire for war, Allah extinguished it' [al-Ma'idah 64], and this is what the logicians intend. And they also used for the conditional 'mahma' (whatever), and it originally is an ambiguous noun for non-rational beings that has been imbued with the meaning of condition, like: 'Whatever you bring us' [al-A'raf 132] the verse, and the logicians use it as an adverb. Al-Sa'd said: 'Mahma' according to language is only for the generality of individuals until it becomes suitable as a quantifier for universal categorical propositions, and they have transferred it to the generality of situations, i.e., times and states, and made it a quantifier for universal conditionals.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note about the quantifiers used for universal conditional propositions in logic. The author begins by stating that among the quantifiers of the conditional (al-muttaṣila) is 'kullama' (whenever). Originally, 'kullama' indicates generality of individuals, making the proposition categorical (ḥamliyya), as in 'Everything in the world is perishing.' However, it can also function as an adverb of time because the 'ma' in it refers to time, thereby acting as a connective tool (adāt rabṭ) that requires a condition (sharṭ) and a response (jawāb). The example from the Quran (al-Ma'idah 64) illustrates this: 'Whenever they kindled a fire for war, Allah extinguished it.' This conditional use is what logicians intend. Next, the author notes that logicians also use 'mahma' for the conditional. Originally, 'mahma' is an ambiguous noun for non-rational beings that has been imbued with the meaning of condition, as in the Quranic verse 'Whatever you bring us' (al-A'raf 132). In logic, it is used as an adverb of time. The commentary by al-Sa'd clarifies that 'mahma' in language originally denotes generality of individuals, making it suitable as a quantifier for universal categorical propositions; but logicians have transferred it to generality of times and states, making it a quantifier for universal conditionals. Thus, the passage defines the technical redefinition of these two terms by logicians.

Key Takeaway
Logicians transform ordinary Arabic words like 'kullama' and 'mahma' into precise quantifiers for universal conditionals by shifting their scope from individuals to times and states.

Analyzing the Grammatical Dispute over Logicians' Use of 'Mahma'
Critical analysis of logicians' use of 'mahma' as temporal adverb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the linguistic debate over the logicians' use of 'mahma' as a temporal adverb and evaluate the implications for technical terminology in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Logicians' technical needs can conflict with linguistic purity, with real consequences for Quranic interpretation.

This passage presents a critical dispute: some grammarians accuse logicians of solecism (laḥn) for using 'mahma' as a temporal adverb. Others follow Ibn Malik who allowed it, but his own son reinterpreted the evidence. The controversy extends to the usage of 'qad' with negated verbs. By examining this debate, you will see how Arabic grammar and logic intersect, and why precision in technical terms matters for avoiding hermeneutical errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Locate the two opposing opinions about the logicians' use of 'mahma'. One says it is a solecism (laḥn), the other says it is permissible according to Ibn Malik's school.
The phrase 'فقيل: إن ذلك لحن منهم وقيل: على مذهب من أجاز ظرفيتها' sets up the dispute.
2
Examine the evidence
Ibn Malik adduced poetic evidence (istishhād) to support his view. Note how his son reinterpreted (ta’wīl) those lines to show 'mahma' refers to the event, not time.
Example: Hatim's line: father said 'whenever you give', son said 'a giving that you give'.
3
Recognize the wider linguistic issue
The passage also discusses 'qad la yakun' – logicians use it, but 'qad' normally does not precede a negated verb. This is another alleged solecism, even found in Ibn Malik's own poetry.
The author points out that even Ibn Malik used it, showing inconsistency.
4
Assess the implications
Al-Zamakhshari's strong rebuke shows that this is not just a petty grammatical dispute; misusing 'mahma' as a temporal adverb can lead to misinterpretation of Quranic verses (like al-A'raf 132).
The danger: 'يلحد في آيات الله وهو لا يشعر' – committing heresy unknowingly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَقِيلَ : إِنَّ ذَلِكَ لَحْنُ مِنْهُمْ . وَقِيلَ : عَلَى مَذْهَبِ مَنْ أَجَازَ ظَرْفِيَّتَهَا وَهُوَ ابْنُ مَالِكِ، وَاسْتَشْهَدَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ بِشَوَاهِدَ تَأَوَّلَهَا كُلَّهَا وَلَدُهُ بِأَنَّهَا وَاقِعَةٌ فِيهَا عَلَى الحَدَثِ لَا الزَّمَنِ(۱) ؛ مِنْهَا قَوْلُ حَاتِم : وَإِنَّكَ مَهْمَا تُعْطِ بَطْنَكَ سُوْلَهُ وَفَرْجَكَ نَالَا مُنْتَهَى النَّمِّ أَجْمَعَا فَقَالَ الوَالِدُ [ج/ ۲۷] : «المَعْنَى : مَتَى تُعْطِ»، وَقَالَ وَلَدُهُ: «المَعْنَى : إِعْطَاءٌ تُعْطَى». وَاسْتَعْمَلُوا أَيْضاً : «قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ»، وَهُوَ لَحْنٌ ; لِأَنَّ «قَدْ لَا تَدْخُلُ عَلَى المَنْفِيِّ، وَكَثِيراً مَا يَقَعُ لِغَيْرِهِمْ كَقَوْلِ ابْنِ مَالِكِ : وَالمَصْرُوفُ قَدْ لَا يَنْصَرِفُ». وَالصَّمِيرُ فِي لَيْسَ البَتَّةَ : ضَمِيرُ الشَّأنِ، وَالجُمْلَةُ بَعْدَهُ : الخَبَرُ، وَكَذَا تَقُولُ في «قَدْ يَكُونُ»، وَنَحْوِهِ.
واعترضه البوسيُّ : بأنَّها ليست مِنَ الألفاظ الاصطلاحيَّة حتّى يكون لهم فيها نقل ، بل هي لغوي، فذلك لحن ، أو جارٍ على ما ذكر ابن مالك في التّسهيل» أنَّها ترد ظرفاً، وهذا مراد الشارح بقوله : «فقيل لحن ... إلخ»، لكنَّ الظَّاهر منه : أنَّ القائل بأنَّه لحن هو غير القائل بالثاني، وليس الأمر كذلك.
وعبارة الهلالي : والمناطقة يستعملونها ظرفاً ، فإما أن يكون ذلك لحناً منهم، أو ذهاباً على مذهب مَن أجاز ظرفيَّتها، وهو ابن مالك . اهـ .
قوله : (وَهُوَ ابْنُ مَالِكِ) قال في «المغني» : وهذه المقالة سبق إليها ابن مالك غيره، وتأوّل ذلك ابنه بأنَّها واقعةٌ على الحدث، والزمخشري ممَّن شدَّد النَّكير على من يستعملها كذلك، فقال: هذه الكلمة في عدد الكلمات التي يخرجها من لا يد له في علم العربيَّة، ويضعها غير موضعها، ويظنُّها بمعنى: متى، ثمّ يذهب فيفسر : ﴿مَهمَا تَأْتِنَا بِهِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۳۲] الآية؛ بمعنى: الوقت، فيلحد في آيات الله تعالى وهو لا يشعر.
قوله : (كَقَوْلِ ابْنِ مَالِكِ : وَالمَصْرُوفُ قَدْ لَا يَنْصَرِفُ مثله قول خليل: قد لا يحرمن مِنَ الرَّضاع إن جعلت «قد» حرفاً، واستعملها بعضهم اسم فعل، وهو بعيد.
It was said: That is a solecism (laḥn) from them. And it was said: (it is) according to the school of whoever permitted its adverbial use, and he is Ibn Malik. He adduced evidence for that with testimonies, all of which his son interpreted as indicating that they fall on the event, not on time. Among them is the saying of Hatim: 'And indeed, whatever you give your belly its desire and your private part, they attain the utmost of all slander.' The father said: 'The meaning is: whenever you give.' And his son said: 'The meaning is: a giving that you give.' And they also used 'qad la yakun' (it might not be), and it is a solecism, because 'qad' does not enter upon a negated [verb], and it frequently occurs from others like the saying of Ibn Malik: 'And the fully declinable maṣrūf might not be fully declined.' And the pronoun in 'laysa al-battata' is the pronoun of the matter (ḍamīr al-sha'n), and the sentence after it is the predicate; similarly you say in 'qad yakun' and the like. Al-Busi objected to it: that it is not among the technical terms so that they have a transfer in it, rather it is linguistic, so that is a solecism, or it proceeds according to what Ibn Malik mentioned in al-Tashil that it occurs as an adverb, and this is what the commentator intended by his saying 'it was said: solecism... etc.' But the apparent meaning is that the one who said it is a solecism is other than the one who said the second, and it is not so. And the expression of al-Hilali: The logicians use it as an adverb, so either that is a solecism from them, or it is going by the school of whoever permitted its adverbial use, and he is Ibn Malik. He said: (and he is Ibn Malik) He said in al-Mughni: This statement was preceded by Ibn Malik other than him, and his son interpreted it as indicating that it falls on the event, and al-Zamakhshari is among those who strongly rebuked those who use it thus, saying: This word is among the words that those without skill in Arabic science misuse, placing it in other than its proper place, thinking it means 'when', then they go on to interpret the verse 'Whatever you bring us' (al-A'raf 132) as meaning 'time', thus committing heresy against the verses of Allah without realizing. He said: (like the saying of Ibn Malik: 'And the fully declinable maṣrūf might not be fully declined') similarly the saying of Khalil: 'It might not forbid from breastfeeding' if 'qad' is made a particle, and some used it as a verb, which is far-fetched.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the logicians' use of 'mahma' as a temporal adverb. The author presents two opinions: some say it is a solecism (laḥn), while others follow Ibn Malik who allowed it. Ibn Malik supported his view with poetic evidence, but his son reinterpreted those verses, arguing that 'mahma' in them refers to the event, not time. For example, the line from Hatim: the father (Ibn Malik) interpreted 'mahma' as 'whenever' (mata), but the son said the meaning is 'a giving' (i'ṭā') – i.e., a masdar of the verb. The author then notes another usage: logicians also use 'qad la yakun' (it might not be), which is considered a solecism because 'qad' normally does not precede a negated verb; yet even Ibn Malik used it. The author references al-Busi's objection that these are not technical terms but purely linguistic, so the logicians' usage is either a solecism or follows Ibn Malik's school. Al-Hilali's statement summarizes the options. The passage then quotes al-Zamakhshari's strong criticism: those who use 'mahma' as a temporal adverb misinterpret the Quranic verse and are ignorant of Arabic. The discussion highlights the tension between logical terminology and linguistic correctness, showing that logicians sometimes override grammatical norms for technical convenience.

8 min
Are Logicians' Terms Solecisms? The Debate on 'Mahma' and 'Qad la Yakun'
Examination of linguistic validity of 'mahma' and 'qad la yakun'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the linguistic debates surrounding the use of 'mahma' and 'qad la yakun' in logical propositions, identifying the key positions and their implications for the intersection of logic and grammar.

Core Idea
Logicians' use of certain expressions is either a grammatical error or a license from a recognized school.

This lesson examines a dialectical passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on logic, where the validity of two common expressions—'mahma' (whenever) and 'qad la yakun' (may not be)—is debated. The grammarians and logicians clash over whether these are solecisms or permissible usage, with implications for how we read logical texts and even Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions under debate
Locate in the Arabic text where 'mahma' and 'qad la yakun' are discussed. Note that both are used by logicians but questioned by grammarians.
2
Examine the positions on 'mahma'
Find the two opposing views: some say it is a solecism, others claim it follows Ibn Malik's school permitting it as a circumstantial particle (zarf). Note how Ibn Malik's son reinterprets poetic evidence to argue it refers to the event (hadath) not time (zaman).
The father interprets 'mahma' as 'mata' (when), the son as 'i'ta'un tu'ta' (a giving that you give).
3
Understand the reinterpretation and criticism
Read the al-Mughni citation confirming Ibn Malik's precedence and his son's event-based reinterpretation. Then note al-Zamakhshari's severe criticism: misinterpreting 'mahma' as temporal in the Quran leads to deviation (ilhad).
Al-Zamakhshari warns that taking 'mahma' as a time word in Q.7:132 is a serious error.
4
Analyze the critique of 'qad la yakun'
Identify the claim that 'qad la yakun' is a solecism because 'qad' does not precede a negated verb. Then find the counterexamples from Ibn Malik and Khalil, showing its use in actual speech.
Some even take 'qad' as a verb-noun, but that is deemed far-fetched.

فَقِيلَ : إِنَّ ذَلِكَ لَحْنُ مِنْهُمْ . وَقِيلَ : عَلَى مَذْهَبِ مَنْ أَجَازَ ظَرْفِيَّتَهَا وَهُوَ ابْنُ مَالِكِ، وَاسْتَشْهَدَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ بِشَوَاهِدَ تَأَوَّلَهَا كُلَّهَا وَلَدُهُ بِأَنَّهَا وَاقِعَةٌ فِيهَا عَلَى الحَدَثِ لَا الزَّمَنِ؛ مِنْهَا قَوْلُ حَاتِم : وَإِنَّكَ مَهْمَا تُعْطِ بَطْنَكَ سُوْلَهُ وَفَرْجَكَ نَالَا مُنْتَهَى النَّمِّ أَجْمَعَا فَقَالَ الوَالِدُ : «المَعْنَى : مَتَى تُعْطِ»، وَقَالَ وَلَدُهُ: «المَعْنَى : إِعْطَاءٌ تُعْطَى». وَاسْتَعْمَلُوا أَيْضاً : «قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ»، وَهُوَ لَحْنٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ «قَدْ لَا تَدْخُلُ عَلَى المَنْفِيِّ، وَكَثِيراً مَا يَقَعُ لِغَيْرِهِمْ كَقَوْلِ ابْنِ مَالِكِ : وَالمَصْرُوفُ قَدْ لَا يَنْصَرِفُ». وَالصَّمِيرُ فِي لَيْسَ البَتَّةَ : ضَمِيرُ الشَّأنِ، وَالجُمْلَةُ بَعْدَهُ : الخَبَرُ، وَكَذَا تَقُولُ فِي «قَدْ يَكُونُ»، وَنَحْوِهِ؛ قَصَارَةٍ. لَفْظٌ وَاعْتَرَضَهُ البُوسِيُّ : بِأَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ مِنَ الأَلْفَاظِ الاِصْطِلَاحِيَّةِ حَتَّى يَكُونَ لَهُمْ فِيهَا نَقْلٌ، بَلْ هِيَ لُغَوِيَّةٌ، فَذَلِكَ لَحْنٌ، أَوْ جَارٍ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرَ ابْنُ مَالِكٍ فِي التَّسْهِيلِ أَنَّهَا تَرِدُ ظَرْفاً، وَهَذَا مُرَادُ الشَّارِحَ بِقَوْلِهِ : «فَقِيلَ لَحْنٌ ... إِلَخْ»، لَكِنَّ الظَّاهِرَ مِنْهُ : أَنَّ القَائِلَ بِأَنَّهُ لَحْنٌ هُوَ غَيْرُ القَائِلِ بِالثَّانِي، وَلَيْسَ الأَمْرُ كَذَلِكَ. وَعِبَارَةُ الهِلَالِيِّ : وَالمَنْطِقِيُّونَ يَسْتَعْمِلُونَهَا ظَرْفاً، فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ ذَلِكَ لَحْناً مِنْهُمْ، أَوْ ذَهَاباً عَلَى مَذْهَبِ مَنْ أَجَازَ ظَرْفِيَّتَهَا، وَهُوَ ابْنُ مَالِكٍ. اهـ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَهُوَ ابْنُ مَالِكٍ) قَالَ فِي «المُغْنِي» : وَهَذِهِ المَقَالَةُ سَبَقَ إِلَيْهَا ابْنُ مَالِكٍ غَيْرَهُ، وَتَأَوَّلَ ذَلِكَ ابْنُهُ بِأَنَّهَا وَاقِعَةٌ عَلَى الحَدَثِ، وَالزَّمَخْشَرِيُّ مِمَّنْ شَدَّدَ النَّكِيرَ عَلَى مَنْ يَسْتَعْمِلُهَا كَذَلِكَ، فَقَالَ: هَذِهِ الكَلِمَةُ فِي عَدَدِ الكَلِمَاتِ الَّتِي يُخْرِجُهَا مَنْ لَا يَدَ لَهُ فِي عِلْمِ العَرَبِيَّةِ، وَيَضَعُهَا غَيْرَ مَوْضِعِهَا، وَيَظُنُّهَا بِمَعْنَى: مَتَى، ثُمَّ يَذْهَبُ فَيُفَسِّرُ ﴿مَهْمَا تَأْتِنَا بِهِ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٣٢] الآيَةَ؛ بِمَعْنَى: الوَقْتِ، فَيَلْحَدُ فِي آيَاتِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَهُوَ لَا يَشْعُرُ. قَوْلُهُ : (كَقَوْلِ ابْنِ مَالِكٍ : وَالمَصْرُوفُ قَدْ لَا يَنْصَرِفُ مِثْلُهُ قَوْلُ خَلِيلٍ: قَدْ لَا يَحْرُمَنِ مِنَ الرَّضَاعِ إِنْ جُعِلَتْ «قَدْ» حَرْفاً، وَاسْتَعْمَلَهَا بَعْضُهُمْ اسْمَ فِعْلٍ، وَهُوَ بَعِيدٌ.
So it was said: indeed that is a solecism from them. And it was said: (it is) according to the school of one who permitted its being a circumstantial particle, and he is Ibn Malik. He adduced evidence for that with testimonies, all of which his son interpreted as occurring therein on the event, not on the time. Among them is the saying of Hatim: 'And indeed, mahma you give your belly its desire and your private part, they will attain the utmost of blame altogether.' So the father said: 'The meaning: when (mata) you give,' and his son said: 'The meaning: a giving that you give.' And they also used 'qad la yakun', and it is a solecism because 'qad' does not enter upon the negated; and it frequently occurs to others, like the saying of Ibn Malik: 'And the declined noun may not be declinable.' And the pronoun in 'laysa al-battata' is the pronoun of the matter, and the sentence after it is the predicate. And similarly you say in 'qad yakun' and the like. So he objected to him, al-Būsī, saying that they are not from the technical terms such that they would have a convention for them; rather they are linguistic, so that is a solecism, or it follows what Ibn Malik mentioned in al-Tashil, that it occurs as a circumstantial particle. And this is what the commentator meant by his saying: 'so it was said solecism, etc.' But the apparent from it is that the one who said it is solecism is other than the one who said the second, and it is not so. And the expression of al-Hilālī: The logicians use it as a circumstantial particle; so either that is a solecism from them, or a following of the school of one who permitted its being a circumstantial particle, which is Ibn Malik. End. His saying: (and he is Ibn Malik). He said in al-Mughnī: Ibn Malik preceded others to this statement. His son interpreted it as occurring on the event. And al-Zamakhsharī is among those who severely criticized those who use it thus. He said: This word is among the words that one who has no skill in Arabic science misplaces, and he thinks it means 'when', then he proceeds to interpret the verse 'Mahma you bring us' [Quran 7:132] as meaning time, thereby committing deviation (ilhad) in the verses of God Almighty while unaware. His saying: (like the saying of Ibn Malik: 'And the declined noun may not be declinable') similar is the saying of Khalīl: 'Qad la yahramin from breastfeeding' if 'qad' is made a particle; and some used it as a verb-noun, and that is far-fetched.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the linguistic validity of two terms used by logicians: the conditional particle 'mahma' and the expression 'qad la yakun'. The author presents conflicting opinions. Some consider their use a solecism (lahn) among logicians, while others justify it by following Ibn Malik's school, which allows 'mahma' to be a circumstantial particle (zarf) meaning 'when'. To support this, Ibn Malik's son reinterpreted testimonial verses, such as Hatim's poem, as referring to the event (hadath) rather than time (zaman). The father interpreted 'mahma' as 'when' (mata), but the son reinterpreted it as 'a giving that you give' (i'ta'un tu'ta), shifting from temporal to eventive meaning. The author then addresses the expression 'qad la yakun', noting its solecism because 'qad' should not precede a negated verb in standard Arabic, though it occurs in the usage of some like Ibn Malik. The pronoun in 'laysa al-battata' is the subject pronoun (damir al-sha'n), with the following sentence as its predicate. Al-Būsī objects that these are not technical terms but linguistic, so their use is either a solecism or a license following Ibn Malik. The author clarifies that the apparent distinction between two different opinions is not accurate; the same scholar may hold both views. Al-Hilālī's expression summarizes: logicians use 'mahma' as a circumstantial particle, either as a solecism or following Ibn Malik's permission. The discussion then cites al-Mughnī, noting Ibn Malik's precedence and his son's reinterpretation. Al-Zamakhsharī severely criticizes the misinterpretation of 'mahma' as temporal, warning that it could lead to deviation in interpreting Quranic verses by imposing a temporal meaning. The author concludes with an example from Ibn Malik and a similar one from Khalīl regarding 'qad' used before a negated verb, noting that some incorrectly take 'qad' as a verb-noun, which is far-fetched.

Key Terms

ظرف
مَهْمَا
قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the expression 'qad la yakun' considered a solecism (lahn)?
A) Because 'qad' cannot precede a negated verb in standard Arabic.
B) Because it is never used in classical poetry.
C) Because logicians invented the term without linguistic basis.
D) Because it violates the rules of logic.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic validity of logical terms like 'mahma' and 'qad la yakun' is not settled; it reflects the ongoing tension between technical convenience and grammatical purity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Grammatical Analysis of 'al-Battata' in Logic
The author explains the derivation, required case, and hamza type of 'al-battata'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the grammatical features of the logical term 'al-battata' (derivation, case, hamza type) and evaluate the scholarly debate regarding its hamza.

Core Idea
The logical particle 'al-battata' is a verbal noun in the accusative, and its hamza is a point of grammatical controversy.

The term 'al-battata' (meaning 'absolutely' or 'decisively') is frequently used in logical propositions to express certainty. Understanding its correct grammatical form is essential for precise logical formulation, as errors in its usage can obscure meaning. This passage from Bajuri's commentary clarifies its derivation from 'al-batt' (cutting), its required accusative case, and the dispute over whether its hamza is connective or cutting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the derivation
Locate where the passage says 'al-battata' is from 'al-batt' meaning cutting. Note that it is a form (fi'la) from that root.
2
Determine the case
The passage states it necessarily takes the accusative (naṣb) as a verbal noun (māṣdar) with an implied verb, indicating an absolute object (maf'ūl muṭlaq).
This construction is standard for such intensifiers.
3
Examine the hamza
The claim is that the hamza of 'al' in 'al-battata' is a connective hamza (hamzat waṣl). Understand that a connective hamza is dropped when connected, unlike a cutting hamza which is always pronounced.
4
Explore the dispute
The passage acknowledges that some grammarians (e.g., al-Khalīl) hold it is cutting, but the author asserts it is connective, following Sibawayh and Ibn Malik. The disagreement centers on whether 'al' entered upon 'batt' or another word.
The author dismisses the contrary view in al-Taṣrīḥ as not being the core (lubāb).
5
Reflect on implications for logic
Since 'al-battata' is used in logical propositions (e.g., 'laysa al-battata'), its proper case and hamza affect reading and interpretation. Accurate knowledge prevents mispronunciation and preserves intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالبَتَّةَ مِنَ : «البَتْ»؛ بِمَعْنَى : القَطْعِ، يَلْزَمُهَا النَّصْبُ عَلَى المَصْدَرِ بِفِعْلٍ مُقَدَّرٍ، وَهَمْزَتُهَا وَصْلِيَّةٌ كَغَيْرِهَا، وَلَا يَجِبُ قَطْعُهَا، خِلَافاً لِمَا فِي التَّصْرِيحِ». ✿ ✿ قصارة . قوله: (وَالبَتَّةَ مِنَ : «البَتٌ») أي: فعلة مِنَ البت، وهو : «القطع»، يلزمها النَّصب على المفعولية المطلقة بفعل محذوف. قوله : (وَهَمْزَتُهَا وَصْلِيَّةٌ كَغَيْرِهَا) يعني : لأنَّ همزة (أل) وصليَّة على قول سيبويه، وعليه درج ابن مالك في «ألفيَّته» فقال : وَهَـ زَةُ «أَلْ» كَـ ـذا أي وصل قوله : (وَلَا يَجِبُ قَطْعُهَا أي: كما ذهب إليه الخليل، فالخلافُ في همزة «أل» دخلت على «بت» أو غيره. قوله : ( خِلَافاً لِمَا فِي التَّصْرِيحِ ) أي : حيث نقل عن اللباب»: أنَّها همزة قطع، وهو يقتضي : أنَّها ليست كسائر همزات «أل»، فلذلك يقال : ما في «اللباب» ليس بلباب.
And 'al-battata' is from 'al-batt' meaning cutting; it necessarily takes the accusative case as a verbal noun (māṣdar) with an implied verb, and its hamza is a connective (wasliyya) like others, and its cutting is not obligatory, contrary to what is in al-Taṣrīḥ.
✿ ✿ A marginal note.
His saying: (and 'al-battata' is from 'al-batt') i.e., it is a form (fi'la) from al-batt, which is 'cutting'; it necessarily takes the accusative case as an absolute direct object with an omitted verb. His saying: (and its hamza is a connective like others) means: because the hamza of 'al' is connective according to Sibawayh, and upon that Ibn Malik proceeded in his Alfiyya, saying: [quote]. His saying: (and its cutting is not obligatory) i.e., as al-Khalīl held; so the disagreement is about the hamza of 'al' entering upon 'batt' or other. His saying: (contrary to what is in al-Taṣrīḥ) i.e., where it is transmitted from al-Lubāb that it is a cutting hamza, which implies that it is not like the other hamzas of 'al', hence it is said: what is in al-Lubāb is not the core (lubāb).
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'al-battata' (meaning 'absolutely' or 'decisively') used in logical propositions. The author derives it from 'al-batt' (cutting), indicating decisiveness. Grammatically, it must take the accusative case (naṣb) as a verbal noun (māṣdar) with an implied verb, i.e., as an absolute object (maf'ūl muṭlaq). The second point is about its hamza: the hamza of 'al' in 'al-battata' is a connective hamza (hamzat waṣl), like other 'al' hamzas, meaning it is dropped in pronunciation when connected. Therefore, it is not a cutting hamza (hamzat qat') that is always pronounced. The author notes that this is contrary to what is stated in al-Taṣrīḥ (a grammatical work) which claims it is a cutting hamza based on al-Lubāb. The commentary explains the rationale: according to Sibawayh, the hamza of 'al' is always connective, and Ibn Malik followed this in his Alfiyya. The disagreement is only about whether 'al' entered upon 'batt' or other words. The author asserts that the correct view is that it is connective. The passage ends with a critical remark: 'what is in al-Lubāb is not the true core (lubāb)', implying that al-Lubāb contains an error.

Key Terms
البَتَّة
همزة وصل
همزة قطع
مصدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what grammatical case is 'al-battata' required to take, and why?
A) Nominative (raf') because it is a subject.
B) Accusative (naṣb) as a verbal noun (māṣdar) with an implied verb.
C) Genitive (jarr) because it follows a preposition.
D) No specific case; it is indeclinable.

Key Takeaway
The correct grammatical analysis of 'al-battata' – its derivation from 'batt', accusative case as a verbal noun, and its connective hamza – is crucial for precise logical discourse, highlighting the interplay between grammar and logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
