The Logical Definition of Contradiction (Tanāquḍ)
Defines tanāquḍ in logical terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the complete logical definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and identify its three essential components: two propositions, opposition in quality, and necessary truth of one.

Core Idea
Contradiction is the opposition of two propositions in affirmation/negation such that one must be true and the other false.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary on al-Sullam establishes the precise meaning of tanāquḍ in Islamic logic. The definition is not just about any opposition but specifically between propositions differing in quality, with the necessary condition that one is true and the other false. Understanding this definition is crucial for all subsequent logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined (tanāquḍ) in the Arabic text. The author indicates what contradiction (تناقض) means in technical usage.
2
Extract the genus
What broader category does the author place it in? It is 'khulf al-qadiyyatayn' (opposition of two propositions). This is the general class.
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes logical contradiction from other oppositions? Two things: (1) it is in quality (fi kayf), i.e., affirmation vs negation; (2) it necessarily entails that one is true and the other false (ṣidq wāḥid wa kadhib al-ukhrā).
4
Test the definition
Apply the definition to an example: 'The world is originated' and 'The world is not originated'. Both are propositions, they differ in quality, and necessarily one is true. This satisfies the definition.
5
Compare with common misunderstanding
Note that the definition deliberately excludes terms (like 'horse' vs 'not-horse') and performative sentences (like 'stand' vs 'do not stand') because they are not propositions that can be true or false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتناقض في الاصطلاح هو ما أشار إليه المصنف بقوله: (٦٣) «تَنَاقُضُ»: خُلْفُ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي كَيْفٍ، وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ أَمْرٌ كُفِي. خُلْفُ) بِمَعْنَى : اخْتِلَافِ ، وَالكَيْفُ : الإِيْجَابُ وَالسَّلْبُ، وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ) أَيْ: فَقَط ، وَقُفِيَ بِمَعْنَى : تَبِعَ ; أَي لُزُوماً، وَالجُمْلَةُ حَالِيَّةٌ فَهِيَ قَيْدٌ فِيمَا قَبْلِهَا مِنْ تَمَامِ التَّعْرِيفِ، فَكَأَنَّهُ يَقُولُ : التَّنَاقُضُ هُوَ : اخْتِلَافُ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ بِالإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ عَلَى وَجْهِ يَقْتَضِي بِمُجَرَّدِ ذَلِكَ الاِخْتِلَافِ لُزُومَ صِدْقِ إِحْدَاهُمَا وَكَذِبِ الأُخْرَى». فَخَرَجَ : اخْتِلَافُ غَيْرِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ ; كَاخْتِلَافِ مُفْرَدَيْنِ ; نَحْو: «فَرَسٍ، وَلَا فَرَسٍ»، وَاخْتِلَافِ إِنْشَاتَيْنِ ; نَحْوُ : «قُمْ، وَلَا تَقُمْ»، فَذَلِكَ وَمَا أَشْبَهَهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ تَنَاقُضاً فِي المَعْنَى، لَكِنْ لَا يُسَمَّى تَنَاقُضاً فِي الاِصْطِلاحِ . وَخَرَجَ بِـ ذِكْرِ الكَيْفِ : الِاخْتِلَافُ بِغَيْرِهِ كَاخْتِلَافِ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ بِالعُدُولِ وَالتَّحْصِيلِ، وَالكُلّيّةِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ ، وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ وَالحَمْلِيَّةِ ، وَنَحْوِ ذَلِكَ ; عَلَى أَنَّ قَيْدَ الكَيْفِ يُغْنِي عَنْهُ مَا بَعْدَهُ; لأَنَّ مَا خَرَجَ بِهِ يَخْرُجُ بِمَا بَعْدَهُ، كَذَا قِيْلَ، وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ ; إِذْ يَخْرُجُ بِالكَيْفِ نَحْوُ : «العَالَمُ حَادِثٌ، العَالَمُ قَدِيمٌ»، وَهُوَ لَا يَخْرُجُ بِمَا بَعْدَهُ، تَأمَّلْ . قوله : (فَكَأَنَّهُ يَقُولُ) فيه إشعار بأنَّ عبارة النَّاظم غير وافية بهذا المعنى كله، لكن قوَّة كلامه تُعطي ذلك . قوله : (فَخَرَجَ : اخْتِلَافُ غَيْرِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ ) إِنَّما خصُّوا بحثهم بتناقض القضايا دون تناقض المفردات، وإن كان ينبغي أن تكون مباحثهم عامة ; لأنَّ التَّناقض لا يُتصوَّر في غير القضايا، كما قرَّره السَّيِّد في شرح المواقف» في غير موضع وذلك لأنَّ المتناقضين هما المفهومان المتمانعان لذاتهما، ولا تمانع بين التصورات، فإنَّ مفهومي «الإنسان» و : «لا إنسان» لا يتمانعان; إلا إذا اعتبر ثبوتهما لشيء، كما تشهد به البديهة وحينئذ يحصل هناك قضيَّتان متنافيتان صدقاً وكذباً، فمن زعم أنَّ بين مفهومي «فرس» و «لا فرس» تقابل الإيجاب والسَّلب مطلقاً فقد سها; إلَّا إن بني ذلك على النظر إلى الظاهر، فرفع المفرد إنَّما يسمى: «سلباً» لا : «نقضاً». قوله : (وَمَا أَشْبَهَهُ ) كاختلاف مرکبین ناقصين :کـ «ثوب زید» و «لا ثوبه»، واختلاف مفرد وقضيَّة . قوله : (كَذَا قِيْلَ) قائله الهلالي، وبيَّنه بقوله : لأنَّه لا يقتضي شيءٌ مِنَ الاختلافات لزوم صدق أحدهما وكذب الآخر إلَّا الاختلاف في الكيف . اهـ . وعليه فالاختلاف بغير الإيجاب والسَّلب لا يقتضي لذاته صدق أحدهما وكذب الآخر . قوله : (وَفِيهِ نَظَر) في نظره نظر .
And contradiction in terminology is what the author indicated by his saying: (63) 'Contradiction: opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a matter sufficient.' 'Khulf' means disagreement, and 'kayf' is affirmation and negation. His saying 'and the truth of one' means exclusively, and 'qufiya' means 'followed', i.e., necessary. The clause is circumstantial, so it is a restriction on what precedes it from the completion of the definition. It is as if he says: contradiction is the disagreement of two propositions in affirmation and negation in a way that by mere that disagreement necessitates the truth of one and falsehood of the other. So excluded is disagreement of non-propositions, like disagreement of two simple terms: 'horse' and 'not horse', and disagreement of two performative sentences: 'stand' and 'do not stand'. That and its like, even if it is contradiction in meaning, is not called contradiction in terminology. And by mentioning 'kayf' excluded is disagreement other than it, like disagreement of two propositions in modification and actualization, universality and particularity, conditionality and categoricalness, and the like. On the basis that the condition of 'kayf' suffices over what follows it, because what is excluded by it is excluded by what follows it. Thus it was said, and there is a consideration in it: because by 'kayf' is excluded like 'the world is originated, the world is eternal', and it is not excluded by what follows it. Consider. His saying: 'It is as if he says' — there is an indication that the author's expression is not fully sufficient for all this meaning, but the strength of his words gives that. His saying: 'So excluded is disagreement of non-propositions' — they restricted their discussion to contradiction of propositions rather than contradiction of terms, even though their discussions should be general, because contradiction is not conceivable in non-propositions, as al-Sayyid established in his commentary on al-Mawaqif in more than one place. And that is because the two contradictories are two concepts that mutually exclude each other by themselves, but there is no mutual exclusion between conceptions. For the concepts of 'human' and 'not human' do not mutually exclude each other unless their attribution to something is considered, as is witnessed by intuition, and then there arise two propositions opposite in truth and falsehood. So whoever claims that between the concepts of 'horse' and 'not horse' there is the relation of affirmation and negation absolutely has erred, unless it is based on considering the external, for the negation of a term is called 'salb' not 'naqd'. His saying: 'and its like' — like the disagreement of incomplete compounds: 'Zayd's garment' and 'not his garment', and disagreement of a term and a proposition. His saying: 'Thus it was said' — its speaker is al-Hilali, and he explained it by his saying: because none of the disagreements necessitate the truth of one and falsehood of the other except the disagreement in quality. End. According to this, the disagreement other than by affirmation and negation does not by itself necessitate the truth of one and falsehood of the other. His saying: 'and there is a consideration' — in his consideration there is consideration.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of contradiction (tanāquḍ) in logical terminology, as distinguished from its linguistic meaning. The author (the poet of al-Sullam) states: 'Contradiction is the opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is sufficient.' The commentator explains each term: 'khulf' means disagreement, 'kayf' refers to the quality of a proposition, i.e., affirmation (ījāb) or negation (salb). The phrase 'wa ṣidqu wāḥid amrun kufī' means it is sufficient that one of the two is true (and the other false), and this clause is a circumstantial qualifier that completes the definition. Thus the full definition becomes: contradiction is the disagreement of two propositions in affirmation/negation in such a way that merely this disagreement entails the truth of one and the falsehood of the other. Then the commentator excludes cases that are not technically contradiction: disagreement of two simple terms (e.g., 'horse' and 'not horse') and disagreement of two performative sentences (e.g., 'stand' and 'do not stand') — even if they are contradictory in a linguistic sense, they are not called contradiction in the logical technical sense. Next, the passage discusses whether the mention of 'kayf' (quality) alone is sufficient to exclude other differences, such as differences in modification (taḥṣīl/ʿudūl), quantity (kulliyya/juzʾiyya), conditionality vs. categorical, etc. Some have claimed that the 'kayf' condition alone suffices because any difference not in quality will be excluded by the later condition of truth/falsehood entailment. But a critique (nazar) is raised: consider two propositions 'The world is originated' and 'The world is eternal' — they differ in quality? Actually both are affirmative, but they differ in matter (ḥaqīqa vs. qadīm) yet are contradictory? The point is that the definition's conditions need careful application. The commentary then delves into why contradiction is discussed only for propositions, not for terms: because concepts do not truly oppose each other unless they are considered as predicated of something, producing propositions. For example, 'human' and 'not human' as concepts are not contradictory until they are attached to a subject. Hence, the technical definition is precise and guards against common misunderstandings.

Checkpoint
According to the definition in Bajuri Bannani, which of the following is a valid example of logical contradiction (tanāquḍ)?
A) 'Stand' and 'Do not stand'.
B) 'Horse' and 'Not horse'.
C) 'The world is originated (ḥādiṯ)' and 'The world is not originated (laysa bi ḥādiṯ)'.
D) 'The world is eternal (qadīm)' and 'The world is eternal (qadīm)'.

Key Takeaway
Logical contradiction is a precise relation between two propositions differing in quality, with the necessary consequence that one is true and the other false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a pair of contradictory propositions on an Islamic theological topic (e.g., regarding divine omniscience). Check that they satisfy all three conditions: both are propositions, differ in quality, and only one can be true.

Propositions
intermediate
5 min
Understanding Exclusions in Contradiction
Discusses exclusions from the definition and a debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the exclusions from the logical definition of contradiction and evaluate the debate over the sufficiency of the 'kayf' condition.

Core Idea
Not every opposition is logical contradiction; only propositional pairs differing in quality with necessary truth/falsehood qualify.

The definition of tanāquḍ is carefully bounded. This lesson explores what is excluded and why, and the debate among scholars about the role of the quality condition. Understanding these exclusions prevents misapplication of the concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusions mentioned
List what the author excludes: disagreement of simple terms (e.g., 'horse' and 'not horse'), performative sentences (e.g., 'stand' and 'do not stand'), and differences in modification (al-ʿudūl), actualization (al-taḥṣīl), universality/particularity, conditionality/categoricalness, and the like.
2
Understand why each exclusion fails the definition
For terms and performatives, they are not propositions that can be true or false. For differences other than quality (like quantity or type), they do not by themselves ensure that one is true and the other false unconditionally.
3
Examine the debate over the 'kayf' condition
Some scholars (like al-Hilali) claim that the condition of 'kayf' (quality) alone suffices to exclude all other differences, because only disagreement in quality necessitates truth/falsehood. Others (including the author of the commentary) critique this, giving the example of 'The world is originated' vs 'The world is eternal' – both are affirmative but differ in matter, yet they are contradictory? The point is that the definition's conditions must be applied carefully.
4
Form your own assessment
Based on the commentary, consider which view seems more consistent. Does the example of 'originated vs eternal' truly satisfy the definition? What does it tell us about the necessity of the truth/falsehood condition?

And contradiction in terminology is what the author indicated by his saying: (63) 'Contradiction: opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a matter sufficient.' His saying: 'and its like' — like the disagreement of incomplete compounds: 'Zayd's garment' and 'not his garment', and disagreement of a term and a proposition. Commentary
This passage defines the concept of contradiction (tanāquḍ) in logical terminology, as distinguished from its linguistic meaning. The author (the poet of al-Sullam) states: 'Contradiction is the opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is sufficient.' The commentator explains each term: 'khulf' means disagreement, 'kayf' refers to the quality of a proposition, i.e., affirmation (ījāb) or negation (salb). The phrase 'wa ṣidqu wāḥid amrun kufī' means it is sufficient that one of the two is true (and the other false), and this clause is a circumstantial qualifier that completes the definition. Thus the full definition becomes: contradiction is the disagreement of two propositions in affirmation/negation in such a way that merely this disagreement entails the truth of one and the falsehood of the other. Then the commentator excludes cases that are not technically contradiction: disagreement of two simple terms (e.g., 'horse' and 'not horse') and disagreement of two performative sentences (e.g., 'stand' and 'do not stand') — even if they are contradictory in a linguistic sense, they are not called contradiction in the logical technical sense. Next, the passage discusses whether the mention of 'kayf' (quality) alone is sufficient to exclude other differences, such as differences in modification (taḥṣīl/ʿudūl), quantity (kulliyya/juzʾiyya), conditionality vs. categorical, etc. Some have claimed that the 'kayf' condition alone suffices because any difference not in quality will be excluded by the later condition of truth/falsehood entailment. But a critique (nazar) is raised: consider two propositions 'The world is originated' and 'The world is eternal' — they differ in quality? Actually both are affirmative, but they differ in matter (ḥaqīqa vs. qadīm) yet are contradictory? The point is that the definition's conditions need careful application. The commentary then delves into why contradiction is discussed only for propositions, not for terms: because concepts do not truly oppose each other unless they are considered as predicated of something, producing propositions. For example, 'human' and 'not human' as concepts are not contradictory until they are attached to a subject. Hence, the technical definition is precise and guards against common misunderstandings.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT excluded from tanāquḍ according to the passage?
A) Two simple terms like 'horse' and 'not horse'
B) Two performative sentences like 'stand' and 'do not stand'
C) Two propositions differing in universality (kulliyya/juzʾiyya)
D) Two propositions differing in quality (affirmation/negation)

Key Takeaway
Logical contradiction is a narrow technical term; many oppositions in everyday language and other dimensions do not meet its precise conditions.

Consider two propositions: 'Alcohol is impure (najis)' and 'Alcohol is not impure'. Is this a logical contradiction? Compare to: 'Alcohol is impure' and 'Alcohol is intoxicating'. Explain which qualifies as tanāquḍ and why.

Why Contradiction Only Between Propositions
Explains why contradiction is only between propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logical contradiction is defined only between propositions and not between terms or concepts, using the example of 'human' and 'not human'.

Core Idea
Concepts alone do not contradict; contradiction arises only when they are asserted about something in propositional form.

This passage addresses a subtle but important point: why do logicians restrict their discussion of contradiction to propositions? The answer lies in the nature of concepts themselves. As al-Sayyid al-Sharif explains, concepts do not mutually exclude; only when they are attributed to a subject do they form contradictory propositions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of tanāquḍ
It requires two propositions. The commentator emphasizes this by discussing why logicians restrict their discussion to propositions.
2
Understand the argument against term contradiction
The commentator says that the two contradictories are two concepts that mutually exclude each other by themselves. But there is no mutual exclusion between mere concepts. For example, the concepts of 'human' and 'not human' do not contradict each other without a subject.
3
Consider the counterargument
Some might claim that 'horse' and 'not horse' are contradictory in meaning. The author corrects this: the negation of a term is called 'salb' (negation), not 'naqd' (contradiction). True contradiction requires the concepts to be considered as predicated of something.
4
Apply to a new example
Take 'knowledge' and 'not knowledge'. As concepts, are they contradictory? Only when used in a proposition like 'Zayd is knowing' and 'Zayd is not knowing' do they form a genuine contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتناقض في الاصطلاح هو ما أشار إليه المصنف بقوله: (٦٣) «تَنَاقُضُ»: خُلْفُ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ فِي كَيْفٍ، وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ أَمْرٌ كُفِي. خُلْفُ) بِمَعْنَى : اخْتِلَافِ ، وَالكَيْفُ : الإِيْجَابُ وَالسَّلْبُ، وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَصِدْقُ وَاحِدٍ) أَيْ: فَقَط ، وَقُفِيَ بِمَعْنَى : تَبِعَ ; أَي لُزُوماً، وَالجُمْلَةُ حَالِيَّةٌ فَهِيَ قَيْدٌ فِيمَا قَبْلِهَا مِنْ تَمَامِ التَّعْرِيفِ، فَكَأَنَّهُ يَقُولُ : التَّنَاقُضُ هُوَ : اخْتِلَافُ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ بِالإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ عَلَى وَجْهِ يَقْتَضِي بِمُجَرَّدِ ذَلِكَ الاِخْتِلَافِ لُزُومَ صِدْقِ إِحْدَاهُمَا وَكَذِبِ الأُخْرَى». فَخَرَجَ : اخْتِلَافُ غَيْرِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ ; كَاخْتِلَافِ مُفْرَدَيْنِ ; نَحْو: «فَرَسٍ، وَلَا فَرَسٍ»، وَاخْتِلَافِ إِنْشَاتَيْنِ ; نَحْوُ : «قُمْ، وَلَا تَقُمْ»، فَذَلِكَ وَمَا أَشْبَهَهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ تَنَاقُضاً فِي المَعْنَى، لَكِنْ لَا يُسَمَّى تَنَاقُضاً فِي الاِصْطِلاحِ . وَخَرَجَ بِـ ذِكْرِ الكَيْفِ : الِاخْتِلَافُ بِغَيْرِهِ كَاخْتِلَافِ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ بِالعُدُولِ وَالتَّحْصِيلِ، وَالكُلّيّةِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ ، وَالشَّرْطِيَّةِ وَالحَمْلِيَّةِ ، وَنَحْوِ ذَلِكَ ; عَلَى أَنَّ قَيْدَ الكَيْفِ يُغْنِي عَنْهُ مَا بَعْدَهُ; لأَنَّ مَا خَرَجَ بِهِ يَخْرُجُ بِمَا بَعْدَهُ، كَذَا قِيْلَ، وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ ; إِذْ يَخْرُجُ بِالكَيْفِ نَحْوُ : «العَالَمُ حَادِثٌ، العَالَمُ قَدِيمٌ»، وَهُوَ لَا يَخْرُجُ بِمَا بَعْدَهُ، تَأمَّلْ . قوله : (فَكَأَنَّهُ يَقُولُ) فيه إشعار بأنَّ عبارة النَّاظم غير وافية بهذا المعنى كله، لكن قوَّة كلامه تُعطي ذلك . قوله : (فَخَرَجَ : اخْتِلَافُ غَيْرِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ ) إِنَّما خصُّوا بحثهم بتناقض القضايا دون تناقض المفردات، وإن كان ينبغي أن تكون مباحثهم عامة ; لأنَّ التَّناقض لا يُتصوَّر في غير القضايا، كما قرَّره السَّيِّد في شرح المواقف» في غير موضع وذلك لأنَّ المتناقضين هما المفهومان المتمانعان لذاتهما، ولا تمانع بين التصورات، فإنَّ مفهومي «الإنسان» و : «لا إنسان» لا يتمانعان; إلا إذا اعتبر ثبوتهما لشيء، كما تشهد به البديهة وحينئذ يحصل هناك قضيَّتان متنافيتان صدقاً وكذباً، فمن زعم أنَّ بين مفهومي «فرس» و «لا فرس» تقابل الإيجاب والسَّلب مطلقاً فقد سها; إلَّا إن بني ذلك على النظر إلى الظاهر، فرفع المفرد إنَّما يسمى: «سلباً» لا : «نقضاً». قوله : (وَمَا أَشْبَهَهُ ) كاختلاف مرکبین ناقصين :کـ «ثوب زید» و «لا ثوبه»، واختلاف مفرد وقضيَّة . قوله : (كَذَا قِيْلَ) قائله الهلالي، وبيَّنه بقوله : لأنَّه لا يقتضي شيءٌ مِنَ الاختلافات لزوم صدق أحدهما وكذب الآخر إلَّا الاختلاف في الكيف . اهـ . وعليه فالاختلاف بغير الإيجاب والسَّلب لا يقتضي لذاته صدق أحدهما وكذب الآخر . قوله : (وَفِيهِ نَظَر) في نظره نظر .
And contradiction in terminology is what the author indicated by his saying: (63) 'Contradiction: opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is a matter sufficient.' 'Khulf' means disagreement, and 'kayf' is affirmation and negation. His saying 'and the truth of one' means exclusively, and 'qufiya' means 'followed', i.e., necessary. The clause is circumstantial, so it is a restriction on what precedes it from the completion of the definition. It is as if he says: contradiction is the disagreement of two propositions in affirmation and negation in a way that by mere that disagreement necessitates the truth of one and falsehood of the other. So excluded is disagreement of non-propositions, like disagreement of two simple terms: 'horse' and 'not horse', and disagreement of two performative sentences: 'stand' and 'do not stand'. That and its like, even if it is contradiction in meaning, is not called contradiction in terminology. And by mentioning 'kayf' excluded is disagreement other than it, like disagreement of two propositions in modification and actualization, universality and particularity, conditionality and categoricalness, and the like. On the basis that the condition of 'kayf' suffices over what follows it, because what is excluded by it is excluded by what follows it. Thus it was said, and there is a consideration in it: because by 'kayf' is excluded like 'the world is originated, the world is eternal', and it is not excluded by what follows it. Consider. His saying: 'It is as if he says' — there is an indication that the author's expression is not fully sufficient for all this meaning, but the strength of his words gives that. His saying: 'So excluded is disagreement of non-propositions' — they restricted their discussion to contradiction of propositions rather than contradiction of terms, even though their discussions should be general, because contradiction is not conceivable in non-propositions, as al-Sayyid established in his commentary on al-Mawaqif in more than one place. And that is because the two contradictories are two concepts that mutually exclude each other by themselves, but there is no mutual exclusion between conceptions. For the concepts of 'human' and 'not human' do not mutually exclude each other unless their attribution to something is considered, as is witnessed by intuition, and then there arise two propositions opposite in truth and falsehood. So whoever claims that between the concepts of 'horse' and 'not horse' there is the relation of affirmation and negation absolutely has erred, unless it is based on considering the external, for the negation of a term is called 'salb' not 'naqd'. His saying: 'and its like' — like the disagreement of incomplete compounds: 'Zayd's garment' and 'not his garment', and disagreement of a term and a proposition. His saying: 'Thus it was said' — its speaker is al-Hilali, and he explained it by his saying: because none of the disagreements necessitate the truth of one and falsehood of the other except the disagreement in quality. End. According to this, the disagreement other than by affirmation and negation does not by itself necessitate the truth of one and falsehood of the other. His saying: 'and there is a consideration' — in his consideration there is consideration.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of contradiction (tanāquḍ) in logical terminology, as distinguished from its linguistic meaning. The author (the poet of al-Sullam) states: 'Contradiction is the opposition of two propositions in quality, and the truth of one is sufficient.' The commentator explains each term: 'khulf' means disagreement, 'kayf' refers to the quality of a proposition, i.e., affirmation (ījāb) or negation (salb). The phrase 'wa ṣidqu wāḥid amrun kufī' means it is sufficient that one of the two is true (and the other false), and this clause is a circumstantial qualifier that completes the definition. Thus the full definition becomes: contradiction is the disagreement of two propositions in affirmation/negation in such a way that merely this disagreement entails the truth of one and the falsehood of the other. Then the commentator excludes cases that are not technically contradiction: disagreement of two simple terms (e.g., 'horse' and 'not horse') and disagreement of two performative sentences (e.g., 'stand' and 'do not stand') — even if they are contradictory in a linguistic sense, they are not called contradiction in the logical technical sense. Next, the passage discusses whether the mention of 'kayf' (quality) alone is sufficient to exclude other differences, such as differences in modification (taḥṣīl/ʿudūl), quantity (kulliyya/juzʾiyya), conditionality vs. categorical, etc. Some have claimed that the 'kayf' condition alone suffices because any difference not in quality will be excluded by the later condition of truth/falsehood entailment. But a critique (nazar) is raised: consider two propositions 'The world is originated' and 'The world is eternal' — they differ in quality? Actually both are affirmative, but they differ in matter (ḥaqīqa vs. qadīm) yet are contradictory? The point is that the definition's conditions need careful application. The commentary then delves into why contradiction is discussed only for propositions, not for terms: because concepts do not truly oppose each other unless they are considered as predicated of something, producing propositions. For example, 'human' and 'not human' as concepts are not contradictory until they are attached to a subject. Hence, the technical definition is precise and guards against common misunderstandings.

Logical contradiction requires propositions because contradiction is a relation between assertions, not between concepts.

Definition of Contradiction and Its Conditions
Author defines contradiction and excludes differences that allow joint truth or falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify genuine contradictions by applying the conditions of tanāquḍ and distinguishing cases where joint truth or falsehood occur due to predicate scope or external knowledge.

Core Idea
True contradiction requires the mere difference in quality to force opposite truth values.

In this passage, the author defines the logical relation of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and clarifies what is not contradiction. The key condition is that the difference in quality (affirmation vs. negation) must be the sole cause of the incompatibility. The author then excludes cases where both propositions can be true or both false due to the relationship between subject and predicate, or where an external premise mediates the conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central condition of tanāquḍ
The author states that contradiction must arise 'by mere that difference' (bi-mujarrad dhālika al-ikhtilāf), meaning the difference in quality alone forces opposite truth values.
This condition excludes any mediation or external factor.
2
Recognize cases where both are false
When two universal propositions differ in quality and the predicate is more specific than the subject, both are false (e.g., 'Every animal is human' and 'No animal is human').
The falsehood arises from the matter (mādda), not from the logical form alone.
3
Recognize cases where both are true
When two particular propositions differ in quality and the predicate is more specific, both are true (e.g., 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human').
Again, joint truth depends on the specific matter.
4
Recognize mediated contradiction
The pair 'Zayd is human' and 'Zayd is not rational' is not a contradiction because the incompatibility only appears if we know that every human is rational.
The contradiction is mediated by an external premise, violating the 'mere difference' condition.
5
Apply the condition to test given pairs
For any pair of propositions differing in quality, check if they must have opposite truth values without any extra assumptions. If joint truth or falsehood is possible, it is not a genuine contradiction.
This isolates the logical core of tanāquḍ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

نَحْوُ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ ، وَلَا يُعَدُّ هَذَا الاخْتِلَافُ تَنَاقُضاً ; لِأَنَّ افْتِسَامَهُمَا الصِّدْقَ وَالكَذِبَ إِنَّمَا هُوَ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ، وَلِذَا صَحْ صِدْقُهُمَا مَعاً أَوْ كَذِبُهُمَا فِي بَعْضِ المَوَادِّ . وَقَوْلُنَا : بِمُجَرَّدِ ذَلِكَ الِاخْتِلَافِ قَيْدٌ لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ، وَقَدْ بَقِيَ عَلَى النَّاظِمِ، خَرَجَ بِهِ: الاخْتِلَافُ الَّذِي يَقْتَضِي صِدْقَ إِحْدَاهُمَا فَقَط لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ بِوَاسِطَةٍ; نَحْوُ : «زَيْدُ إِنْسَانُ، زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِنَاطِقٍ، فَإِنَّهُ يَقْتَضِي صِدْقَ إِحْدَاهُمَا وَكَذِبَ الأُخْرَى بِوَاسِطَةِ مَعْرِفَةِ أَنَّ كُلَّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، فَلَيْسَ قَوْلُنَا : زَيْدٌ إِنْسَانٌ نَقِيضاً في الاِصْطِلَاحِ لِقَوْلِنَا : زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِنَاطِقٍ ». قصارة. ووجهه : أنَّ التَّركيب الأوَّل في مثاله غير الثَّاني ; لأنَّ المحمول قد اختلف لفظاً، والاختلاف إنَّما يعتبر في مسمَّى التَّناقض بعد اتحاد القضيَّتين في كلِّ وجه، وسيأتي في الوحدات اشتراط اتحاد المحمول، وقد سلم بناني كلام الهلالي هذا في حاشية المختصر . نعم; يُجاب عن اعتراض الهلالي بأن إغناء المتأخّر عن المتقدّم لا يضرُّ ، وإِنَّما المضرُّ العكس; لأنَّ الثَّاني حينئذ يكون زائداً لغير غرض. قوله : (فَلَيْسَ قَوْلُنَا : زَيْدٌ إِنْسَانُ نَقِيضاً فِي الاصْطِلاح) اعترض اليوسي خروج نحو هذا الحد بأنَّ هذا إن كان اصطلاحاً لأهل الفن فنعم، وإلا فلا خفاء في أن يقال لمن قال : هذا إنسان هذا ليس بناطق إنَّه تناقض كلامه في المعنى. اهـ.
Section on contradiction. And by the last condition is excluded: difference in quality in a way that both can be true and both false, such as 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is not laughing', or only both false, like two universal propositions in affirmation and negation that are both false when the predicate is more specific than the subject, e.g., 'Every animal is human, No animal is human'. If the predicate is more general or equal, only the negative is false, e.g., 'No human is animal or rational'. If it is completely different, only the affirmative is false, e.g., 'Every human is a horse'. Or in a way only both can be true, like two particulars that are both true when the predicate is more specific, e.g., 'Some animal is human, Some animal is not human'. If more general or equal, only the affirmative is true, e.g., 'Some human is animal or rational'. If different, only the negative is true, e.g., 'Some human is not a horse'. This difference is not considered contradiction because the division of truth and falsehood is due to the specific matter; thus both can be true or both false in some matters. Our statement 'by mere that difference' is a necessary condition, and it was omitted by the versifier. By it is excluded: the difference that entails the truth of only one, but not by itself, rather by mediation, e.g., 'Zayd is human, Zayd is not rational', for it entails the truth of one and falsehood of the other by mediation of knowing that every human is rational. So our statement 'Zayd is human' is not a contradictory in technical terminology to our statement 'Zayd is not rational'. Qasara. And its explanation: the first composition in his example is not the second because the predicate has changed in wording, and the difference is only considered in the meaning of contradiction after the two propositions are unified in every respect, and the condition of unity of predicate will come in the unities. And al-Bannani accepted al-Hilali's statement in his gloss on al-Mukhtasar. Yes, the objection of al-Hilali is answered that the posterior enriching the anterior is not harmful; the harmful is the opposite because the second would be redundant for no purpose. His statement: 'So our statement Zayd is human is not contradictory in technical terminology' al-Yusi objected to the exclusion of such a definition, saying: If this is technical terminology of the experts, then yes; otherwise, there is no doubt that one would say to someone who said 'This is human, this is not rational' that it is a contradiction in meaning. End.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage defines the logical term 'contradiction' (tanāquḍ) and clarifies what is excluded from it. The author begins by stating that the last condition excludes cases where two propositions differ in quality (affirmation vs. negation) but can both be true or both false. Examples are given: 'Zayd is standing, Zayd is not laughing' can both be true if Zayd is standing but not laughing. Similarly, universal affirmative and negative pairs like 'Every animal is human' and 'No animal is human' are both false because the predicate 'human' is more specific than 'animal' – not all animals are human, and some are human, so both are false. The author then examines different relations between subject and predicate: if the predicate is more general or equal (e.g., 'no human is animal'), only the negative is false; if the predicate is completely different (e.g., 'every human is a horse'), only the affirmative is false. For particular propositions, when the predicate is more specific, both can be true (e.g., 'some animal is human, some animal is not human'). Such cases are not considered contradictions because the truth values depend on the specific matter (mādda), not on the logical form alone. The next condition is that the difference in quality must be the sole cause of the truth-value contrast; if the truth of one proposition depends on an external premise, it is not a true contradiction. For example, 'Zayd is human' and 'Zayd is not rational' – these are not technically contradictory because they would only be incompatible if we know that all humans are rational; the contradiction is mediated by that knowledge. The commentary then addresses an objection by al-Hilālī and a response, and finally notes an objection by al-Yūsī about the technical definition.

Key Terms
تناقض
اختلاف الكيف
قيد
مادة
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is 'Zayd is human, Zayd is not rational' not considered a technical contradiction (tanāquḍ) in this passage?
A) Because the two subjects are different.
B) Because the predicate is not identical (human vs. rational) even if related.
C) Because the truth of one depends on an external premise (every human is rational).
D) Because both can be true if Zayd is human but not rational.

Key Takeaway
A genuine contradiction requires that the difference in quality alone forces opposite truth values, excluding cases where matter or mediation causes joint truth or falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate whether the pair 'The world is eternal' and 'The world is originated' is a genuine contradiction according to the conditions of tanāquḍ. Explain your reasoning using the passage's criteria.
?
Consider the pair 'Zayd is a scholar' and 'Zayd is not writing'. According to the passage, is this a technical contradiction? Why or why not? If not, what condition does it violate?

The Eight Unities Required for Contradiction
Author enumerates the eight conditions of unity necessary for a true contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight conditions of unity necessary for a true contradiction and diagnose which condition is violated in given proposition pairs.

Core Idea
A true contradiction requires unity in subject, predicate, modality, time, place, relation, condition, and whole/part.

The condition that only one of two contradictories can be true implies that the two propositions must be identical in every relevant aspect. This passage from al-Bajuri enumerates eight such aspects, with clear examples from everyday language and Islamic history.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overarching condition
The phrase 'unity of the relation' (ittiḥād al-nisba) means the two propositions must be identical in all aspects that affect truth value. The author lists eight specific unities.
2
Memorize the eight unities
The eight are: subject, predicate, potentiality/actuality, time, place, relation, condition, whole/part. Use the Arabic terms.
Practice with the Arabic terms: waḥdat al-mawḍū‘, waḥdat al-maḥmūl, waḥdat al-quwwa wa al-fi‘l, waḥdat al-zamān, waḥdat al-makān, waḥdat al-iḍāfa, waḥdat al-sharṭ, waḥdat al-kull wa al-juz’.
3
Analyze each example
For each unity, a counterexample is given showing how varying that aspect allows both propositions to be true or both false. For instance, differing subjects (Zayd vs Amr) breaks contradiction. Work through all eight examples.
4
Apply to new pairs
Given a pair of propositions about an Islamic topic, determine if they are genuine contradictories by checking each unity.

And know that from the restriction of necessary truth of only one of them, it is understood that the unity of the relation is necessary in eight matters, for contradiction is only realized through them, and they are: unity of subject, unity of predicate, unity of potentiality and actuality, unity of time, unity of place, unity of relation, unity of condition, unity of whole and part. For if any of these unities is absent, contradiction is not realized. Because: 1. When there is difference in subject, e.g., 'Zayd is a writer, Amr is not a writer', both can be true together or both false together, and you know that there is no contradiction between two true propositions nor between two false ones. 2. Similarly in predicate, e.g., 'Zayd is a writer, Zayd is not a poet'. 3. In potentiality and actuality, e.g., 'Zayd is a writer (potentially), Zayd is not a writer (actually)'. 4. In time, e.g., 'Our Prophet prayed towards Jerusalem before the command to face the Kaaba, our Prophet did not pray towards Jerusalem after that'. 5. In place, e.g., 'Zayd is praying in the mosque, Zayd is not praying in the house'. 6. In relation, e.g., 'Zayd is a father of Amr, Zayd is not a father of Khalid'. 7. In condition, e.g., 'Zayd will enter Paradise on condition of dying with faith, Zayd will not enter it on condition of dying with disbelief'. 8. In whole and part, e.g., 'Zayd is black (part of him), Zayd is not black (all of him)'. Qasara. And perhaps he did not come across al-Sa'd's statement, the text of which: They only considered the unity of predicate and subject so that our statement 'Zayd is rational' is not a contradictory to our statement 'Zayd is not human', even if it is equal to its contradictory, because equality is frequent; if they did not consider unity of the two sides, it would be difficult to control contradictories. End.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage lists the eight conditions of unity (waḥdāt) that must be satisfied for two propositions to be genuine contradictories. The author explains that the condition of necessary truth of only one implies that the relation (nisba) must be identical in eight aspects. First, unity of subject: if the subject differs, e.g., 'Zayd is a writer' vs. 'Amr is not a writer', both can be true or false, so no contradiction. Second, unity of predicate: e.g., 'Zayd is a writer' vs. 'Zayd is not a poet' – here the predicates differ, so both can be true. Third, unity of potentiality (quwwa) vs. actuality (fi‘l): e.g., 'Zayd can write' vs. 'Zayd is not writing now' are compatible. Fourth, unity of time: e.g., the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) prayed towards Jerusalem before the command to face the Kaaba, but not after; so the times differ. Fifth, unity of place: e.g., praying in the mosque vs. not praying in the house. Sixth, unity of relation (iḍāfa): e.g., being father of Amr vs. not being father of Khalid. Seventh, unity of condition: e.g., entering Paradise if die upon faith vs. not entering if die upon disbelief. Eighth, unity of whole and part: e.g., if part of Zayd is black vs. the whole is not black. The passage ends with a citation from al-Sa‘d explaining why unity of subject and predicate is crucial: to avoid confusing a proposition with its logical equivalent, e.g., 'Zayd is rational' is not the contradictory of 'Zayd is not human' because they are not identical in predicate (and 'rational' is coextensive with 'human').

Key Terms
وحدات
اتحاد النسبة
قوة وفعل
كل وجزء

Key Takeaway
For genuine contradiction, two propositions must be identical in subject, predicate, modality, time, place, relation, condition, and whole/part — any difference breaks the contradiction.

Why Unity of Subject and Predicate is Crucial
Al-Sa'd's justification for why subject and predicate must be identical in contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why unity of subject and predicate is essential to avoid confusing logical equivalents with contradictories.

Core Idea
Without identical terms, even equivalent propositions can masquerade as contradictories.

While all eight unities are necessary, the first two—subject and predicate—are especially important. Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) points out that without them, we might mistake 'Zayd is rational' and 'Zayd is not human' for contradictories, even though they are logically equivalent to a genuine contradictory pair. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the unity of subject and predicate
The two propositions must have exactly the same subject and exactly the same predicate term. Not merely a synonym or coextensive term.
2
Understand the problem of equivalence
The proposition 'Zayd is rational' is equivalent to 'Zayd is human' (since rational animal defines human). But 'rational' and 'human' are different predicates.
3
Analyze al-Sa'd's example
If we said 'Zayd is rational' and 'Zayd is not human', these are not contradictories because predicates differ. The genuine contradictory of 'Zayd is rational' is 'Zayd is not rational'. Al-Sa'd warns against laxity.
4
See the practical benefit
By requiring identical terms, we avoid slippery debates where opponents use synonyms to evade direct contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Understanding Mashhurat (Widely-Accepted Premises)
Author defines widely-accepted premises (mashhurat) with causes and contrast to primary axioms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes of mashhurat and distinguish them from primary axioms (awwaliyat).

Core Idea
Mashhurat are widely accepted but can be false; they rest on culture, not pure reason.

In dialectic (jadal), not all premises are self-evident. Many arguments rely on widely accepted beliefs (mashhurat). The author distinguishes their sources: general benefit, natural tenderness, protective zeal, and religious emotion. Understanding this helps you recognize premises that are persuasive but not certain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four causes of mashhurat
From the Arabic text, list the four causes: general benefit (مصلحة عامة), natural tenderness (ما في الطبع من الرقة), protective zeal (الحمية), and religious emotion (انفعالات من الشرع).
Note that these are external factors, not logical necessity.
2
Match examples to causes
Each cause has a clear example in the text: e.g., 'justice is good' for general benefit, 'supporting the weak' for tenderness, 'exposing the private parts' for protective zeal, and 'saying Bismillah' for religious emotion.
3
Distinguish mashhurat from awwaliyat
The test: if you imagine yourself stripped of all external influences (culture, emotion, religion), you would still accept awwaliyat (like 'the whole is greater than the part') but not necessarily mashhurat.
This shows mashhurat are contingent, not self-evident.
4
Recognize that mashhurat can be false
The author explicitly states mashhurat may be true or false, unlike awwaliyat which are always true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالمَشْهُورَاتُ هِيَ : قَضَايَا تَتَطَابَقُ الآرَاءُ عَلَى الحُكْمِ بِهَا، وَيَعْتَرِفُ بِهَا النَّاسُ بِسَبَبٍ شُهْرَتِهَا فِيمَا بَيْنهم : إِمَّا لِمَصْلَحَةٍ عَامَّةٍ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : «العَدْلُ حَسَنٌ، وَالظُّلْمُ قَبِيحٌ» أَوْ لِمَا فِي الطَّبْعِ مِنَ الرِّقَةِ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : «مُوَاسَاةُ الضُّعَفَاءِ مَحْمُودَةٌ». - أَوْ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنَ الحَمِيَّةِ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : كَشْفُ العَوْرَةِ مَذْمُومٌ». أَوْ لِانْفِعَالَاتٍ تَنْشَأَ مِنَ الشَّرْع؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : «التَّسْمِيَةُ فِي أَوَّلِ كُلِّ ذِي بَالٍ مَحْمُودَةٌ». وَرُبَّمَا تَبْلُغُ الشُّهْرَةُ إِلَى حَيْثُ تَلْتَبِسُ بِالأَوَّلِيَّاتِ، وَيُفَرَّقُ بَيْنَهُمَا : بِأَنَّ الإِنْسَانَ لَوْ فَرَضَ نَفْسَهُ خَالِياً عَنْ جَمِيعِ مَا سِوَى عَقْلِهِ لَحَكَمَ بِالأَوَّلِيَّاتِ دُونَ المَشْهُورَاتِ؛ ثُمَّ هِيَ قَدْ تَكُونُ صَادِقَةٌ وَقَدْ تَكُونُ كَاذِبَةٌ، بِخِلَافِ الأَوَّلِيَّاتِ. وَأَمَّا المُسَلَّمَاتُ فَهِيَ : قَضَايَا يُسَلِّمُهَا أَحَدُ الخَصْمَيْنِ لِلآخَرِ؛ لِيَبْنِي عَلَيْهَا حُكْماً؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَتْ فِي نَفْسِهَا صَادِقَةٌ أَوْ كَاذِبَةٌ، وَقَدْ يُسَلِّمُونَهَا لِكَوْنِهَا مُبَرْهَناً عَلَيْهَا فِي عِلْمٍ آخَرَ؛ كَتَسْلِيمِ الفَقِيهِ : أَنَّ خَبَرَ الوَاحِدِ حُجَّةٌ»؛ لِكَوْنِهِ مُبَيَّناً فِي عِلْمِ الأُصُولِ.
The widely-accepted (mashhurat) are propositions on which opinions agree in judgment, and people acknowledge them due to their fame among them: either for a general benefit, like our saying 'Justice is good and oppression is evil'; or due to natural tenderness, like our saying 'Supporting the weak is praiseworthy'; or due to protective zeal in it, like our saying 'Exposing the private parts is blameworthy'; or due to emotions arising from religion, like our saying 'Saying Bismillah at the start of every important matter is praiseworthy.' And sometimes fame reaches a point that it becomes confused with primary axioms (awwaliyat), and the distinction between them is that if a person imagines himself stripped of everything other than his intellect, he would judge primary axioms but not the widely-accepted. Moreover, they may be true or false, unlike primary axioms. As for the conceded (musallamat), they are propositions that one opponent concedes to the other in order to build a judgment upon them, whether they are true in themselves or false. And they may concede them because they have been demonstrated in another science, like the jurist's concession that 'the report of a single narrator is a proof' because it has been proven in the science of principles (usul).
Source pages p. 716

Commentary
This passage defines two types of premises used in dialectic (jadal): mashhurat (widely-accepted) and musallamat (conceded). The author first defines mashhurat as propositions that all or most people agree upon due to their widespread fame, stemming from four causes: (1) general benefit, e.g., 'Justice is good'; (2) natural compassion, e.g., 'Supporting the weak is praiseworthy'; (3) protective honor, e.g., 'Exposing the private parts is blameworthy'; (4) religious emotion, e.g., 'Saying Bismillah is praiseworthy.' These can be so widely accepted that they resemble primary axioms (awwaliyat) such as 'the whole is greater than the part'. The distinction is that primary axioms are known by pure intellect, independent of culture or custom; if one imagines being stripped of all external influences, one would still judge primary axioms but not necessarily the mashhurat. Moreover, mashhurat can be false, while primary axioms are always true. Then the author defines musallamat as premises that one debater concedes to another for the sake of argument, regardless of their truth value. These may come from another science where they have been established, e.g., a jurist conceding that a single-narrator hadith is a proof because it is proven in usul al-fiqh. This clarifies how debate can proceed by accepting premises from the opponent's own field.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a cause for a proposition being widely accepted (mashhura)?
A) General benefit
B) Logical necessity
C) Natural tenderness
D) Religious emotion

Key Takeaway
Mashhurat are premises accepted due to social convention, natural disposition, or religious emotion, not pure reason, and they may be false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a common mashhura premise in contemporary Islamic ethical debates (e.g., 'Intention is important for the validity of acts of worship'). Determine which of the four causes makes it widely accepted and explain why it is not an awwaliya.
?
Take the awwaliya 'The whole is greater than the part' and explain why it would not be considered a mashhura.
